THE RED PAGE.
IN THE ROARING FIFTIES
EDWARD DYSON’S romance of Aus-
tralian life In the Boaring Fifties
gives new occasion to praise his talent
and craftsmanship. The story is
pitched in the golden heyday of Victoria ;
when came ‘ ‘ the four corners of the world in
arms ” to Port Phillip, and life’s tide rose in
a flood of splendid virility.
To lead his story Dyson has had a fine idea.
He has taken a sullen English boor—the butt
of his village, yet with the radical virtue of
his English stock—and thrown him to the
whirlpool of men fighting in the freedom of
the Bush. Friendship cheers him, strife
mellows him, the Bush inspires him; shackles
of birth and breeding loosen from his limbs ;
and in the arms of a lusty daughter of Aus-
tralia he is disenthralled.
Scene and time give amole material to
picture the moulding of a man. Dyson
sketches the voyage, the landing, and the
journey to the goldfields : then he has all
the rich character of the epoch at command,
and he displays it well. Turbulent life on
the fields, with its incidents of struggle and
success ; the denizens of the fields—-diggers,
troopers, Chinese, and a racy embodiment
of Mother Shanty ; —here Dyson stands on
intimate ground. The hero, self-educated in
books and fists, plods and fights till enlisted
under Lalor at Eureka. Dyson has not given
a final account of the scramble at the stockade
—so insignificant, so momentous ; for he
realises people better than scenes, and wants
a lift of emotion in the clean cleverness of
his style—too formal for the highest flights.
Yet he is graphic as far as he goes.
Then the nominal hero’s convict brother
fills the space of mending a wound, and we
acknowledge a hero indeed. The chapters
of the convict’s adventures at Boobyalla
station, his arrest and escape, are chapters
that breathe enchantment: the rest of the
book is stiff' by comparison.
It is on account of this doubling of heroes
and heroines that Dyson comes short of his
proper success. A pair goes well ; a unicorn
team may go better ; but four-in-hand are
hard to manage in fiction. The convict
needs a book to himself : Dyson is em-
barrassed with his material. Theory and
practice fight, and the close is a novelist’s
disaster. Yet the book is well worth read-
ing for the pictures and character and those
dramatic chapters : Macdougal and his
revenge are not to be forgotten.
J*
For Australians and nature-lovers there
are charming glimpses :
He began to feel a friendship for the towering
gums in their flaunting independence ; their proud
individuality pleased him. To his mind they
reflected the spirit of the people—it must be the
spirit of the land. Nowhere in their feathery
elegance did he find a law of conformity ; each
tree was a law to itself, tall and strong and
slender, youthful and buoyant, opening fond arms
to the blue sky.
A wattle gully in full blaze, a stream of golden
bloom sweeping along the course of a little moun-
tain creek as far as the eye could see, each tree a
huge bouquet, the whole mass foaming in the
gentle breeze, a rich feast of colour, lit up by a
glowing noonday sun, and bordered by the sub-
dued green of the mountain gums.
Elsewhere Dyson’s style lacks speed where
it is needed : he frames his sentences with a
caudal clause that tucks in awkwardly.
There is stilted writing that delays the
action and injures one’s impression of
reality :
Suffused with a melancholy tenderness, he told
himself he had never forgotten her; her image
had lived in his heart as in a shrine, screened,
perhaps, but only for sanctity’s sake...
Men upon whose every feature and limb were
stamped the repulsive brands of the lag...
These are faults easily mended, and they are
far outweighed by merits.
4
The useful Magazine of Commerce (April) prints
a lucid article on processes for reproducing illus-
trations.
Home and Camp, by William Dean, is a little
book of verses published by Louisa Lawson,
Sydney. The subjects are a bush township's
topics, discussed in honest prose neatly rhymed
and measured.
The Threshing Floor, by T. 9. Fletcher (Lond.,
Unwin ; 2s. 6 d.), is set apart from the mass of
novels rather by subject than by Style, though the
style is good. The scene is in North England ;
the characters belong to a remarkable family of
“ hard drinkers, hard livers, hard fighters,” im-
pressively portrayed ; and from the incidents of
the ruin of this family springs the story. In
several points the book might be compared with
Hardy’s I'ess. The first and realistic half is espe-
cially interesting : in'the second half the writer’s
conscifentious theory is less effective.
Steele Rudd's Magazine for May has an article
upon Popular Phraseology—signed “ As Now”—
thoughtful, quaint, and suggestive. The writer
compares English with Gaelic, lauding the latter’s
idiom. And he says : " The habit of calling out
to passers-by the single word ‘Hullo*’ in a kindly, ■
friendly spirit of comradeship, and expecting
nothing back but a friendly word of acknowledg-
ment. is a most pleasing and highly characteristic
trait in Australian children.”
VERSIONS OF HEINE
AN enlarged edition of the verses and
translations of Monica Turnbull is
published by John Murray (Lond. ;
t s s -)■ The book’s title is A Short
Day s Work: the author died five years ago,
aged 22, from injuries received through the
breaking of a lighted lamp which fell at the
feet of a younger sister. Rushing to render
assistance, she became herself enveloped in
flames, and both girls lost their lives. The
pathetic accident ended bright literary
hopes.
Miss Turnbull’s original work remains
little more than an unfulfilled promise. She
could write thus before she was thirteen
years old :
Love asks no questions, heeds no bitter words.
Love shineth bright and sharp as warriors’ swords
killeth not;'tis Love that raiseth up.
1 is Love will drink even the bitterest cup,
And smile and dwell on every drop of sweet.
T is Love will run a race with willing feet,
Yet patient wait if Heaven thinks it meet.
In silence, Love will work for many years.
Love weeps to buy a tender thought with tears.
Love liveth on a single glance from eyes
A long while closed—the bright sun in the skies
Gives far less light than Love—Love never dies.
Trite ideas ; yet in their combination there
is a certain freshness and a vigour. These
qualities remain, with deeper emotion, in
the quick and vivid impressions of later
work ;
Oh, beyond Heaven and Song,
Could there be waiting the best!
Past Immortality, sle.ep,
After Eternity, rest.
She could see so well and feel so keenly, her
words came so aptly to meet her vision, that
it is no wonder her work has been praised
rather for what it might have been.
A*
The part that will remain longest is a set
of versions of Heine. Miss Turnbull had a rare
power to paraphrase, and imitate, and some-
times to match that rare poet. She approaches
his melody and cadence, and can even trans-
fuse his emotion. To do this she omits or
varies : a grave defect. Yet what she renders,
she renders exceptionally well, with a femi-
nine adaptation that follows but loosely the
phrase and line, yet; may yield almost an
exact impress of feeling. Compare two
stanzas of her virtue : Lyrical Intermezzo 55.
Wenn Zwei von einander scheiden
So geben sie sich die Hand’,
Und fangen an zu weinen,
Und seufzen obne End’.
Wir haben nicht geweinet,
Wir seufzten nicht “ Weh ! ” und “ Ach ! ”
Die Thranen und die Seufzer,
Die kamen hintennach.
First Bowring ;
When two fond lovers are parted,
They give each other the hand,
To weep and to sigh beginning,
And losing all self-command.
But not one single tear wept we.
No Ah ! or Alas ! did we sigh ;
Our tears and our sighs both together
Too surely came by-and-by.
Dullard. And Theodore Martin :
When it comes to lovers’ parting
Each other’s hands they press,
And then they fall a-weeping,
And sigh sighs numberless.
We wept not, thou and I, love,
Nor “Oh !” nor “Ah ! ” sighed we !
The tears and sighs came after,
But alas ! they were to be.
Dolt. And Brooksbank :
When comes the hour of parting
The tears stream from the eyes ;
Then hands grasp one another
With endless sobs and sighs.
We two wept not at parting;
We made no sigh, no moan !
Our sighs and tears, my darling,
They came when all was done’.
Clumsy. And Monica Turnbull:
When twain depart forever,
Their hands will meet and hold ;
And then they fall a-weeping
With sighs and tears untold.
We wept not when we parted,
With shallow seeming eyes ;
Not then, not then, but later
I hey came, the tears and sighs.
J*
Imperfect, yet easily best of the batch.
That artlessness was her gift as well as
Heine’s ; arid she does reproduce him in
English, though this is not her most success-
ful version. And the more it is looked at,
the more likeable it seems : even the later
loses distaste (it cannot be after , because of
the false rhyme with parted, and for other
reasons). If.in doubt, try yourself :
When two come to parting
ri Eaeh clasps a fond hand :
Their tears are set flowing,
They sigh without end.
But we did not weep, or
A “ Woe’s me ! ” exclaim— *
The tears and the sighing,
I was since that they came.
Too light and flippant. More file :
When a couple have met for parting
They give each other their hands,
And soon begin a-weeping
And sighing that never ends.
But we did not fall to weeping
rni^ r ’ ” 4l Alas ! ” exclaim—
-1“e tears an d endless sighing,
T was afterward they came".

DAN'S SPA CHE ON THE EARTHQUAKE
[BY SPSCIAL FABLR.]
ME frinds an’ countrymen, —Listen
to me ! Niver in the whole carse
av me existence in this grand sub-
luniary sphair, niver in the woildest an’ most
unparalalarralered dhrames av me imagina-
tion, niver in me poobhlic carayr, have Oi
iver sayn, have Oi iver heard, has it iver
intered into the heart av me for wan
moment to concaive, that such a tirrible,
such a devastatering, such an unroighteous
calamity, should stroike innocence in its
beauty and youth in its bloom.
‘ ‘ Me frinds, —The tirrible tirror of it!
Whin Oi hit the bid roisin’ benathe me an’
the sailin’ roisin’ above me as Oi lay in the
arrums of Murpheeus—whin Oi sat up an’
says to meself, says Oi, ‘Good God,’ Oi
says, ‘ fwhat’s this ? ’ —whin Oi wint to me
windy an’ saw the moighty buildings fahl-
ing down with that tirrible crash, an’ the
ground fahling up to mate them—Oi says to
meself, ‘ Shure, ’ Oi says, ‘ it’s the Day of
Judgment at last,’ an’ Oi remimbered me
sins that minit. But ye know what it was
—it was the Earthquake, yis ! —the greatest
an’ most tirrible Earthquake iver known in
the histrry av the whole wurruld ; an’ thanks
be ! here Oi am to till the tale.
“ Me frinds and fellow electors, —Had Oi
the iloquence av Demmystheens, the tragic
jaynius av Harristoephaynes, and the glorious
narraytive powers av the great Grake his-
torian Eurypieds, niver could Oi hope to
do justice to the scane Oi saw befure me !
Tirrible ! tirrible ! Min dyin’, women cryin’,
and harses shyin’ in ivery diriction ! Me
frinds, Oi niver closed me oies wan wink for
three days an’ noights for thinkin’ av it, ex-
cipt whin Oi was aslape from shayr exhars-
tion.
“Mr. Spaker an’ honourable gintlemin,—
Oi wint out, an’ what a mahn can do Oi
did in hilpin’ to save loife an’ property.
Whin the foire came, Oi was there ! Whin
the wather came, Oi was there ! Whin the
polis came, Oi was there ! ‘ Lit me hilp ! ’
says Oi, an’ iverybody that knows me
knows will that Oi did hilp wid the lasht bit
av stringth in these arrums. What wid the
tirror an’ the distriss an’ the anguish av
moind, it was tirrible, tirrible ! Oi niver
hoped to say anything so tirrible in ahll me
loife. The swate ladies, the jewels av their
country, slapin’ in the parrk be the soide av
dliirty Choineymin in all their beauty and in-
nocence and vartue, it intered into me sowl ;
an’ if Oi live to the age av Methusely the
king av the Jews that ye Jade about, niver
will Oi forgit that tirrible toime.
“Me frinds, —Glad Oi am to say that Oi
uphild the honour av me dear country an’
resaved the thanks av the Mare for me
exartions, an’ may Oi niver live to see
another earthquake till Oi doi.”

Curious things have happened to a Lond.
secondhand bookseller. Accidentally a copy of
Secret History of the Court of England , by Lady
Anne Hamilton (first edition) got into his six-
penny box. The book is chiefly a sensational
attack upon George IY, published in 1832. Pains
were taken to suppress it, with the natural result
of a crop of reprints. As much as 1000?. is said
to have been offered for the original edition,
which is valued for its rarity, though probably at
nothing like that sum. The bookseller offered
only 1 51. reward for return. But the announce-
ment of his blunder brought him hundreds of
letters offering to sell, for anything up to 100?.,
later editions of the book—one of them current at
a shilling. Many of the would-be vendors sent
their shilling books along and trustfully awaited a
cheque.
Similar errors are common enough in this
country. One often sees a book worth about 3d.
held as a great treasure because it is 100 years old
or thereabouts. As a matter of fact, very few
books published after 1600 are worth anything on
account of their age ; and in most cases one has to
go back to “ black letter ” before touching really
nigh prices. It is the first edition of a book
that bibliophiles usually value, especially if the
leaves are uncut and the covers intact and clean
as issued. Doubtless the collector’s demand im-
plies a satisfaction as well as a fashion ; but the
origin of the satisfaction is obscure. Is it
scion of the egoistic superstition that covets the
unsullied, the virginal, unsoiled by alien fingers ?
“ Authors and journalists would look better if
they slept more,” says The Lady: “they are
notoriously plain and pasty.” Yes ; and some of
them are notoriously scissors-and-pasty.
In reference to the scheme to weld Russian
refugees into an autonomous Jewish state under
British suzerainty, Zangwill collects in The Fort-
nightly opinions from Eng. men of letters. Hew-
lett wrote: “My reading of history constrains
me to point out that never since this world was
first put in order has a community been perma-
nently established by means of pamphlets or the
opinion of philosophers, to say nothing of literary
men.” Zangwill retorts that “ New Zealand and
South Australia were both established by the
pamphlets of the philosopher. Gibbon Wakefield,”
“ Established by ” ? The claim is too large. They
were established on Wakefield’s plan ; but the
establishment did not depend on his pamphlets,
would have proceeded without them. .
MEDICAL GRAPHOLOGY
It is Dr. Quintard, of Angers in France, who
instructs his medical brethren in the diagnostic
value of handwriting. “You’re a doctor,” says
he, “and you’re called to a patient you’ve never
seen before. He tells you his little yarn and
answers your little questions while you examine
him, and then you make up your mind—wrong,
for a ducat ! For, look you, this patient may be
a nervous kind of person who exaggerates her
troubles ; or, on the other hand, he may be a dull
person who troubles about nothing. She may
wallow in irrelevant detail; he may hide what he
ought to tell you. Would n’t you like an easy
way of testing veracities ?
You find it in handwriting. If it slopes slightly,
with the loops well above the line and a beautiful
array of flourishes, it’s certain you’re dealing
with an individual dowered with, an agreeable
imagination ; while a certain sobriety of script,
with letters loosely formed and sometimes un-
finished, indicates phlegm. Again, by a hand
almost undecipherable, with the o’s and a’s
hermetically sealed, you will recognise the dissim-
ulator. Free and hearty people leave their
vowels open. There’s the character of your
patient at a glance !
Jt
But graphology can do more for you. Suppose
a cure is only possible by regular, tedious treat-
ment : how are you going to know if the patient
follows the prescription ? Often enough people
follow only half—and grumble because they don’t
get well. Look at the hand-writing : a sample
signature will do. If you see a firm fist, with the
i’s dotted and the t’s regularly crossed, that man
has Will! Irregular letters, penned loosely, warn
you against caprices capable of delaying a cure :
and when you see curves clearly formed, careful
punctuation, and every letter doing its duty, be
sure that your client has a docile, methodical
disposition.
Best of all, but to be mentioned delicately, the
—ahem !—question of—er—remuneration. Just
scrutinise your client’s hand-writing before
sending in your bill. If he crams his words
together, leaving the scantiest margin on the paper,
and signs his name in a villainously complicated
tangle, you may conclude that he is stingy ; and,
in order to avoid trouble, it will prove worth while
to be accommodating and apply the minimum
tariff. If the writing is fine, clear, and dignified,
with well-shaped letters, then the patient has a
noble and generous mind, capable of appreciating
at its proper value a physician’s self-sacrificing
labour. If the convalescent writes a large, bold
hand, with plenty of space between his words—
few lines upon a page, and few words in each line
—then you have the man who does n’t count his
cash too closely. Meet him half-way with fees on
the highest scale ; bleed him well; and be grate-
ful to graphology.
EDGAR ALLAN POE.
Thus, you see, “ W.M.” betters the inventor :
Here is no sun, but dusk and endless shade ;
Here is no laughter, whispered speech alone :
Save when afar there sounds a ghostly moan
From some white figure flitting down a glade,
The half-seen vision of a phantom maid ;
Beyond, vast halls with trailing vines o’ergrown,
Where one sits brooding on an ivory throne,
In great, dull gems and kingly robes arrayed.
Once he who rules this realm of mystery
Ate the strange fruit of tragic fantasy :
Within the land of wraiths he entered soon,
And in the poppied cup Life's fever drowned :
Thenceforth he saw Earth’s shapes and shadows croivned
In alien lands with idols of the moon.
The penultimate wants improving.
Said an Eng. barrister to an Eng. judge the
other day : “ Your honour, the defendant has
not turned up!” Said the Eng. judge to the
Eng. barrister : “ Pray, do not use such slipshod
expressions! ” Said the Eng. barrister to the
Eng. judge : “I apologise, your honour, but this
is a high-pressure age; and since your honour’s
days at the bar, we have no longer time to indulge
in perfect English ! ”
Two more of Capt. Wynter’s stories, with a
becoming clerical twang :
Tom Duffield, a typical hunting-man and joint-master
of the Old Berkshire Hounds, was at a farmer’s house
one afternoon when his eyes fell on a picture hanging on
the wail opposite to him. “Hullo!” said he, “ what is
that picture about?” The farmer explained that it was
called “ The Martyrdom of Captain Cook,” who was rep-
resented on his back in a canoe with a swarm of savages
beating out his brains with clubs and tomahawks, while
in the middle distance a clerical-looking missionary, in' a
white tie and bands, was making tracks as fast as his legs
could carry him. The farmer asked Tom for his opinion
on this work of art. “ Well,” replied he, with his mouth
full, “ they seem to have chopped Captain Cook, but no
doubt they ’ll have a good thing with the reverend gentle-
man.”
Some of the old-fashioned clerics were certainly plain-
spoken even when addressing a superior. One well-
known incumbent in the diocese of Oxford was having a
visit from his bishop, who desired to see the parish
schools in which the boys were divided from the girls by
a wall or some strip of garden. Throwing open the door
of the boys’ school, waving his hand, and addressing the
bishop, the rector said, “ That’s the dog pack, m.v lord —
the bitches are on the other side.” This same divine,
when requested to say grace at a cricket dinner, de-
livered himself thus, “For what we are going to receive,
we shall each pay three and sixpence.”
C. C. Fitzgerald, arguing for an Australian
navy in The National Review, adds that “Aus-
tralia offers the curious spectacle of a poor country
where very high rates of wages prevail.” The
fact is, of course, that, judged by the yearly pro-
duction of wealth in proportion to population,
Australia is possibly richest of all countries.
Anybody interested in the drama may read in
April Contemporary an article by Phillip Littell
that is both meat and sauce. L. underlines other
things incidentally, neatly : “We read a bit of
Buskin, and say it’s very like a cloud ; or a bit of
Mr. Frank Bullen, and say it’s very like a
whale...”

Lecture to Ladies only, delivered by the Medical Diagnostician of The Freeman and Wallace Electro-Medical Institute,
Photo, by Richards Co Sydney and Melbourne, at Her Majesty's Theatre, Ballarat (Vic.).
COLAC and WARRNAMBOOL
NEWSPAPERS SAY.
We give here a few brief extracts from Colac
and Warrnambool journals, commenting upon
the delightfully descriptive discourses given by
our Diagnostician :
COLAC.
COLAC “HERALD.”—The Colac Herald com-
ments upon the lecture to “ Ladies Only,” in the
Victoria Hall. The journal says :
“ The lecture was well delivered. The ladies
no doubt appreciated the masterly manner in
which the lecturer handled his subject and the
important knowledge he imparted to them.”
COLAC “ REFORMER.”—This journal eulogis-
tically remarks upon the Diagnostician’s abilities
as a lecturer in its comments upon his lecture to
“Men Only”:
“ In a discourse upon ‘ The Disobedience of
Nature’s Laws,’ the lecturer convinced his
hearers that he knew every' point about which
he was speaking. Many stated it was the best
effort bearing upon such a subject they' had
ever heard.”
WARRNAMBOOL.
WARRNAMBOOL “ STANDARD.”—This journal
announced that on Wednesday afternoon and
evening a lecturer, representing The Freeman
and Wallace Medical Institute, would lecture at
the Town Hall. In the afternoon the lecture
would be For Ladies Only, and the subject,
“ Suffering Woman and Her Heroic Fortitude
to Endure.” The evening lecture would be for
“ Men Only,” the subject being “ Our Disobedi-
ence of Nature’s Laws.” It further added :
“ Judgingby the reports, the speaker must be
a capable lecturer. The Geelong Times writes :
‘ Yesterday' afternoon, at His Majesty’s Theatre,
a lecturer, representing the Freeman and
Wallace Institute, of Melbourne & Sydney, de-
livered a lecture to ladies, and in the evening to
men only'. On both occasions the theatre was
filled. The speaker took up the diseases of both
men and women from the cradle to the grave,
and handled his matter in a manner that
convinced all present he was master of the
subject. These lectures are intended to be of
an educational character, pointing out how'
men should live, and what they should do to
preserve perfect health. It was of little use to
point out the advantages of the Freeman and
Wallace Institutions. They had been estab-
lished in Sydney for 25 years. ‘ The most pro-
gressive ’ is their watchword, and upon those
lines they have created the name they enjoy
throughout Australasia.”
BALLARAT and BENDIGO
JOURNALS STATE.
BALLARAT.
BALLARAT “COURIER,” a journal of high
reputation, comments at some length upon the
general usefulness of the lectures given in Her
Majesty’s Theatre, for “Women Only," as
follows :
“ The unique appearance of a theatre crowded
to the door by the fair sex was presented last
night, when an address was delivered, ‘ Suffer-
ing Woman and Her Fortitude to Endure.’
The speaker is a perfect master of his subject,
and his lecture was listened to with rapt atten-
tion throughout.”
Again, the same journal says :
“In Her Majesty’s Theatre last night the
speaker, in his lecture to ‘ Men Only,’ entitled
‘ Our Disobedience to Nature’s Laws,’ ad-
dressed an appreciative audience, the theatre
being crowded in every part.”
The same journal, commenting upon the litera-
ture of the Freeman and Wallace Institute,
remarks:
“ ‘ Clinical Experiences,' a book issued by
the Specialists of the Institute, is calculated to
be the means of bringing back hundreds of
human wrecks to health and happiness.”
EVENING “ ECHO,” Ballarat, says :
“The high reputation which The Freeman
and Wallace Institute enjoys justifies the pub-
lication of their medical work, ‘ Clinical Ex-
periences,’ 570 pages, 150 Medical Plates."
Again, the Echo comments :
“The Health lectures delivered by the
diagnostician to the Institute are greatly in-
structive, and have drawn crowded houses.”
BENDIGO.
BENDIGO “INDEPENDENT”:—
“ Last evening the Masonic Hall was packed
to overflowing, when the diagnostician of The
Freeman and Wallace Institute, delivered his
lectures to ‘ Ladies Only.’ The lecturer ex-
plained that the lectures were given in the
interests of public morality. He traced the
life of a woman from the cradle to the grave.
The lecture was an interesting one, and was
listened to with great attention.”
BENDIGO “INDEPENDENT”—
“ The diagnostician delivered his lecture to
‘ Men Only,’ ‘ Our Disobedience to Nature’s
Laws,’ last evening in the Masonic Hall. He
spoke at length upon the evils affecting the
human system. He pointed out that The
Freeman and Wallace Institute had at its
disposal the finest Specialists practising in the
Commonwealth.”

Writing under date of August 15, 1904,
Mrs. H. C. Walker, of 5 Tremont Street,
Woodfords, Me., says: “ My sister had a
terrible humour on her shoulder when
she was eighteen months old, causing
intense suffering for two years. We had
several doctors, and tried everything, but
in spite of all we did it kept spreading.
One day it would scab over and then
crack open and a watery matter ooze from
it and the scabs would all fall off. It
would be raw for a time, then scab over
again. Some one recommended Cuticura,
and we immediately procured a box of
Cuticura Ointment, and a cake of Cuticura
Soap. She was much better after the first
bath with warm water and soap, and an
application of the Ointment. Before it
was half gone we saw a marked change
for the better, and she was entirely cured,
without a scar being left, by the one box
of Ointment and one cake of Soap. Her
skin is now entirely clear, and she has
not had a sign of trouble since.”

“ J.Gr.” : I enjoyed the hospitality of an
outback boundary rider for one night during the
drought. Water had given out, and he was using
water-melon in its place. I had tea made with
melon, damper worked up with melon, and corned
beef boiled in melon. The flavor was curious, but
I enjoyed the meal immensely.
The cult of the cow is growing in Maori-
land, and newspapers are beginning to publish
the names of the “best and smartest milkers.”
This adds a new terror to the life of the unfor-
tunate kid who is already roused at 4 a. m. to pursue
a muddy brute called Strawberry. In addition
to being driven out among the beasts when they
should be in bed building up muscle and brain,
the poor little mites will be also worried into
strenuous efforts to break the record. Like
England’s cotton industry, Maoriland’s dairy
farm prosperity is built on the hearts and misery
of its children. The slaves of the cult of the cow.
“ Turrgal ” writes :
Of all Australian ports, surely Derby (nor’-west W.A.)
is the least like a port. I went there for the
first time in a small cutter, and arrived at low
tide. The tide has a rise and fall of 40ft. The
scene is neither romantic nor inspiring. A gentle
slope of mud rises from the edge of the dirty
water to a remote dead-level line of mangroves, which
forms the horizon pro tem. This view is entirely unre-
lieved, stretching on either side as far as vision carries.
The jetty, with its long piles uncovered, looks very
gaunt and very lonely, and a stranger would wonder why
it was put there. It seems miles from the water. Not
another vessel of any sort is in sight. There are never
two simultaneous vessels at Derby. When the tide rises,
another remarkable view is presented ; you can then see,
over the tops of the mangroves, a hare salt plain about a
mile wide. A tramline runs straight across this from the
jetty, and loses itself in a shimmering mirage, where a
few white blobs indicate the houses. Strong tides and
alligators make sea bathing impossible. When you get |
into the town, you can easily imagine yourself 100 miles
inland. Enormous baobab trees give the place a tropical
appearance, which is further borne out by the fact that
Derby sleeps on its verandahs almost throughout the
year. There are no old salts knocking around, and no sea
talk. The people you meet are station hands, drovers,
etc., and the talk is all of sheep, cattle, blacks or kangaroos.
An outback teacher sends this as an
example of what the bush pedagogue has to put
up with :
It was this way. The boys were anxious to save their
country from the devastations of bunny, and (inciden-
tally) the price of skins was high. Bill “ wagged it ”
from school and went rabbitin’, and incidentally Bill’s
dog and Ted’s, Jack’s, Mick’s and Monty’s dogs went too.
Bill saw to that. By the time Ted, Jack, Mick and Mont3 r
had finished their studies and were also ready to go
rabbitin’, Bill, Bill’s dog, and Ted’s, Jack’s, Mick’s and
Monty’s dogs were right in the enemy’s camp, and Bill
had already two bobs’ worth of skins.
As already mentioned, Ted, Jack, Mick and Monty also
had designs on bunny’s hide. So they began to whistle.
They called out, “ Here, boy, here,” and whistled every
kind of whistle they knew. Later on, Bill returned.
When Bill saw how things stood, he hid in a hollow tree
until dusk.
The dogs went home. They wished they’d stayed with
Bill—Bill didn’t get his ribs almost kicked in for going
with that d d fellow.
Bill went to the school next day, but Ted and Mick were
missing :so were all the dogs. Ted and Mick hid in the
trees this time.
Monty went through the performance next day. The
day next Bill again, and Jack with him.
Each time all the dogs went. The dogs were getting
nearly worn out by this time, but were still determined
to keep going to the last.
The teacher got desperate, for the average attendance
was enough to get him the sack. In self-defence he
poisoned all the dogs.
Bill accused Jack, Ted, Mick, and Monty of conspiring
to kill his dog. Jack accused Bill, Ted, Mick and Monty.
Ted, Mick, and Monty made similar wholesale accusa-
tions. By the time the teacher arrived to stop the fight
Mick was the only one with any clothes on him worth
mentioning.
Their parents whacked Bill, Jack, Ted, Mick, and
Monty.
The parents of Bill, Jack, Ted, Mick, and Monty all
interviewed the teacher to know why he didn’t keep
better order.
There was almost a complete disappearance of the
school while the five already mentioned were away
acquiring fresh dogs, for they took Thomas and Samuel
with them, and that left hardly anybody at the school
except Sarah—Bella being laid up with measles.
They all acquired fresh dogs (lawfully or otherwise),
and the only visible result of all the trouble was that
Thomas and Samuel also acquired the rabbitin’ habit.
The average attendance is lower than ever now.

s
His Majesty

Royal Family.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tribute.
My heart is full as it can hold,
Of Sydney’s quaint and gracious ways,
Of all her royal blue and gold
And laughing coves and nooks and bays,
Her crowded town, and winding streets,
Her headlands and her white-sailed fleets.
I close my eyes and see the square,
The flower men with their baskets bright
(Umbrella-shaded from th.e glare),
The vivid awnings, red and white,
The dazzling azure of the sky,
The trams that clang and rush and fly.
I see the busy miles of quay,
The hansoms standing all arow,
The harbor’s shining mystery,
The ferries passing to and fro.
I hear the blind man’s blatant voice
Rising above the city’s noise.
Oh Sydney, till again we meet
My eyes are hungry for your blue !
My heart is aching for your heat,
Your scent, and sound, and radiant hue.
To-night, across the leagues of sea
My soul goes out in love to thee !
VIC.
DOROTHY FRANCES McCRAR.
The Lovers.
One idle hour she sought to see
Whose image ’twas he cherished so
(All fondly certain whose ’twould he),
And found—a girl she did not know.
A trusty maiden’s modest face,
All innocence and purity.
“ What nun is this that fills my place ?
Alas, he loves me not!” sighed sho.
“Nay, daughter, let no foolish fears
Your trust in his devotion mar,”
Her mother said. “ Come, dry your tears ;
That is the girl he thinks you are.”
All fondly curious with love
(Half guessing what he would lay bare)
He rifled her heart’s treasure trove,
And found—a stranger’s image there.
“ This is the man she loves ! ” said he,
And, searching in the noble face,
Read high resolve and constancy.
“ This saint,” he cried, “ usurps my place ! ”
“ Nay, ’ spake his friend. “Your anger cool;
Gaze on that God-like face once more;
And then be satisfied, O fool;
That is the man she takes you for.”
S.A.
C. J. DENNIS.
Dusk and a Boat.
Out from the shallows ! The last flying gleam
Flames from the hill-saddle, crested and cleft.
Nosing the bubbling rush of the stream,
Thrust from the fern-shadows, facile and deft—
Sweep —
Low 1
While the bows lift and go
Up through the quarrelling current-race. Row,
Swinging and swift, from the wide trailing wake ;
Bend to it, shoulders—brown arms, to it—so !
Hearts, beat the rhyme of it out to the sun !
Shoulders, sway true to our chanson that soars
(Time, chant the time of it, viol-voiced One !)
Up through the rattle and knock of the oars.
Swing —
Slow 1
While the blades give and go
Down through the rose of the ultimate glow,
Sundered and shafted, that swims on the de§ps,
Flushing the ford and the reaches below.
Down through the darkness; an anchorite star
Blinks to his shade in the ripple behind ;
Out where the thicketed darknesses are
Mutters a vagrant irresolute wind . . .
Sweep —
Slow !
While the oars dip and throw.
Grey is the flush of a short hour ago,
Stridently whispering down in the hills,
Wanders the wraith of a song that ye know.
vio.
JAS. A. ALLEN.
The Lay of the Lost.
Outcasts under the social ban,
Damned and doomed by our fellow man.
Excommunicate, shunned and shamed,
Unclean ! Unclean ! by the crowd proclaimed.
We fall and our fellows will make no fuss—
A plain pine coffin will do for us.
Brothers, ere spirit its shell forsakes,
Drink we a toast —to the Lord’s mistakes.
Now and again we hear their hail—
Dealers in dogma with faith for sale;
The outcast’s soul they would save at cost
Of all—and it’s little—he has not lost.
But we are contented to take our chance,
Leaving the rest to circumstance.
What matters the doom or the shape it takes?
Another health —to the Lord’s mistakes.
Preaching—aye, pleading, they urge “repent!”
Mockery ! Never to us was sent
Message of mercy and promised peace ;
Ours is a sentence that shall not cease.
Can he repent who is hot with hate,
With a wasted wrath at a callous Fate,
With a broken spirit, a heart all aches?
Repent! Nay, drink to the Lord’s mistakes.
N.S.W.
W. A. PEAKE.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
bat to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for ‘‘copy.’’ Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 2000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed Bui no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood, that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 17, 190 G.
THE “PRODUCER,” AND THE OTHER
PRODUCER.
The recent Sydney Agricultural Show gave the
Foreign-trade papers their usual opportunity to
make feeling remarks about “the producer,” and
Sydney Telegraph, in its excitement, put “The
Producer” at the head of its article in letters
nearly an inch high, and then it gave another big
heading to “Horses, Cattle, and Produce .”
“Produce,” in the vocabulary of the Foreign-
trade paper, means wheat and oats and potatoes
and hay and onions and vegetable-marrows, and
the man who digs up these things is the producer
and the backbone of the country. The man who
makes cloth and machinery is only the cunning,
underhanded and scheming manufacturer, and his
output isn’t produce at all. This attitude has
become so much a matter of course that it is with
something like a shock that one digs up some
plain, hard facts about the respective values of
the two producers. Leaving out all the spread-
eagleism concerning the backbone of the country ”
and so on, State Statistician Hall gives these
figures as regards N.S. W. for 1904:
Total value of all agricultural produce in
N.S. W. (this is the gross return, and out
of it has to come cost of machinery,
freight by cart, railway and steamer, seed,
in many cases rent, also repairs and im-
provements, leaving a mere remainder
for wages) . .. ■ ■ • • £5,413,710
Wages paid by manufacturing industries .. 5,012,758
In other words, the wages alone paid by N.S.IV.
manufacturers aie nearly equal to the whole value
of the State’s agricultural industries. And when
it is considered that only a part of the agricultural
output is available for wages—either the wages of
the farmer himself, of his sons, or of his hired
servants, saying nothing of his wife, who often
works as hard as any of her male relatives and
that the (possibly) £3,000,000 which the bucolic
industry produces net has to be divided among
nearly 80,000 people actually employed in agri-
culture, while the £5,000,000 and over which
manufacturers pay in wages has only to be di-
vided among 68,000 employees, tlie final result is
that manufactures yield about £1 in wages per
employee for short hours where agriculture "pays a
miserable 10s. for long hours. And still, in the
lexicon of Foreign-trade, the agriculturist is the
producer and the backbone of the country, while
the manufacturer is nothing in particular.
* * * *
Even the poor wages which agriculture pays are
only maintained by making the industry a para-
site, and propping it up by the carriage of its
goods at miserably unprofitable rates, while the
manufacturer is fleeced by heavy extra charges to
make good the dead loss on the “backbone of the
country.” And it may be mentioned that the
agriculturist is by no means the only “primary
producer” who does the parasitic act in this
fashion. Here are Statistician Hall’s latest
figures:—
Rates on N.S.W. Railways.
Average Charge
for Carrying 100
tons of goods
for one mile.
Hay, Straw and Chaff 0 3 4
Grain, Flour, &e. (up journey only) .. 0 3 7
Crude Ores 0 3 9
Coal, Coke and Shale 0 5 1
Firewood .. .. .. .. .. 067
Average for all Goods 0 8 8
General Goods (truck loads) .. .. £lO 6
When the farmer is charged an average of about
4s. per mile for 100 tons, and the other primary
producers are also let off at a huge reduction, and
the manufacturer pays from 12s. to £l, a general
average of Ss. Bd. is arrived at. But it is impos-
sible to cli irge the manufacturer enough to quite
make good the dead loss on the farmer, so the
N.S.W. railways are run on a chronic deficit,
except in very exceptional years. They showed a
loss every year under the late Chief Commissioner
Eddy’s management, and under Chief Commis-
sioner Oliver they showed a small and doubtful
profit in two years only.
* * * *
But even this is not all; there are other ways
in which the alleged backbone of the country is a
parasite, and other directions in which the spine
of the land has to be subsidised to keep it from
crumpling up. In N.S.W. the farmer has
hitherto paid practically no local rates what-
ever, while the people of the cities and
towns have been taxed considerably in this
direction. Even the land-tax has fallen
most heavily on the municipalities and least
heavily on the farmer. It is certainly now pro-
posed to give the bucolic person a system of local
government, which should naturally imply local
taxation, but the local government, in the
farmer’s case, is to be accompanied by a huge
system of endowments which will, in many cases,
leave him almost as free from local rates as he
was before. And in order to keep the backbone
of the country from sinking altogether it has been
considered necessary to let him escape from prac-
tically all the costly restrictions as to short hours,
fair wages, half-holidays, sanitation, &c., with
which the factory-owner has to struggle. The
individual, to whom the Foreign-trade press
always alludes as the sturdy, independent pro-
ducer and the principal support of the
community, lives by being allowed to work
his employees for a pittance from jack-
ass to jackass; by being permitted to work
his young children without pay in a fashion
which would land any manufacturer in the police
court, at short notice ; by exemptions from taxa-
tion ; and by enormous subsidies levied for his
benefit on the general community in the shape of
railway concessions. And with all this encourage-
ment the agricultural backbone of N.S.W., in a
good year, only produced about as much gross as
the manufacturer paid away in wages alone.
* * * *
Yet it is not in any great degree the agricul-
turist’s fault that he is a burden, and a fraud, and
a parasite, except in so far that he is occasionally
fool enough to vote for the Freetrade or semi-
Freetrade conditions which make him all
these things. For want of a larger manufactur-
ing population to consume his produce he has
reached, and in ordinary good years he has passed,
the limits of the local market. If N.S.W. had a
sane, high Protectionist tariff it would manufacture
for itself the great bulk of the £14,000,000 worth
or so of goods that it now imports annually from
outside the Commonwealth. That would mean
an immense increase of population in 11 or more
different classes. There would be •
(X) The people who would manufacture these
£14,000,000 worth of goods required for the existing
population.
(2) The people who would manufacture goods to supply
the wants of the people mentioned in class I.
(3) The people who would manufacture goods to supply
the people mentioned in class 11. and so on indefinitely.
(4) The families and dependents of all these people in
classes 1., 11., and 111.
(5) The shopkeepers, builders, the banking, insurance,
and other office people, the railway, tramway, and ferry
employees, carters, teachers, actors, policemen, parsons,
and a hundred other classes whom the people in classes
1., 11., 111., and IV., would employ,
(6) The extra country population—farmers, dairymen,
sheep and cattle and vegetable raisers—whom they would
require to feed the people included in all the classes up
to V.
(7) The families and dependents of the people men-
tioned in classes V. and VI.
(8) The shopkeepers, &e. (as enumerated in class V.),
that these extra farmers, &c. (as enumerated in class
VI. would employ, also their dependents.
(9) To start the circle all over again, the extra mass of
manufacturing population required to supply goods to
the extra population enumerated in classes IV., V., VI.,
VII. and VIII.
(10) To start the whole round a second time, the agri-
culturists, &c. (as enumerated in class VI.), required to
feed the people alluded to in class IX.
And so the circle goes on indefinitely—but then,
uuhappily, it is all a dream as yet. If Australia
had the high Protectionist tariff, if it had manu-
factures to supply practically all its own wants, if
it had the population that that would involve, if
its farmers were feeding the people who manu-
factured for them here instead of in England,
all would be different. But while the only possi-
bilities in the way of large agricultural expansion
consist in export to England, the agriculturist
seems to have no chance to be anything but a poor,
miserable parasite. He has got beyond the
capacity of the'local market. He has to compete
in England with the produce of more fertile
countries, of cheap labor countries, and of
countries which are not burdened with half his
freight charges. Again falling back on N.S.W.
Statistician Hall, these are the figures of the case
for 11)04 (apparently the export figures for 1905
were not made up when the “Year Book” was
published)which mostly refer to the export of the
wheat grown in 1903 :
Export of wheat from N.S.W.
(1904) .. .. .. .. 11,200,000 bushels.
Cost of exporting to London .. 1 )jd. per bushel.
Total cost of export to London,
i.e. , loss through the market
being in England instead of
Australia for want of a large
local manufacturing popula-
tion (about) £520,800.
* ♦ * *
And even this takes no account of an average
item of 4fd. per bushel for getting the wheat to
the seaport, an item which would be partly
saved if there were manufacturing towns dotted
over the interior of the country. .Also, the table
deals with wheat alone ; the whole N.S.W. crop of
wheat in the great boom year alluded to (1903)
was worth less than £4,000,000, and in 1904 it was
worth less than £2,500,000. A loss of over
£500,000 on a £4,000,000 crop, solely because the
manufacturing population is in England instead
of here, is something like an income-tax of 2s. 6d.
in the £ paid by the farmer to support Freetrade.
And if the crop had been larger, and a greater
proportion of it had been exported, the tax would
have been more. Of course the farmer didn t
really pay this amount himself; he couldn’t in many
cases. The community paid it for him by carrying
his grain on the railways at unprofitable rates,
by letting him off payment of local taxes to sup-
port liis own road and bridge and culvert, and by
allowing him to shamefully sweat his employees.
Then it charged the manufacturer extra railway
rates to make up the loss on the farmer’s produce;
it made him pay his own local taxes; it made him
help to pay for the farmer’s roads, bridges, and
culverts; it demanded that he should pay his em-
ployees decent wages for eight hours’ work, and
there is talk in some quarters of reducing the
artisan’s day to six hours ; and, on top of all this,
the Foreign-trade press called the manufacturer a
parasite. And, with all these drawbacks, in 1905,
which was a remarkably good year for agriculture
inN.S.W., though not quite equal to the great boom
year 1904, the manufacturer paid almost as much in
wages alone as the whole agricultural industry was
worth. A man who does things like that, with a
mere shadow of a tariff, and with all manner of
drawbacks and special exactions, should be en-
couraged. There is plainly far more hope of
wealth, progress and population in manufactures
than in all the blessed agricultural “ backbone of
the country.”
COMMONWEALTH LIFE ASSURANCE.
Two Bills dealing with life assurance have, it is
said, been prepared for the Australian Parliament
to worry ; and, though it would be an unspeakable
Bill that wouldn’t improve the present position,
no measure will be as good as it ought to be that
does not provide for Federal Life Assurance.
Even the anti-Socialist ought to have little to say
against that. It would interfere with no vested
interests. There are one or two proprietary life
offices certainly; but practically there is no good-
will whatever in Australian life assurance. Also,
it would cost the Commonwealth nothing at
all. The insurers would put up their own
capital, and pay the expenses of management.
At the very most the Commonwealth would be
required to advance £lO,OOO or £20,000; most
likely, however, it would not have to advance a
penny. The largest and most successful life
offices are purely mutual; they started without
any advance or paid-up capital, and the Common-
wealth Insurance Department could start on the
same footing.
* * * *
The anti-Socialist professes to be deeply inter-
ested in encouraging thrift. Even G. H. Reid
says he is burning with a desire to use the func-
tions of the State to promote that virtue, and any
better way of encouraging it than by establishing
an insurance department is not visible. For the
most effective way to encourage thrift is obviously
to provide a means by which the results of the
thrift are sure. And the State can’t be sure of
any concern which is outside its own control. No
system of mere inspection can give complete pro-
tection. If it is so complete as to amount to
management, then the State might as well manage
the thing itself and take the responsibility. To
take the real management out of the hands of
directors who have the responsibility is unfair ; to
take the responsibility without the management
is insane. Yet the State cannot give assurance
without undertaking management and control.
Under those circumstances, if it wants to give
perfect assurance, which is the most effective way
of encouraging thrift, it must establish and main-
tain its own Life Assurance Department.
* • * *
A Commonwealth Life Assurance Department
would not be called upon to spend vast sums in
tongue and shoe leather. It no more need canvass
for policies than the State canvasses for Savings
Bank deposits. Its first obvious duty will be done
when it draws up a set of tables, and makes a plain
announcement that it is prepared to take charge
of and invest the premiums which the public
pays in, and is equally prepared to pay
out on maturity of the policy. At present
the public is paying from 14 to 70 per cent, of its
premiums, not for insurance, but in salaries of
canvassers, superintendence of agents, travelling
expenses, &c. And there are other expenses
which are never reckoned as such. For purposes
of advertisement private life offices build palaces
which they call offices, and for which they get
a return of about 2 per cent. The other 2 per
cent, ought to be charged up as expenses, but it
isn’t. There are old-fashioned, conservative offices
in Great Britain which employ no canvassers,
&c. They offer insurance to such as come in
and apply for it, provided the applicants can
satisfy the medical referee; and their expense-
rate is no more than 5 or 6 per cent. The
Commonwealth Department could offer all the
advantages of offices of that type, and many
besides ; for it would become known to 50 per
cent, of the population, where the private office of
that type would not become known to 5 per cent.
When it soaked into the minds of the public that
every 10 per cent, of premiums saved out of
expenses meant from 25 to 50 per cent, higher
bonuses, the rush of insurers would probably be
greater than an army of canvassers could whip up.
If the A.M.P. could cut down its expense rate by
10 per cent, of premiums in this way, it would
be able to divide 29 per cent, more in
bonuses; and if other offices could get their
expenses down to the same level, their bonuses
would improve to a vastly greater extent than
that. Even, however, if there were no rush to
the Commonwealth office —even if it is true that
the average person has to be almost forced into
taking out a policy—the department would cer-
tainly get the business that would be most likely
to stick ; and, in spite of a pretty general belief
to the contrary, that is the only life assurance
business that is well worth having.
* 4 • •
But the chances are that, if the average person
has to be driven to take a policy, it is usually
because (1) he has his vague doubts whether it is
good enough to pay large sums for the mainten-
ance of expensive staffs ; and (2) he has his vague
doubts whether the money will be there when he
or his widow call for it. There isn’t a director of
the A.M.P. who was a director 20 years ago ; and
the manager and actuary are not the same as they
were when a 20-year-old policy-holder began
paying his premiums. In 20 years more there
may be as complete a change—it is almost
certain there will be. The present directors and
management of a private office may be financial
saints; in 20 years time, when the widow calls round
—and when, so far as she is concerned, it is more
desirable than it ever was that the office should
be manned by saints—the distressed but hopeful
lady may find that the saints have gone to their
reward and the 'premises have been closed for
repairs. When, on the other hand, the Common-
wealth closes its doors, it will be because the
Commonwealth has gone smash. In a private
office there can be no guarantee that 20 years
hence the office will not be in the hands of sharks.
In a Commonwealth office there is at least
the guarantee that 20 years hence the office will
be as good as Australia itself is at that time.
» * * *
It is not proposed that the Commonwealth
should compel anybody to join its Life Assurance
Department—except newly-appointed Common-
wealth officers; membership ought to be com-
pulsory in their case. There is no reason why
they (and indirectly the Commonwealth) should
pay large amounts to cover the cost of premium-
collection, etc., when the Commonwealth can
collect the premiums from itself practically
without any cost whatever. Everybody else
would be just as free to join private offices as they
are to-day. But while that was so, Australians
would have the chance of trusting Australia with
their life assurance business, ana any Common-
wealth Assurance law that does not provide for
that much, leaves out the simplest and most
effective means of providing Australians with
life-assurance that they can be sure of.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Craving for an Imported General.
On Friday of last week the S.M. Herald, which
always yearns to import something or somebody,
broke out into two shrieks. One was a positive
shriek because Defence Minister Flayford doesn t
propose to import a new military commander, but
to appoint a local man. The other was a tenta-
tive shriek, because the Herald doesn t yet feel
quite so sure as it would like to be that btate
Premier Cakruthers intends to sack the Aus-
tralian-born Chief Commissioner of N.S.W. rail-
ways and put an imported man in his place. On
the military question it asked wildly :
How can we expect or hope, then, to gain efficiency in
military preparation with none but Australian officers at
the head of our forces ?
How, indeed ? For over 50 years, ever since the
Crimean war let daylight in on the ineptitude of
British military methods, the want of any
decent organisation has been the despair of the
British army. Yet the Herald, with its im-
porting disease thick upon it, holds that it is
only in that disorganised army that we can hope to
find the leader we want. The British army is the
one possible school, and the man who isn t trained
there is useless. True that Joubert, Cronje,
De Wet and De la Bey were not trained there,
or anywhere else in Europe, and they held up
their end for three years with their home-made
tactics against the British army at great odds.
True, also, that the only land attack we have to
fear is through the landing of a regular army on
these shores —an attack by some such Power as
Japan or Germany—and there is not one officer
now on active service in the British army who
has fought, unless perhaps under a foreign flag,
against a highly-trained and disciplined foe, such
as Japan or Germany. There are plenty who
have scrambled fiercely with Soudanese, Arabs
and Pathans, and scrapped manfully with Dacoits
and the like, but the last semblance of a really
organised enemy that Britain met was the
Russian army that defended Sebastopol, and its
tactics were crude enough—also, the officers
who served there are dead, anyway. France,
Germany, Austria and Italy are in the same
situation ; the men who fought under Chanzy, Von
Moltke, Benedek, and La Marmora are almost
all dead, or superannuated, and there only remain
book theorists and men trained in manoeuvres
and sham fights. It was the same with Japan
until the other day, both on land and sea. And
Australia can do as they do—grow its own book
theorists', and have its own manoeuvres. It can
certainly procure from Britain a commander who
has seen plenty of active service, and whose ex-
perience would be invaluable if this country were
attacked by Dervishes or Beloocheese, but then
Australia will not be attacked by any such people;
and it is a question whether, when the only pos-
sible kind of enemy arrives, that kind of com-
mander with that kind of experience, might not
find that he had a whole lot to unlearn after
the battle had commenced. He might have
forgotten the book-theories, by which such
men as Oyaha and Togo evolved themselves,
while learning the best method whereby to
meet a whirlwind of black spearmen, or to
dislodge the mountain snipers who peg away
with their long “ trade ” guns behind rocks, and
the absence of the knowledge he had forgotten
might be of more importance than the presence
of the knowledge he had acquired. It certainly
seems a fair and reasonable thing to try an Aus-
tralian commander. He can learn his business
just the w y that the present French, German,
Austrian and Italian commanders have done, and
just as the Japanese commanders did before they
had experience with their first organised and dis-
ciplined opponent. Also, as far as concerns any
warfare with a disciplined enemy—the only kind
we need fear —he can learn his business just as the
British generals of to-day have done and are
doing. And if he hasn’t learned any Beloocheese
or Tibetan tactics—which are the principal kind
this generation of British officers has acquired
in the battle field—there is always the hope that
he may get on better without them.

The Craving for an Imported Railway Genius.
Again the S.M. Herald puts up an utterly
illogical wail for an imported Chief Railway
Commissioner for N.S.W. :
We shall do best to cut ourselves and our chief State
asset clear from the conditions which have encumbered
us, and make a fresh start with new Commissioners and
an amended Railway Act. We want a Chief Commis-
sioner with wide experience to take charge, and after
that we want to ensure that he will have a free hand, un-
embarrassed by jealousies in the Commissioners’ room, or
by partisan disaffection in the service, or by political con-
trol under any specious guise, or by a defective Act ....
It would serve no good purpose to constitute a new' Com-
mission which would perpetuate the evils of the old. To
prevent this the Railway Act must be so amended as to
give the Chief Commissioner the power and responsi-
bility. The majority vote must come to an end. The
Chief Commissioner must govern in fact and earnest as
well as in name. To overlook this primary condition
would only leave the door open to a still more flagrant
repetition of what has gone before.
This is the ease put forward by every paper which
demands that Chief Commissioner Oliver shall be
got rid of to make room for an imported man (like
Dick Speight, of Victoria), and it is a case of
which any fair-minded paper which isn’t muddled
in its mind should be utterly ashamed. When
cut up into verses this is how it reads
(1) Chief Commissioner Oliver should be discharged
because he failed to agree with the other two Commis-
sioners. That is the only accusation against him, and m
the most important case brought before the Commission
of Inquirv it was held that he did right to disagree. The
Herald reckons that the disagreements were brought
about “ by jealousies in the Commissioners’room,” and
by the evils of a system which made Oliver Chief only in


name. But he is an Australian, and must go all the
same. .
(2) The new imported genius, the Herald reckons,
would not get on a bit more smoothly than Oliver if he
were made nominal Chief under the same conditions as
Oliver had to struggle w ith. In fact, it suggests that he
would probably do worse.
(3) The new imported British expert should, in the
Herald’s opinion, come in under a set of conditions
which will make the position possible.
(4) And the Australian expert should go out, in the
Herald’s opinion, because he couldn’t get along smoothly
under conditions which were impossible, and which no
one would dream of asking the British man to face.
(5) Oliver has produced very good results from the
railways, despite the fact that he was hampered by con-
ditions under which the S.M. Herald wouldn’t ask an
imported British manager to produce good results, or
anv results at all. But that doesn’t count in his favor.
(6) The idea of giving Oliver, the Australian who has
served N.S.W. well, a chance to show what he can do
under the same favorable conditions that it is proposed
to give to a British stranger is, in the Herald’s idea,
absurd. Oliver is only an Australian.
(7) And if the imported English expert gets on without
friction (which he can hardly avoid doing if he is abso-
lute boss) and yet produces no better cash results than
Oliver, the Herald will quote him to the Day of Judg-
ment, or thereabouts, as an evidence of the necessity of
importing men for every well-paid billet. And, inferen-
tially, the mean and silly old paper will point at Oliver
all the rest of his days as a proof of Australian incapacity
because he didn’t do what the Herald itself admits
couldn’t be done, i.e., keep two men in subjection who
had authority equal with his own.
Once more, unless the Herald editor can run that
paper coherently as one of three cantankerous
editors, all with equal power, or unless Car-
ruthers can get along smoothly as one of three
equal Premiers, the other two being his consistent
enemies, neither the Herald nor Carruthers has
any right to throw out the chest of self-righteous-
ness or protrude the stomach of patronage in this
case.
State Kids.
About State children :
Recently there has been in NiS.W. an epidemic of
cruelty to State children on the part of monsters to whom
they have been indentured. That is to say, a number of
cases have been brought to light, and a few fiends have
been brought to justice, or something less than justice.
Unfortunately, there is more than a suspicion that this
torturing of State waifs is fairly common, and,
as the Chief Justice said recently at Goul- '
burn, the defencelessness of these unfortunate children
made necessary the passing of sentences that would
strike terror into the hearts of such as were only to be
appealed to through fear of the consequences. One of
the Goulburn fiends was sentenced to imprisonment, and
the other to a fine of £l5O. That was by the Chief J us-
tice, who evidently had a proper appreciation of his
responsibility as the protector of the helpless children
who are, owing to their misfortunes, doomed to a period
of what is supposed to be benevolent slavery.
Stipendiary Magistrate McNevin, of Burwood (Sydney),
however, had different ideas when Ellen Downing, of
Five Dock, was brought before him on a charge of having
cruelly beaten a State girl, aged 14, and otherwise mal-
treated her to such an extent as to reduce her to
a state bordering upon idiocy. Before going to
Downing’s service the child was sworn to have
been bright and intelligent. She was returned
to Ormonde House as “useless” by this brazen virago,
and when the nurses came to examine her she was found
to be a mass of bruises, and in such a state of hysterical
nervousness that she was almost unable to answer even
the kindest questions. The Crown pressed for a
term of imprisonment to act as a deterrent, but
Stipendiary Magistrate McNevix said he “was not
disposed to accept the suggestion that the case
was one calling for the infliction of a term of imprison-
ment as the accused had a young child to nurse, and he
thought the infliction of a ‘ substantial fine ’ of £2O
would be a deterrent to others.” Well, either Stipendiary
Magistrate McNevin or Chief Justice Darlev is wrong.
Mr. McNevin must have been aware of how the Chief
Justice dealt with similar cases, and he deliberately
ignored the lead of the Judge. So either the Chief Jus-
tice or the Stipendiary Magistrate is quite unfit for his
billet, and the Department of Justice ought to signify
which it considers is wrong. Common humanity declares
in favor of the Judge.
Five Dock, where this child was so hideously mal-
treated, is a suburb of Sydney and a place where inspec-
tions are fairly frequent. As a matter of fact, it was
sworn that the inspections were frequent, and al-
though the inspectors noticed that the child was
becoming scared and losing flesh they failed to discover
the systematic torture to which she was being subjected.
At any time an examination of the child’s body would
have proved the sort of savage Mrs. Downing was, but the
examination was never made. The inspectors, no doubt,
thought Mrs. Downing was an ordinary human being, but
State inspectors should consider every woman who has a
child in her care a hardened criminal till they have
proved her to he otherwise, and then they should con-
stantly regard her as a person who is about to
become a criminal, and be constantly on the look-
out for the first symptoms. On all occasions
the bodies of the children should be examined
for signs of violence, and the law should be amended so
as to protect these children in the first case from the fury
of fiends like Ellen Downing, and in the second from the
mercy of magistrates like Mr. McNevin, of Burwood. It
is hard to say which the child requires the most protec-
tion from—in The Bulletin’s opinion magistrates like
Mr. McNevin are the more dangerous.

Cohen’s Straight Tip.
Fob once in a way an English boat has brought
Australia something that ought to do it good.
The desirable import is nothing more, however,
than a newspaper paragraph to the effect that the
Canadian Province of Ontario had asked J. Bull for
a conversion loan of £1,200,000, and had got only
£120,000. It doesn’t seem a matter of much Aus-
tralian consequence, perhaps; but when it has
properly sunk in, it ought nevertheless to give the
Australian State-Frights people a violent start.
Canada —the Canadian Federation —has borrowed
in London at a rate that works out at just under
3 per cent, interest. It has 21s per cent, stocks
which were selling when the mail left at 86
ex dividend. Yet, though Canada—the Cana-
dian Commonwealth —can borrow at under
3 per cent., and (in view of its sinking fund)
has, in fact, money thrown at it, Ontario,
which is a Province with a population as great
as that of N.S. Wales and Queensland put
together, could not float a per cent, loan—
could, indeed, get only one-tenth of what it
asked. Moreover, the refusal wasn’t because
Ontario had been on a financial jag ; on the con-
trary, this was the very first external loan Ontario
had ever sought, and until it floated some Treasury
bills a while ago to build the North Bay railway, it
had no Provincial debt whatever. Ontario and Mani-
toba stood together in the matter of having abso-
lutely kept out of the pawnship. The £1,200,000
asked for from Bull was to pay off the Province’s
Treasury bills; and the loan would not have
added a dollar to the Provincial debt. Under the
circumstances, failure seemed to Ontario too ridi-
culous to think about; and it was only on second
thoughts that it had the loan underwritten.
Yet, but for that underwriting, Ontario —this
Province that is half as populous again as N.S.
Wales—would by this time have had to be
making something like a forced levy upon
its local banks, or telling the holders of Treasury
bills that their paper was compulsorily re-
newed. And Cohen, whose other name is
Bull, is right. Why, for instance, should he
lend money to N.S. Wales or S. Australia or
Westralia, whose position is the same as Ontario’s
—or will be when the Bbaddon clause dies—why
should Cohen lend to these States when the prac-
tical means to pay interest belong to some other
authority — in this case the Common wealth? Cohen
tells Ontario that he will lend to Canada, but he
isn’t going to fool round double-banking the
bill of sale; and, anyhow, he isn’t going
to lend to any scrap of Canada when there
is a power exercising authority over the
whole country. He isn’t going to know any frac-
tion of the country in this money-lending business
when there is an authority that can give security
over the whole lot. Sooner or later Cohen will
tell the Australian States the same thing—and
Heaven knows in the next seven years he will
have chances enough to tell them, seeing the
number of loans that must come up for renewal in
that period. If the Commonwealth had a sinking
fund, invested in Australian securities, it would
soon be in a position to save the States from
disaster when that sort of thing happened ; it
could take up the loan itself, or at least take up a
great deal of it and finance the rest. What stands
out so far that even a States.-Frights politician,
should be able to see it is that, if Bull-Cohen
will not lend to Ontario to renew its bills, it
is lunacy to expect him to lend again and again
to the six Australian States even for renewal
nurposes, seeing that the Australian States are
vastly more heavily burdened with debt than
Ontario is. It is Cohen’s tip to the Australian
States to leave off playing the vain, cantankerous
goat and to get the debts passed on to the
Commonwealth promptly ; and it is Cohen’s tip
to Australia to start a sinking fund, invested in
State securities, whether the States leave off
being vain, cantankerous goats or not.

A Hundred Million Asset.
As a national asset, Queensland’s 40 million acres
of forests have been set down by experts at
£100,000,000. Nevertheless, up till a few years
since, nearly all the soft woods used in the State
were imported,—mostly from America. With the
usual prejudice against everything Australian,
the old Boom and Burst Governments imported
teak from India and pine from the land of the
Harvester swindle to furnish their railway
carriages, and walnut from the Cold Land for
other purposes. But already all this is changed.
The railway workshops at Ipswich use none but
local Woods, which are found to be riot
only much cheaper, 'but also much better.
The whole of the Timbers used in the
fitments at the' Brisbane Lands pile are of native
woods. But for an asket estimated at £100,000,000
it is complained that the forests give too little
revenue to be worth considering. Last year all
that was got in was £12,000 ; and it is suggested
that, in order to increase the amount and make
everyone pay his fair due, the royalty should be
collected, not from the cutters, but from the
millers. Some small royalty should be charged,
no doubt; but this isn’t a branch of the subject
that need be worried over. The importance of
the forests is not to be judged by the amount of
revenue they can yield directly, but by the num-
ber of men they can furnish with employment.
The forests may be valued as a national asset at
£100,000,000 ; but that means little more than that
here is the raw material out of which Aus-
tralia may earn £100,000,000 of wages. The
disposition of the politician to judge things
by the amount of money credited to
them in the Treasury is a good disposition to be
suspicious about. It has been, for one thing,
largely responsible for the wholesale destruction of
forests. Ministers have consented to the fire-
stick being put to hundreds of thousands of
pounds worth of raw products, because the
Treasury would get nothing to speak of out of them
—nothing directly, anyway —and the Treasury
wouldn’t trouble to see what it could get in-
directly—simply because behind the firestick was
a man with ss. or 10s. per acre in his hand.
Whether things will ever improve as long as the
Minister of Lands is also Minister of Forests is
doubtful; certainly they are not likely to improve
until the Minister of Forests is capable of throwing
himself as Minister of Lands out of the office,
when he hangs around in order to pouch a few
more acres ot forest land, and misapply the sale-
proceeds by using them as revenue.
The Great Improvement Lease.
Tom Nicholls writes from Queensland
Before the N.S.W. Lands Commission lapses, it might
give the public a glimpse of the •present administration,
especially in connection with Improvement Lease policy.
A settler between Singleton and Richmond asked
the Lands Department to make available for home-
stead selection a certain classification area, but the
reply was that the Department intended offering the area
as an Improvement Lease ; and this, mind you, in face of
the fact that the place thereabouts is studded with dairy
farms; in face of the fact that this area has a double
frontage to permanent water, and that the average
annual rainfall is something over 30in. ; and regardless,
too, of the fact that the spot is situated within 100 miles
of Sydney.
Well, I tried my luck from here (S.W. Queensland) and
asked the Department to make the area, or part of it,
available for homestead selection. I added that I would
select 1280 acres, and would bring with me other family
men who would take the balance —the whole of the area
if available. Further, I undertook to at once place any
sum of money within reason in the hands of the Depart-
ment as a guarantee of our bona fules. Also, I pointed
out that I was a native (as were my friends) of N.S.W-
But all that failed to budge the Deform crowd —which is
so very desirous of peopling the dear old “ Mother State.”
(Its troubles about the Old Girl!) I enclose the reply
that came to my request: “ It is intended to offer the
land in question'as an improvement lease.”
Now, it so happened that, about the time I wrote the
Department on behalf of myself and friends—all of us
working men who want a home for the nippers and a show
of giving them some schooling—it happened, I repeat,
that a certain wealthy bachelor asked the Department to
make this identical area available for improvement lease.
And this middle-aged gent already owns the very cream
of the land thereabouts (outside dairy farms), miles and
miles of the best of it. Well, the Department had to decide
whether to let the stout middle-aged bachelor ha vet he 'land
(he already held miles of land), ortogive closersettlemerita
chance. And the middle-aged bachelor (who has miles
of land already) got the area. The Deform gentlemen
(who nearly died of shock when Crick let a man have an
improvement lease somewhere out on the verge of
Hades) are playing it pretty low down when they offer for
improvement lease land that would keep several families,
as in the case under notice. The Department assures us
that there is “ a large tract of country ” thereabouts similar
to that which we asked for. The Department is a [libellous
statement cut out] and I don’t mind tilling it so. I’m
satisfied that political land agency is rife in Sydney to-day,
and before I, at all events, can hope for a home, I must
get a relative into Parliament.
In partial corroboration of the above, The Bulle-
tin has before it a letter, dated last month, from
the N.S.W. Lands Department, in which it
defines the land (situated in the county of Hunter,
which is just behind Newcastle), and declines to
consider the request for a homestead selection on
the ground that this area is intended to be let
“on improvement lease at a moderate rental.”
Apparently it was being saved up for this inferior
and unproductive form of tenure. Hunter county
is a fertile region with four inhabitants to every
five square miles, and the fact that it has alto-
gether too much big landlord, and needs people
a great deal more than it does improvement
leases, seems to be borne out by the fact that it
has a diminishing population; it had 1973 people
in 1891, and only 1792 in 1901.
A Lesson in Closer Settlement.
Victorian Minister of Agriculture Swinburne
was up in North Gippsland lately, and summed
up what he saw at Boisdale in this way : “ Some
day the State will have to do exactly what is
being done here.” That estate, of 6000 acres,
Foster Brothers have cut up into farms of 100
and 150 acres, each with a comfortable brick
cottage, sheds, stables, etc. There is a central
cheese and butter factory and an experimental
farm, the lessons learned from which are handed
on to the tenants. Foster Brothers have of late
abolished the tender system under which the
tenant —often a Syrian, Indian, or Chinkie —
sweated himself and his children, and ground the
last ounce he could get out of the land ; they now
offer the farms at an annual valuation, and pick
their tenants. With itself as landlord, and assum-
ing that the tenant is guaranteed some reasonable
fixity of tenure, that would be almost an
ideal system of Closer Settlement for any State to
adopt. All the tenant has to find is the stock,
the muscle and some brains. He gets the use of
sufficient land to make a good living, yet not so
much that he can afford to let any lie idle ; he
would get the benefit of scientific experiment and
supervision; and he would pay less rent
than the Boisdale farmers, for the State tenant
has no private landlord to maintain. That is the
sort of work Swinburne should undertake at, say,
Willaura (Vic.) at once, not “some day.” Here
the rascally profit-sharing system obtains. Half-
a-dozen owners of 45,000 acres have cut up
their huge blocks and let them to landless toilers.
They provide nothing but the land and the fences
—neither houses, implements, nor experimental
farms. The tenant has to plough, sow, harrow,
fertilise, reap and hand over a third of the gross
returns, with the infamous addition that the
tenant gets absolutely nothing till the grasping
soil-owner has first collected a bag (of .wheat) to
the acre. These 45,000 acres were, previous to the
drought of a few years back, merely pasturage for
sheep, and worth £3 per acre at most—which
means that they were worth an annual rental of
3s. or 4s. per acre. This year’s results pan out
like this :—Gross returns from the blocks, 156,000
bags of wheat; landlord’s share, about £30,000, or
13s. 4d. an acre; tenant’s share, after paying
for manure, labor, etc., £13,350. That is in a good
season; in a poor one the landlords would get
about the same return, the tenant little or no*-
thing. And on top of all this the tenant is
perennially improving the land-shark’s property,
yet gets not one farthing for that improvement.
