“ Natural Industries a Discouragement.
“Real Australian” writes:—
I have visited Argentina and Brazil and compared
Argentina from an agricultural productive standpoint
with Australia, the comparison being all in favor of
Argentina, because, while its climate and soil are like
Australia’s, it has water and cheap labor without stint,
to make the climate and soil intensely prolific. Argen-
tina is one vast pampa or plain—treeless,-.stone less, well-
watered on the surface and below, ready for the plough,
and capable of growing every plant, and rearing every
beast most useful to mankind. The breeding of horses,
cattle and sheep is conducted in Argentina more scienti-
fically than in Australia, and as the years roll by our
stock-breeders will send to Buenos Ayres for prize
animals. Argentina will always be able to beat Australia
in the markets of Europe, with its wheat and meat and
all the produce of the farm and estancia, because its ports
are only three weeks’ steam from Europe. Buenos Ayres
is 7160 miles from Southampton and Melbourne 11,267.
Droughts are rare in Argentina and do not last long, and
water is found everywhere by sinking. Lucerne yields
four or five crops a year, and carries four or five times
more cattle and sheep than natural grasses, while the
cattle are double the weight of those fed on pampa grass.
The Riverina country of N.S.W. is identical with the
lucerne districts of Argentina. That country’s wool pro-
duction is increasing rapidly, and though the wool is a
third less weighty and a third less valuable than the
Australian, it is improving in quality and quantity every
year. Cheap Italian labor is plentiful, and immigration
is constant from Southern Europe. We cannot hope to
keep up the competition with Argentina in primary pro-
ducts, and the Republic’s largely-increased production
may very soon drag down prices in London of meat,
butter, wheat, and wool. In Argentina there are seven
great freezing works with a killing capacity of 54,000
animals a day! The United States can scarcely
grow enough wheat to feed its 80,000,000 peo-
ple, and Argentina, with 80,000,000 acres of wheat
lands, will feed both Britain and the United
States. Therefore is the need pressing to foster our
manufacturing industries to augment the number of con-
sumers within our gates and of our own foodstuffs. Before
our new manufactures are well under weigh under a re-
vised Federal Customs tariff, the United States will be
swamping the Pacific markets with the overplus of its
goods, and there is no reasonable doubt that America
means to capture the sovereignty of the Pacific. This is
what Secretary to the Treasury Shaw said four years ago,
referring to the Spanish-American war: “We then
gave notice to the world that we proposed to
police the street in which we live. The United
States will police not only the street in which
it lives, but the entire Western Hemisphere and
with it all the countries and islands washed by the Pacific.
American wealth and energy, plus Hawaii and the Philip-
pines which are ours, plus the Isthmian Canal which we
shall surely construct, plus the merchant marine which
we shall surely build, will transfer the sovereignty of the
Pacific from the Union Jack to the Stars and Stripes.”
This flamboyant spread-eagleism is in deadly earnest!
The competition of rival producing nations is becoming
keener, and America and Argentina are countries whose
antagonism must be feared by Australia and proper pro-
vision made for instantly meeting and combating it. We
should strive to attract population here by the sim-
plest plan—the public and private support of existing
Australian industries, and by granting the amplest
succor to individuals who will start new industries and
manufactures of whatever kind. This can be done best
by at once establishing a broad and enlightened scheme
of Protection to Australian industry. Without Protec-
tion we cannot have manufactures, and without manu-
factures we cannot expect more population. More people
in Australia would mean more consumers and more de-
fenders, both of which classes we require badly if the
expansion of Australia is to keep pace with the expansion
of America and Argentina.

“ Vica” : Westralia has got together a queer sort
of stop-gap Ministry. Mooke (Premier), Keenan
(Attorney-General), and Price (Works) have been
in Parliament only since the last election, smd the
two latter have seats snatched from the Labor
party, but which are not likely to be lost to that
party for any length of time. Premier Moore (he
always calls himself Newton Moore) is a fat,
short-winded Majah of militia, whose, role in
politics has hitherto been to bark about immigra-
tion on Canadian lines. He seems to have
become Premier chiefly because he is an easy-
going sort of person whom nobody dislikes very
actively. Treasurer Frank Wilson is a capitalist
in timber and coal. He is described by some as
one of the best-hated employers in the West.
Keenan, who turned out Labor-man Johnson at
Kalgoorlie, is a lawyer with a yet unproved repu-
tation for cleverness. Works Miiiister Price,
who is supposed to be “ progressive,” ran a steam
laundry at Fremantle before becoming a states-
man. Connelly, M.L.C., is an old Parliament-
arian of the useful and harmless type. Probably
the best-placed of the Ministers is H. Gregory
(Mines and Railways), who, though unpopular
with his officers, showed that he understood his
work when he held the Mines billet before.
Piesse, M.L.C., is one of an ancient Sandgroper
family, which owns most of the South-east. The
members of the family, from their hatred of elec-
tion troubles and their care of themselves, are
known as the PiESSE-at-any-price crowd.

Re the question as to whether Riverina be-
longs by the right of ancient and dusty inherit-
ance to Victoria or to N.S.W., why not let
Riverina itself decide the question ? If it wants
to remain pare of N.S.W., that should outweigh
all the faded parchments that the Age or Thomas
Bent can dig up from all the recesses of history
or legend. If it wants to join Victoria, that
should be sufficient in itself, provided it is willing
to take its fair share of the N.S.W. debt along
with it when it changes its State allegiance. If
it desires to set up for itself, that should also be
definite. Live people amount to a great deal
more than dead sheepskin, and this principle
should be recognised all over Australia,. which
badly wants to be a great deal more subdivided.
If the capital and metropolitan district of any
State keep an outlying region in that State
by compulsion, it is just the case of Ireland
or Poland over again in a modified form, and
Australia has expended a good deal of sympathy
on Ireland and Poland. North and Central
Queensland, which were sold in bondage to Bris-
bane as the price of the State’s adhesion to the
Commonwealth, are cases even more to the point
than Riverina. When Australia starts to improve
its Federal Constitution, the radical amendment
of clauses 123 and 124, which make the consent of
a State Parliament necessary before any outlying
district can secede, should receive earnest atten-
tion.
—.-
Apropos of the Buninyong-Criffel collision in
Sydney harbor, it is surely time that some more
definite rules were made about towage. A tug
carries a certain disarrangement of lights to inti-
mate that it is towing something. The tow may
consist of one or more vessels behind, or of a vessel
alongside. In this particular case the economy
was effected of towing a vessel alongside and one
behind. The Buninyong people saw the towing
signal, and they saw, alongside the tug, the vessel
that was in tow. The idea that there was also a
vessel lumbering along in the gloom behind the
tug didn’t occur to them, and the towing signal
gives no hint as to the number of craft that one
tug may be dragging. So the craft that was
sagging away at the end of half-a-mile (more or
less) of rope rose out of the darkness and took the
Buninyong by surprise, long after the tug and
what appeared to be its only burden had gone by.

Thus Herbert, Easton, who recently arrived in
Melbourne from S’ Africa, where he put in a
lengthy spell inquiring into settlement matters :
As far as Britishers are concerned, there has been
but poor progress, and, while the Boers are increasing
in numbers, many of the British people and colonials have
been driven out of the country. That is largely the
work of foreign financiers and their policy of employing
Chinese coolies and cheap Continental labor in place of
that of Britishers. Though Johannesburg is turning
out £2,000,000 sterling in gold every month, the depres-
sion is far worse there than it was when the out-
put was half as much. It lias been many times repre-
sented that the employment of Chinese coolies
would bring ; about an influx of, and provide
work for, white labor, but the only result has
been to increase cheap Continental labor. The
only hope of maintaining the colonies as British
possessions is to settle people of our own race on
the land, and it would pay the British Government to
subsidise people to occupy the land. As it is there is no
likelihood of the people of the new colonies paying back
the £30,000,000 advanced by the British Government for
public works. Even if the Boer regime were reverted
to it would be better than the present rule of foreign
capitalists who desire to crush out the British element.
It was not unlikely that such people are supporting the
natives in the present rising.

“ Two Seas ” writes :
It makes the old stagers smile to read all the agonised
yelps in the Sydney Telegraph's “ Soil ” column about
the gradualness of the N.S.W. Lands Department. If a
selector doesn’t get his application dealt with in a month
or two the outcry is something tremendous, yet present
writer remembers plenty of instances where selections
were not even surveyed within ten months of being taken
up. But the rent was demanded, all the same, from date
of application, and the lessee, whose rent ceased on the
same date, calmly ran his sheep over the selection until
To-morrow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Serious affairs to-morrow.” — Greek proverb.
Come, let us give thanks for To-morrow, our
gospel, our faith, and our joss ;
’Tis on it we rely when we borrow, instead of
propelling our Cross;
What a manifold blessing we’ve found it —or has
it proved really a curse ?
It’s our creed, anyway—we’ll expound it with
yards of procrastinate verse ;
Let us pile I O U’s on its altar, let us paste the
P.N.’s on its shrine ;
Let us never forget it, nor falter from a creed
that is yours and is mine !
For we pile all our trust on To-morrow, and we
take the world easy and gay ;
And blow young Posterity’s sorrow, if we can be
jolly to-day !
Oft I muse who invented To-morrow-—the cutest
of statesmen was He,
Who, using that sponge-full of chloro-form, fooled
the electors B.C. !
Many faiths have appeared through the ages, to
wilt like spring blossoms away :
And the cult of To-morrow still rages—we load
all our fears in that dray !
A fig for Mahomet and Buddha —their gospels
are recent and cheap ;
Yea, To-morrow has served for a rudder to steer
us across the great deep !
So I call for three cheers for To-morrow —for the
creed that is hoary and grey ;
And it’s blow young Posterity’s sorrow, if we can
be cheerful to-day !
Aye, we’ll fix up Australia To-morrow ; we shall
arm both ourselves and our sons.
The trenches of war we will furrow, and line
them three deep with our guns.
But to-day we’ll play football or cricket, or will
send a remittance to “ Tatt’s ;
We’ll spend the glad hours at the wicket, and
welt useless globules with bats !
For there’s nothing so painful as thinking when
you’re not in the humor to think ;
While there’s nothing so pleasant as drinking
when you’re just in the humor to drink !
So I say, let us drink to To-morrow —let us have
a loud time while we may—
For it’s blow young Posterity’s sorrow if we can
wave corkscrews to-day !
VIC.

the selector fenced him off, which he might be permitted
to do in a year or two. Know more than one man who
was kept waiting fifteen months before his deposit
and survey fees were returned to him after an un-
successful application. All the peculiar methods of the
Lands Department, and a forecast of the result of the
recent Lands Commission of inquiry, may be found
recorded in the back files of The Bulletin, yet, like the
sailor who punched the Jew, the Telegraph’s corres-

writer belted the Lands Department for 16 years before
he recognised that he was bucking against an immovable
object. Apparently Dr. Arthur imagines that he is
the irresistible force that is going to shift it. I

position. It’s the “ understrappers ” who run the show.
They teach the new-chum Minister how the game is
played.
That playful old political elephant, John
Hurley, of N.S.W., has been dancing on a tub to
the great joy of the N.S. VV. country alderman,
whose god is mostly Proputty. John represents
one of the wealthy districts of N.S. Wales—a
district which, on account of its coal and iron,
might be vastly wealthier if it acquired the sense
to leave off sending the foreign-trader Joe Cook
to Parliament. ’Urley, it seems, has 14 parks in
his electorate ; but instead of the local property-
owner maintaining his own parks, he cadges from
the Treasury; so that the man at Tibooburra, who
hasn’t any park, is compelled to pay for keeping
the goats out of the place where ’Urley’s con-
stituents play cricket by day and wander with
Merely Mary Ann by night. This year he
couldn’t cadge more than £5O ; whereupon John
wrote and scattered broadcast a letter that was
intended to be wildly humorous, suggesting that
the park trustees should erect a tin tablet to
“ commemorate the Minister’s generosity.” It is
a depressing thing to think that that keen, delicate
rapier-thrust will most likely win John Hurley a
hundred votes. For so long as the Treasury can
be fleeced for the upkeep of country roads, cul-
verts, cricket-grounds, pumpkin shows, benevo-
lent societies, Marie Corelli clubs, billiard-
saloons, and so on, the man who can get the most
from the Treasury—or who can make the most
fuss when he doesn’t get what he wants from the
Treasury—will be “ the chap to save the country.”
Vast numbers of the people don’t care two straws
about politics—at least they don’t care fcr such
politics as are left to the State Parliaments —but
some of them are solid on dodging all the local
rates they can, and heaps more of them are inter-
ested up to the neck in the cricket club or the
pumpkin show. N.S. Wales was supposed to
have achieved something in the way of local
government lately. But the thing takes a long
while getting a start, and when it does start it
promises to be much too ineffective a thing to
scotch the professional cadger for special park and
culvert votes.

From the land which Seddon is putting in
pawn:—
Premier Seddon has ceased to cast round for fresh
conquests ; the glamor and the glory of the ring fizzled
out with his last blare on the Boer war. His energy is
now spent in mollifying his erstwhile enemies, and
making his peace with the Mammon of unrighteousness.
Instead of gathering to his counsels the sturdy Demo-
crats who stood loyally at his shoulder in his fighting
days, he is surrounded by milk-and-water Liberals, who
only joined him when their own party was pounded
out of shape. These new-found friends are not built on
fighting lines ; and, even if they were, the grinding of
discordant axes would bring them back to the starting-
point with nothing done.
Thus, while the so-called Farmers’ Union has been
kicked and cuffed from Auckland to Invercargill,
Seddon is more concerned about applying restoratives
than making use of his overwhelming victory. Against
an Opposition which is only a faint streak ; against a
leader of no ability ; against a handful of men who
haven’t the remotest glimmering of a policy, the
opportunity for lighting up the world presented itself.
M.L. was at least promised a very necessary increase in
the graduated land-tax and an equally necessary reform
in the protective tariff. But the land-grabber and the
one-eyed single-taxer croaked dismally, whereupon
Seddon hastened to say that the graduated tax will only
be imposed if he finds his finances are not as strong as
they ought to be. With this little proviso the proposal
is hung up for years, because Treasurer Seddon will never
know the pinch of poverty so long as a Cohen is left to
hang out the three-balls sign of Lombardy. And he does
not even feel equal to the task of a tariff revision. Yield-
ing to the croaking of the anti-tariff crowd, a few years
ago, a reduction of a penny per lb. was made on the duty
on candles, with this luminous result: —
Maoriland. Imported,
lb. lb.

1901 .. 2,614,741 2,258,177
1902 .. 2,792,351 2,268,914

There are many other branches of industry which are in
need of a stimulant, especially where the Arbitration
Court has ordered the payment of fair wages—notably
the woollen industry. Indeed, for want of more Protec-
tion, Arbitration itself is in great danger. But the voice
of the man with the cow has prevailed, and the importer
is given a new lease of his premises. Meanwhile the
borrowing craze continues ; so everyone’s stomach is full,
and everyone is warming his hands while the house burns.

Ever since ’Enry Wrixon wrote of the young
Australians’ alleged fondness for a black coat the
Argus has been harping on the theme. It broke
out angrily again the other day because business
men report that, whereas there was a time when
parents asked for jobs for their sons in the works,
they now solicit “places” for them in the
offices. Naturally, the Argus overlooks the
fact that this development is an inevit-
able effect of the condition it struggles its
darnedest to maintain. Anti-socialism is a battle
to uphold a system which makes the job in the
factory hateful, despicable, and barren of profits,
and the place in the counting-house highly
respectable and desirable, since it gives a lad the
chance of growing up to own workers instead of
working for owners. The Aegus and its follow-
ing rage heathenishly because boys shun the idea
of being mere grafters, and they rave more fear-
fully still against the reformers who are seeking
to make the carpenter’s billet as desirable as the
shopman’s from every point of view.
:
A doleful Londoner writes to one of the loca
weeklies :
For centuries the upper classes have had, more or less,
the handling of legislation, with a natural result, until
during recent years the organisation of the laboring class
has forced several measures for the latter’s benefit.
During this period it has more or less silently, but ever
surely, gained in power, and now a new force is sprung
upon us, and we are faced with a party whose efforts are
to be confined to obtaining measures for the amelioration
of its own class. The contest will doubtless be severe,
but every tooth drawn looks undoubtedly like coming out
of the heads of the middle classes. Year by year we see a
growth in national expenditure, and a succession of
bounds in municipal taxation, and to the latter there
seems to be no climax. On whom does this fall ? On the
middle classes mostly—and yet on all sides we hear of
nothing but pandering to the working-men.
In Freetrade Britain, with its vast army of clerks,
shopkeepers, and meanly-paid “commercial”
workers of the non-productive order, there is truth
in the contention that a Conservative middle class
stands to be financially squeezed between the new
Labor party and the old unyielding party of privi-
lege and capital. Increased expenditure for the
education of the poorest classes and the improve-
ment of their social conditions comes hardest on
the middle folk, who can least well afford it.
Therefore, the policy of the doleful Londoner
aforesaid and his grumbling brethren should be
to send their own representatives to Parliament
to work with the Labor party for the passing of
new land laws and other anti-Fatman measures.
There are in Britain two numerous classes, and
one smaller class, which hitherto has pulled all
the strings of Government with the approval of
one of the numerous classes, the Labor party hav-
ing no representation at all. Now that the Labor
party has arrived, the two great classes are bound
to combine against the all-powerful lords of the
and. The middle class must drop its con-
ervatism or bust.
♦
Bent, who at some times is a loud, hollow joke,
can at other times put a thing through about
twice as fast as most men. While Private
Enterprise has for three years been gradually
building the Essendon (Vic.) electric tramway,
and municipal councils have been squabbling for
almost as long over the Kew to St. Kilda,
Malvern to Prahran, and Esplanade lines, Thomas
has, per electrician Bradford, built and started
the St. Kilda to Brighton line in about a year.
And it has every appearance of paying from the
jump. The cars —to hold 40 passengers, at a penny
a section—are stoutly-built, neatly finished, well-
lighted, and easy running. They travel from 10 to
15 miles an hour, with more certainty and less noise
than any other vehicle round Melbourne. The
power-house is a severely-utilitarian-looking struc-
ture of galvanised-iron, painted the same ginger-
pink color as Tait’s engines. But so far the track
is only a single one, with half-a-dozen side tracks
in the three miles and a-half; and that isn’t cal-
culated to give the best results. Nevertheless,
even as it is, this bit of line will set going a
movement to electrify the whole of the Melbourne
suburban railways, giving a speedy and easily-
handled and unwasteful means of locomotion ;
and the sooner the better.
The memory, of the Bush veldt
Carbineers tragedy is revived in
Sydney by a visit from Mr. Easton,
of S’Africa, who was behind the
scenes from the first in the crime by
which the British Government
made a group of Australians the
scapegoats for the sins of a
whole army. Mr. Easton reports
that the Canadian who was pun-
ished with Morant, Handcock,
Witton, and the others, has been
amply compensated by the British
Government for the miscarriage of
justice ; as also has been an Ameri-
can citizen who was concerned.
Only the Australians get no com-
pensation. In Morant’s case there
is, of course, no remedy. But
Handcock left a widow and child-
ren who call for consideration.
Witton has suffered many months
imprisonment. Lenehan (who had
absolutely nothing to do with the
tragedies and wasn’t even in their
vicinity) was subjected to grave in-
dignity, imprisonment and trans-
portation. These people have
lacked redress simply because the
Australian Government has been
so careless of its responsibilities
to its citizens that it has not cared
to ask for compensation for what
are now admitted to have been
cruel wrongs. Apropos, they tell in
S’Africa a yarn of how Witton’s re-
lease finally came about. A petition
had gone to the King, but it was intercepted and
never reached him. Finally a S’African in the
bacon-and-butter business, who knew tea-man
Lipton, put the facts before him, and he put them
before President Roosevelt. Then the help of a
very charming society woman was enlisted. She
told the King, and the thing was done. It is a
circuitous track that leads to the fount of mercy
in England, and even the road to the fount of
justice isn’t quite straight.

All farce is divided into three parts, of
which the first is the husband going to a doubtful
supper-house, and his wife going also to observe
him, and the police raiding the place ; the second,
the weird lies which everybody tells everybody
else because to tell the truth in a farce would be
to spoil the fun; and the third, the lame ex-
planation which arrives at curtain-fall time.
“The Man from Mexico,” now running at
Sydney Criterion, breaks away from none of
these essential traditions. It differs from others
only in the fact that the leading prevaricator
actually gets to gaol, instead of escaping it by a
whisker’s breath, and thus it provides an original
Erison scene which is highly diverting—gaol being a
umorous sort of place viewed from the outside.
Something more is done in the way of giving an
air of freshness to the proceedings by providing
diverting songs and dances. For the rest it’s the
same old farce of “The Magistrate,” “What
Happened to Jones,” and “Are You a Mason ? ”
order. Fitzhugh has been out all night and comes
home with the milkman, a policeman, and a
sentence of 30 days in the cells. Mrs. Fitzhugh
has also been out all night. She had, from the
best of motives, gone to the same hall of riot as
Fitzhugh, and was arrested, spent the night in
gaol, and isn’t quite sure that there is not
some more gaol ahead. Fitzhugh, who has been
sentenced Under a false name, is allowed a few
hours grace to settle his affairs. He announces at
home that he goes on a trip to Mexico, and starts
for Jug. In the second act he is in J ug. In the
third act he is in a morass of perjuries, and finally
comes on to safe ground with the statement that
he really has been in Jug, and not in Mexico—in
Jug, doing time to save his wife from disgrace.
Curtain.
» * * *
But with such entertainment as “The
Man from Mexico,” it is not the play, but the
players, that matters. And here the production
scores, for the cast is as strong as any
that has attempted this class of work in Aus-
tralia. Miss Grace Palotta, very much improved
since her last visit, in singing and dancing, with
all her old grace of face and figure and piquant
charm of manner, is the ideal lead for a merry,
extravagant comedy. It is hard to define in a
word the Palotta manner-—something more than
“ chic,” something less than “ dashing.” It con-
sists of high spirits, expressed with absolute grace,
and nowhere marred with even a, trace of
clumsiness. In the last act a Mexican dance
with Hugh Ward, full of : vivacious coquetry
MISS MARGARET THOMAS, the charming
singer who marries Mr. Gordon, K.C., of
Sydney.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney.
and languorous surrender, gives the lady a
chance to show how much her dancing has im-
proved lately. On Hugh Ward falls the bur-
den of playing The Man from Mexico,”
the harassed unfortunate struggling against a sea
of lies and a mountainous headache in the first
act; doing prison steps on a bread-and water diet
in the second ; and winding up as a pseudo-
Mexican in the third, with accounts of boating
trips on the Amazon, and of a great bull fight
(in which his bull ran second). It’s a huge part,
and it was inevitable that on the first night there
should be some little lapses—mostly in the direc-
tion of over-emphasis. But, withal, Ward always
keeps the house merry, and his picture of the
woebegone convict, with a dismal song, “ Nobody
Cares,” and a highly original dance, are the back-
bone of the piece.
* * * *
George Willoughby, as the kind chap
who is always “fixing things up” for his friends,
and leaving them deeper in the mud than before,
has an excellent part. Willoughby has modified
that rather “ bustling ” manner in which he per-
vaded the “Smith” and “Jones” farce, to a crisp
dry humor, which is very entertaining. He would
improve things if he cut out some verses of a
topical song dealing with Williamson, Mus-
grove, Rickards, Flemming, Miss Brune,
Little drops of Peppermint
Made by Mr. Wood,
Don’t they do a body
Such a world of good.
Little drops of Peppermint Cure
Make you well again,
Drive away that hacking cough,
And every ache and pain,*
Miss Ross, and other theatrical personages.
Great assistance is given to the principals
by Reginald Wykeham as a broad farcical
detective, Arthur Cornell, E. Bonfield, C.
W. Gamble, and J. W. Hartman. Practically
all the members of the co. have been imported ;
and some of the minor ones do not warrant the
expense. But that does not interfere with the
success of a comedy which is as hilarious as any
that Sydney has seen.
* -# * *
“ The Prodigal Son ” continues to be pro-
digal in scenic effects, wickedness, remorse, and
the casino variety of gaiety at Sydney Royal. The
play is popular with right-thinking people, who
see in it a great moral lesson. But other-thinking
people seem to come to the conclusion that the
Prodigal Son, who has many years of gaiety and
only a few years of highly affluent remorse as a
popular poet, is a luckier dog in every way than
the Elder Brother who stays at home, and puts
the Icelandic cat out at nights, and has no
girl and no gaiety, and struggles all the years
of his life with the mortgage. The Bulletin is
an other-thinking person, and comes away from
the Royal determined to be a Prodigal Son if the
chance comes along. If it is too old for that it
would rather be a Prodigal Grandfather than not
Prod, at all.

Most persons show their teeth when they
are pleased. They do it for the same reason that a
dog wags his tail. Therefore, there was as great a
tooth-show in the audience watching the revival of
“Florodora” as there was a flower show behind the
footlights. The bloomy spray filled the stage of Her
Majesty’s on Saturday night, and if the audi-
ence had had tails you couldn’t have heard
the songs of the performers for the sound of
enraptured caudal appendages beating the floor.
Miss Florence Young was out of the cast for
resurrection night, but resumed her place as
Dolores on Monday, and the piece promises well.
Haigh Jackson’s “O, My Dolores!” was en-
cored as usual. It has indeed a place in memory
dearest, and has enough seriousness and pathos
to make it appear like really-truly love-making.
Miss Nellie Wilson, who has a sweet soprano of
waffle lightness, took Miss Young’s place as
Dolores the first night, and Miss Clara Clifton
told in basement tones the value of Tact, tact, tact.
Mr. Fred. Leslie, as Cyrus Gilfain, still flouts
the ivory moon with his high kicking, and for
interpolation a burlesque on “The Fatal Wed-
ding” is dragged in. It isn’t dragged in
shrieking, though. The audience does that. Next
Saturday “ A Country Girl ” will be revived.
* * *
Stephenson’s English Musical Company is
keeping up its reputation as a mirthmonger (that’s
as good a word as ironmonger, and much newer).
Anyone wandering into Sydney Palace these
nights is sure to see a stout, superior old
gentleman dressed like an advertisement for a
high-class tailoring firm fall ignominiously over a
bucket and scrubbing brush, or a thin, superior
old spinster kissed by one of the lower orders.
The stumble and kiss are, of course, set to
music. During the week Bill Adams blew
his own trumpet and lighted up the stage
with his ruby nose, and on Saturday last “The
Dandy Doctor,” described as “a musical cocktail
in three swallows,” was staged. It is a humorous
dose, and gives no cause for a wry face. A
girl masquerades as her own servant to
avoid marrying her father’s nominee, and the
maid assumes her mistress’s status. A lay person
takes the place of a consultant physician and pre-
scribes —quite as harmlessly as most doctors—for
patients. Miss May Beatty, who is the servant,
and, for a while, the mistress, is a Tweeny,
who, for comical squalidness, throws the original
Belinda of “Our Boys,” and Dickens’s “Mar-
chioness” into the high relief of dressmakers’
fashion-plates. The music is skittish as young
goats and always tuneful (some tunes, even in grand
opera, are not tuneful—to some people), and
the dialogue is much smarter than one hears
in trams and market-places. In fact, it is smarter
than one hears at swell dinners. The Stephenson
season concludes to-night.
Frank T. Bullen opens his Sydney season
on Monday night in the Lyceum Hall. Bullen
has been everything on the sea except a pirate ;
and he would have been a pirate only that he has a
sense of humor and religious convictions. On
land he is a bright writer and talker, with much
to tell; and in Melbourne he has shown that the
vogue of the lecturer is not dead.
** * *
The Tivoli still stands four-square among
the nicest pawnshops of Castlereagh-street, Syd-
ney, where the Very Best People find a long-lost
uncle up the side passage. Nothing else in par-
ticular has happened there since last week, except
that H. Rickards, Commander-in-Chief of all the
forces, sang a song for one night only by way of
announcing that he was busy packing his carpet-
bag before setting out to rummage the world again
for new artists. Amongst other attractions he
might do worse than get hold of a clerical gent
who has some new ideas concerning the whale
that caused Jonah to disappear some time back,
his own theory being that the whale wore petti-
coats and a picture-hat. If he can’t get him, he
will probably get several girls who will sing “ Lu-
lu [pit-a-pat, pat-a-pit] I love you [pit-a-pit, pat-a-
pit], My own Zulu [pit-a-pat], Indeed I do [pat
pat] ” —and that will be worse. Meantime the
show, even without any newcomers, is worth the
money.

the Wild Australian entertainment aforesaid. At
the solemn hour of midnight on Thursday, the
man with the long back hair entered the watch-
house, his hands handcuffed behind his back, and
a constable bearing a loaded revolver beside him—
ride next morning’s papers. On Friday the police
brought him, in all his showman’s glory, to
Melbourne City Court, where Pan ton, P.M.,
was asked to remand the accused on the
petty charge of stealing a saddle. Among
the fourteen other alleged charges was one
of escaping from custody after he had been
arrested for horse stealing, but for some unstated
reason the police confined their indictment to the
saddle point on which turned the question of bail
for the prisoner during his term of remand. Here
the Wild Australian magistracy of Panton came
bounding into notice. In consideration of the
fact that “Kemp” was before the court as a
simple larcenist and nothing more—the inciden-
tal allegations being non-official—Panton fixed
the bail at two sureties of £5OO each, and the
magnitude of the bail was a further advt. for
the Buckjumping show. The management an-
nounced that the sureties had been duly found,
and Dare-Devil Kemp would positively appear on
Saturday night. At 8 p.m. just as much of Mel-
bourne’s populace as could be squeezed into Fitz-
gerald’s building was waiting to see the Dare-
Devil and co. act up to the terms of the advt.
■K ~K * *
It was half-past 8 before the small party
of crude entertainers got fairly going within the
limited circus space, which had been railed off as
a “ stock-yard.’’ For the next two hours nothing
happened to warrant the excited rushing to and
fro of Professor Kemp. There was a little buck-
jumping and a great deal of tedious preparation
for it. Small boys were called forth to ride
youthful bulls and ponies. A man with a cord
made several poor attempts at lassoo-throwing.
A formidable bull that might have warmed things
up for his tormentors if they had been looking
for trouble, was not given fair play for his feel-
ings. The dullness of the performance in
general caused an expert stockwhip-cracker
to be hailed as a boon, for he at least could do
something excellently well. But the joy he im-
parted wasn’t worth more than a small percentage
of the money charged for admission. Miss Kemp
sat a genuine buckjumper with better success than
the men who had fallen off, but Kemp the Dare-
Devil, Kemp the hero of the two £5OO sureties,
Kemp whom everybody had come to see—Kemp
had shown no horsemanship up to 10.20 p.m. So
the present writer went sorrowfully away, think-
ing of Wild Australian Kernp as a man who ought
to settle down and get his hair cut.
** * *
“ Anti-Bunkum ” writes of the Melbourne
buckjumping business -
Prof. Kemp and a persistent person in a frock coat
said a great deal about things that didn’t happen, and
considerable time was occupied by taking down and
replacing slip-rails, and the saddling of several alleged
buckers which raised very little dust, though one or two
Wild Willies and Dirty Dicks managed to slide off grace-
fully. Miss Kemp, a young person in highly decorative
costume, proved her mettle on a tall, lean brute that
leaped and doubled up like a jack-knife without shifting
his light burden. Prom time to time the “Professor”
yelled vague challenges and offers of “ hundreds,” and in
return the crowd yelled for the much-talked-about Kemp
display. The star item eventually turned up in a restless
grey nag that pawed and side-stepped, the “boys”
having great difficulty in placing the necessary gear
on. At last a bag was ingeniously slid over the
animal’s head by means of three cross ropes held taut
forming a slide over which the bag was pulled, and after
many attempts “ Snowy ” Morgan, who previously rode a
wild steer, mounted and stayed some few moments, only
to assume a restful position. Kemp then began a dis-
course on his intentions during the season, but was
promptly requested to “get work” and show what he
could do. The buckjumping or irritation of animals show
concluded by Kemp, who stands 63 inches, being tied to a
light filly, the animal then careering several laps in a
series of waving motions. The brightest part of a mixed
entertainment was a display of whip-cracking by Dan
Hassett. The weakest item was a Strong Man, whose
feats of bluff and muscle can be equalled by many local
amateurs.
** * *
At the Princess’s the Brough-Flemming
Co. will appear on Saturday (19th), opening, as
afore-stated, with “Dr. Wake’s Patient.” Mel-
bourne is talking about the matter in an interested
tone of voice. Miss Beatrice Day and Herbert
Flemming are looked forward to, and the re-
appearance of Mrs. Brough will give a very
particular power of attraction to the comedy that
brings her back to her old friends.
* -X- *
Miss Tittell Brune’s long stay at Her
Majesty’s (Melbourne) is to be ended next
Wednesday, and then the theatre will go into
recess for two nights whilst the Collier Co. is
making ready to appear. “Dorothy Vernon”
passes out of the bill to-morrow (18th) in order
that “Sunday ” may fall on Saturday and carry
the season to a close. The management states
that the name part in “ Sunday ” is Miss Brune’s
“ most famous impersonation.” A number of
people will think differently, but the management
ought to know. It has the exact cash measure-
ments of Miss B.’s various fames.
* * *
At Melbourne Town Hall, the. pictures of
mighty throbbing London are still throbbing,
except on nights when the Lord Mayor wants the
space for a throb of his own. But Frank Bullen,
who moved from the Athenaeum to the Masonic
Hall for his last two appearances, has gone touring
in the direction of Sydney. Bullen was a great
comfort to his high priest, Smythe, during the
Melbourne season. He repeated each of his four
lectures at the instance of General Desire, and
the General’s army crowded the hall every time.
** * *
“ For the Sake of a Woman,” the latest
Andersonian effort at Melbourne Royal, re-asserts
the essential wickedness of “Stephen,” in melo-
drama nomenclature. The evangelical names
Matthew, Mark and Luke—especially Luke—are
associated with evil-doing, as a rule, but for
downright villainy Stephen is rarely equalled
and never surpassed. Willard naturally is the
Stephen of the piece. He. seems to have a taste
for electrical experiments, and when he gets Miss
Duggan into his laboratory he presses sundry
buttons with murderous intentions, and the
results are amazingly droll. The lady is rescued
from Stephen’s clutches by her husband (George
Cross),: a banker, who has been misunderstanding
her for some time. It isn’t a very reasonable
misunderstanding, but it serves to promote
unhappiness and provide cause for a recon-
ciliation at the finish, what time Stephen
strikes a tragic attitude in the custody of the
police. The people of this drama are more
addicted to wealth and respectability than is
usual on the Andersonian stage. In addition to
the banker and the villain who toys with science,
there is a white-wigged vicar, likewise his son
who has been elegantly brought up, and an adven-
turess described as “Lady Clare.” Moreover,
Bert Bailey, on the present occasion, is a gilded
Trevor, a young lord of the manor, distinguished
among his fellows by a family nasal drawl, which
probably came over with the Conqueror. The
various members of the company fill their exalted
stations very creditably considering that they have
only lately escaped from “ The Midnight Mail,”
where they were cast in humble circumstances,
and Miss Duggan couldn’t raise the means to buy
food for the baby. On Saturday she was recon-
ciled to her banker in an expensive pink hat; also
Miss Guildford was becomingly attired, and Miss
Mabel Lane, as Lady Clare, presented a stylish
appearance when she came to a violent end.
** * *
All the complimentary things said by The
Bulletin re West’s Pictures and the Brescians
during their Sydney season will be equally true of
the same show now that it is quartered at Mel-
bourne Athenasum Hall. The pictures screened
on Saturday were almost perfect specimens of
biograph work, and the eye of an admiring
audience followed them from first to last with-
out feeling worried. They rarely wobbled, and
they flickered not at all, and they were peaceful
subjects for contemplation as a rule—pictures
that came and went without ear-racking noises.
Sandwiched between two periods of biograph the
musical entertainment by the Brescians gave
more than middling satisfaction. In their sing-
ing and instrumental performances the lady
violinist and the contralto make good individual
scores, and the general effect of the little concert
party is in harmony with the pictorial ends of the
show, which has evidently come to Melbourne to
stay.

The Three Lees (who are singing acrobats),
also Brightie Smith and Baby Watson, have joined
the Rickards forces at the Opera House. Other-
wise the bill is about the same as last week, so it
appears that the new arrivals are extras rather
than replacements.
** * *
Adelaide has seen nothing of the ‘ ‘ draw-
ing-room entertainment ” type for years (Mel. B.
Spurr died just when the bills were up to
announce his coming), and Leslie Harris, with the
Yeamans-Titus pair, has put in a merry and
profitable Cup Week at the Royal. The lady has
some very good imitations of opera-singers and
others, but the public insists on and gets
her baby prattle as chief item. Harris
is a fine musician who has got off
the rail; but then musicians are plentiful
and story-tellers with his sense of humor are rare.
“Tambour Major” is just coming in. Frank
Bradley, one of Australia’s chief amateur actors,
has been “farewelled” with a good all-round per-
formance of “ Lady Windermere’s Fan.” He has
a speaking-voice which is a distinct asset, and can
make love in a convincing manner; but London
is a large place to get a start in.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ 0.K.” : A while ago an Antarctic Expeditioner told us
that the popular notion of an iceberg was quite wrong,
the bergs being flat-topped and sadly inartistic Also he
showed some photos, or sketches, of bergs that looked as
smooth as a bowling green on the summit. But Frank
Bullenshows a picture of an iceberg almost as rugged and
peaky as the most popular-notioned of them. Seemingly
they float in all shapes. The Antarctic gentleman had
only the little knowledge which is a dangerous thing.
The sprightly Dartos have fallen on somewhat evil
times. Monsieur sprained his ankle about two years ago,
and has since had to desist from tossing his better half
up in the air in “Zee Pol —ka.” An Australian, writing
from Nice, where the Dartos are resting, says the little
madame’s bodices consist of even less waist-band and
shoulder-strap than heretofore.
“O’Fipp”: Nobody who read the elegant extracts in
Melbourne Her Majesty’s advertisements could complain
of being lured to “Dorothy Vernon” under false pre-
tences. For instance: —
Dorothy : “Oh ! I have so longed for you, John.
My John ! My Jack ! My Johnnie ! ”
Manners : “My Dorothy ! My Doll! My Dolly ! ”
When one comes to such phrases in the sort of novel on
which “ costume ” plays are founded, it is safe to bet that
the continuation will be in this tone: “And as she
trembled to his manly embrace, the ‘ glug-glug ’ of the
nightingale was heard from among the dear old cedars,
while overhead the first paly gleam of Astarte’s crescent,
not yet bediamonded, seemed to cast a benediction
upon the re-united lovers Finis.”
“Edyson”: Re “ Dorothy Vernon.” I don’t recollect
a more brazen or uncalled-for bit of twopenny colored
melodrama in a play with pretensions than the conclusion
of act I. This act has been presented in a light comedy
vein, but Dorothy’s low comedy papa (a fond, if some-
what thunderous parent) wants to kill her lover. The
lover has escaped, and Dorothy holds the key of the back
gate. “ Give it up, you hussy, or I’ll have you whipped ! ”
cries the angry father. Dolly throws the key over the
gate, strips her frock from her shoulders before the whip-
armed myrmidon, and cries : “ Now for the lash !” Cur-
tain. It is wholly factitious and fatuous, for there is not
the remotest possibility of the old parent allowing his fair
daughter to be flogged in his own backyard by the
common butcher, but the situation is stormiiy applauded.
The Turkish method of advance across the
boundary of peace is not the kind of progress
achieved by theassistance of D.C.L. whisky, which
pushes forward the advanced posts of industrial
achievement; not those of gun and bayonet.
D.C.L. puts heart into many an enterprise and
enterprise into many a heart. A nip of D.C.L.,
dispensed by a bright Hebe, is the finest after-
luncheon tonic in the whole business world.*

perience of millions of people in all quarters of the
habitable globe, during the last quarter of a
century, has confirmed the value of Hunyadi Janos
as the best and safest natural aperient. Invalu-

Actor Gerrard has been having more Australian experi-
ence than he bargained for. While rusticating at
McLeod’s station (N.S.W.), a bull came on the scene,
upsetting all Douglas’s meditations and Douglas himself,
with the consequence that the curly-headed one is now
the possessor of three broken ribs.
“ Carson ” : I feel that I can die happy now that I have
seen “ Professor ” Kemp, the alleged buck-jumping man,
face Magistrate Panton’s Court on a charge of larceny of a
saddle. With his long ringlets streaming all over Mel-
bourne, his wide felt hat, heavy black cloak (the kind the
brigand wears in grand opera when he is an armed bari-
tone in a cave), riding pants, tall boots and spurs, he
looked something between a Mexican cow-youth
and a Spanish hidalgo. He also resembled a South
American insurrection, and he reminded me a good
deal of San Martin’s army of liberation crossing the Andes,
combined with a little of Garibaldi and something of Ajax
facing the lightning. When he threw the long picturesque
cloak round him and clanked his spurs he recalled
memories of Columbus finding America, or Cortez annex-
ing the goods and chattels of Montezuma, and when he
left on bail he recalled the back view of Boabdil leaving
Granada. He was a great spectacle, and should have
carried a banner. Yet it was all over the alleged stealing
of some leather object or other.

Cabled that Miss Olga Brandon, once a well-known
actress, and a Sydney native ot American descent, has
died in extreme poverty. She was a very beautiful
woman and appeared with Willie Edouin’s company
at London Royalty in 1887. Why and how she died
in poverty is not explained.
A little play by J. M. Barrie was to have been produced
at the Comedy Theatre, London, on sth April last. It is
entitled “ Punch,” and deals with the domestic life of
that long-nosed homicidal hero. There are only four
characters, and in the cast were included the names
of two actors well-known to Australians—Dion Boucicault
and George Anson.
Bullen’s lectures remind one that there is a great
opening for a deep-sea diver who could spin picturesque
yarns of his experiences with ships’ cargoes and corpses
in Davy Jones’ locker. The divers thus far have been
dull, unimaginative fellows, looking at wrecks in a
salvage-business way, and saying nothing about the
things they might have done but didn’t. Henry Lucy
tells a story that leaked out concerning some divers who
found a large stock of dead bodies packed closely between
decks, but kept the discovery dark because there was no
money to be made by bringing up such goods.
Theodore Kremer, author of “The Fatal Wedding,”
learned the art of play-writing from Dan Barry, with
whom he once toured in Victoria. That accounts for
many things.
“S.T.S.”: The only new thing I learned from seeing
“ Everyman ” was a pretty periphrasis. Said a critic of
the priests : “ Their children, it is said, sit by other men’s
fires.” All decorous editors ought to insist on the use of
this delicate phrase when appropriate: “ Petitioner
then gave evidence that a child of co-respondent was sit-
ting” (or “might be expected to sit”) “by his (peti-
tioner’s) fire.’
“ T.F.M.” : The outer husk of George Coppin’s old
Queen’s Theatre is still standing-, and as it was built by
John Thomas Smith in 1844, it must now be one of the
oldest surviving landmarks of early Melbourne. It is
notable that the three men most closely associated with
the early fortunes of that “ magnificent temple of the
drama ” (so it was described in the newspapers of the
period) all played a part on the political stage in later
days, viz., J. T. Smith, Coppin, and Morton King, The
latter was a heavy tragedian, who became lessee of the
Queen’s when Coppin departed for Adelaide, after making
his first “ positively final appearance on any stage ”; and
under his baptismal appellation—Mark Last King—he sat
in the Victorian Parliament in the sixties, where he was
more of a light comedian than a melancholy Dane. He
was of small account as a law-maker.
Mary Conly, the Melbourne contralto, whose voice had
somewhat more volume but less “color ” than that of her
contemporary, Amy Castles, is to sing at the Hereford
Musical Festival, and the reports on the subject will be
interesting to her former barrackers. Miss Conly, who is
well within the “ twenties,” may possibly have a fine
future as an oratorio singer. At any rate, she has
advanced W'ithouf being-pushed, what time half-a-dozen
other Victorian girls were discovering that Europe had no
use for their crude little warbles.
ENTICEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VIO.
If I were a chorus girl,
How I would adorn the stage
In the dance’s dreamy whirl !
Robed in mist and wreathed in ptarl
I would surely be the rage
If I were a chorus girl.
I would fire the dullest churl,
I would charm the sourest sage,
In the dance’s dreamy whirl.
Like a floweret I’d unfurl
Like a red flag I’d enrage !
If I were a chorus girl.
Like a foam bell I would swirl
Dazzling courtier, prince, and page
In the dance’s dreamy whirl.
Locks and limbs and robes I’d curl
Round the heart strings of the age.
If I were a chorus girl
In the dance’s dreamy whirl 1
DOROTHY FRANCIS McCRAE.

sent by post or handed in; and no MS. ivill be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

will go unchecked M‘Nab : You base your theory on
the idea that “the export of wheat is practically nil,”
and that wheat is carried at a loss on the railways so
that the man in the city can have cheap bread. But the
export of wheat in 1904 (no later statistics available) was
over 33,000,000 bushels —more than half the crop. It
had to be exported because the manufacturing popula-
tion which eats it is in England—not here as it should be.
And seeing that we are the most remote of the wheat
suppliers, it costs nearly lid. per bushel in charges from
Sydney or Melbourne to London, so we have to subsidise
the farmer with cheap railway rates, so that he can com-
pete in England. If the manufacturing population was
here, and the lOd. or lid. per bushel was saved, the
farmer could afford to pay fair railway rates

D. Jelley : (1) There is no guarantee, yet, of that Fiscal
Beferendum. (2) If it comes, there is no guarantee that
it will be made an honest question between Protection
and Freetrade. By Protection we mean high Protection
—the kind that protects. By Freetrade we mean real
Freetrade—not the old NiS.W. Freetrade device of
heavily protecting wine, beer, tobacco, sugar, and two or
tnree dozen other articles, and subsidising Freetrade
newspapers with gratis postage, and paying huge bonuses
to the agricultural industry with railway rebates, and
then calling the results of this Protection a proof of the
blessings of Freetrade. If there is to be a referendum
between real Freetrade and real Protection The Bulletin
is willing 1000 times over, but there won’t be if the
N.S. W. Freetrade Laborists can help it. It will probably

“ Why didn't you call the baby
after me?"
“ We went as near it as we could, Uncle James.
We called it Jemima."
Some day, when there is a strong N.S.W.
Government (not one like the present, which puts
in all its time jumping away from its own shadow),
a drastic cleaning up will be applied to the Dis-
trict Court Bench. Almost without exception
(though Judge Murray may be mentioned as an
honorable example on the other side) the District
Court Judges are, when on the Bench, tetchy,
ill-tempered, and disregardful of their duties to
the public. Suitors and their counsel generally
get consideration in the Supreme Court and cour-
tesy in the High Court, but in the humble Dis-
trict Court they often have to put up with the
impatient discourtesy of a judge who is seemingly
soured, tired, and anxious to cut his work as short
as possible. An extraordinary example of Dis-
trict Court impatience was provided by Judge
Backhouse last week. Mr. Windeyer appeared
in a certain case, which was put third on the list
for hearing. The two cases at the head of the list
happened to fall through, and when at 10.15 Mr.
Windeyer’s case was called he was not there (and
did not appear until five minutes later). Mean-
while Judge Backhouse non-suited the plaintiff,
“as counsel was not in court.” No evidence was
heard, no law points taken. Since counsel was
absent (justifiably absent, under the circum-
stances), plaintiff was condemned to lose her case
and pay all costs. That cannot, in any sensible
community, be good law. Mr. Windeyer,
on his own behalf, intends to protest to the
Bar Council, and there should be some pro-
test to Parliament on behalf of the client.
Judge Backhouse’s methods as a Judge have been
often under question before. He is the gentle-
man who, as an acting Supreme Court Judge,
ensured the conviction of the Broken Hill strike
leaders by stating to the jury the possibility of
only a light punishment being inflicted. He is
also the Judge who applied the First Offenders
Act to Solicitor llofe, of the Butler divorce con-
spiracy.

Now that Jap sailors are swarming over
Melbourne like one of the seven plagues, the soft-
goods person eagerly displays combined Jap-
English legends remarking, “ Banzai for Our
Fourpenny Socks,” or “Smithers and Phipkins
Welcome the Gallant Little Brown Man to their
Great Sale of Braces and U. Wear.” Old
King Cole rejoiceth in the presence and in-
cidental pence of his beloved brown brethren, and
the grins of Cole’s smellful caged monkeys are re-
flected on the other side of the bars. On the day
of arrival one of the Japs walked out of a Swan-
ston-street shop calmly carrying a lump of bacon.
He was pursued by an indignant shopman. It
turned out that the brown man knew enough
English to read the ticket attached to the smoked
pig—“ 6d. per lb., take the piece ” —and he accord-
ingly took the piece, under the impression that
that particular mighty atom was a free sample.
* * * *
The Anglican Provincial Synod (Vic.) has
decided that all ministers of parishes within its
sphere of influence shall be known as rectors
rather than vicars or incumbents. The sinful
outside world had previously surmised that
whereas a rector was always a vicar, he amounted
to something more considerable than an incum-
bent, who sounds so beastly like an encumbrance ;
but it now appears that they are of equal rank,
like an alligator and a crocodile.
* * * *
There is no more homeless position in all
Sydney than that of the woman who has acci-
dentally lost her last train about 10.30 p.m., and
is forced to seek shelter in a hotel
for the night. Unless she has luggage,
she may strike twenty hotels in succes-
sion, and even her money and her
apparent respectability won’t get her a
room. The supercilious person in charge
says that the premises are full. The
spider may be spinning the cobweb of
utter loneliness across the office, and
among all the 40 bedrooms there may be
no sign of habitation except a pair of
muddy boots outside the door of No.
35|A, but the supercilious person says
the house is full just the same. The
tenth-rate beer-house, which assumes
cheerfully that the applicant isn’t
“respectable,” may take her in with a
leer; the high-class hotel won’t. Of
course, if she knew enough to go to the
nearest police-station and explain
things to the sergeant, she would get in
if the hotel register showed that there
was room—she w'ould either get in or a
four-storey hostelry worth £05,236 3s.
9gd. would get into great trouble. But
as a rule she doesn’t know. A recent
case that The Bulletin heard of was
that of a young literary woman who
lives in chambers. Her trouble was
simple, and looked humorous at first.
Her door slammed when she was out
on the landing. It was a spring lock,
and the key was inside, and at that
hour (about 10.30 p.m.) no locksmith
could be procured to force it open.
She had money about her, and
looked as respectable as any girl
could look, but the reception she re-
ceived at the first big caravanserai
to which she applied made her leave
off applying, and spend the night
on the cold stairs. If she had
known what to do she would have
brought a policeman, and he
would have made a hostelry
worth something like £98,122
19s. 2d, shudder for its
license ; but not knowing,
she camped in a cold draught
on the staircase.
Victoria is lining its
prisoners up for lectures, too.
The talks are monthly, on
literary and scientific subjects.
The audience isaddressed as “Gentle-
men,” thanked for its attendance, and
asked to pass a vote expressive of its gratitude to
the chairman.

The Rootes shooting case at Manly
(N.S.W.), in which the affectionate husband (who
was out of work) called on his wife (temporarily
reduced to earning her own living in a hotel), and
fatally shot her and himself, played manifest
havoc with a lot of people’s common sense. The
man, mortally wounded by a bullet through the
heart, fell on the floor, and was expeditiously
pinioned by a constable. He was immediately
released, however, and promptly died. One
account makes it appear that after he died he
“ was past human aid.” Some spectators say he
fell “in the throes of death,” but others maintain
that he slipped “on some bottles.” One man, a
popular entertainer, seems to have been the only
person who judiciously adjusted the limelight, and
he stands forth in the wild newspaper account,
with this par, all to himself :
A man named George King, who is engaged in popular
entertainments on the ocean beach, has stated to the
police that when he heard the shots he rushed into the
bar, picked up a flower-pot, and threw it at Rootes, who
was in the middle of the floor, and did not then appear as
if he was injured.
And even this tale is incomplete, for it doesn’t
clearly appear whether the flower-pot killed the
dead corpse or not.
N.Z. Times (M.L.) publishes this in its
correspondence columns:—
Girls and Fresh Air.
(To the Editor.)
Sir, —I have noted with pleasure the impartial manner
in which you publish from time to time correspondence
relating to various matters of public interest, and I trust
that you may see your way clear to give me space in
which to refer to a matter which I consider to be of vital
importance to Maoriland as a whole and to the youth of
the colony in particular. How do the mothers of young
girlS expect them to have good health when they are con-
tinually watched at night, and no chance is given them
to enjoy the fresh air ? The pernicious system of spying,
as indulged in by a number of parents, is also affecting
the nervous systems of a number of young girls, and it
does very little good in the long run.
The writer signs himself “ One of the Waiters” —
presumably one of the young men who waited at
the front gate for the girl to come out and get the
benefit of the fresh air. And, presumably, owing
to the pernicious spying system, the girl didn’t
come out.
* * * *
A nice point—which probably ought to be
discussed under “Business, Robbery, &c.”—has
been dragged out of the sea in S. Australia. The
question is : When you catch a fish (even if you
only catch it at Paulos Friedprawnolos’s) who
owns the innards ? It was this way: A boy fell
off a pier at the Adelaide Outer Harbor works,
and was partially recovered in instalments. Later
on, a shark was caught; and it was found that
the beast had made a recent meal off a suit of
boy’s clothes —alleged to be the Outer Harbor
boy’s clothes. Meantime, the shark-catcher began
to turn a penny by exhibiting the shark and con-
tents. The authorities (whoever they may be)
then tried to get the clothes so that the father
could identify them ; but the shark-catcher replied
that anybody, even the father, who wanted to see
the clothes must come and pay admission. What
would have happened in case of identification has
not transpired, though perhaps they thought of
arresting the shark for theft.
A POINT OF ETIQUETTE.
MISTRESS: “ Disgusting! Somebody has been
striking matches on this beautiful thing.”
MARTY “ Yes'm, it’s Master. ’E always docs it
when ’e comes ’ome.”
MISTRESS: “ It’s not a question of when but
where he strikes them on it .”
“ Snell ” : When will the literary girl with
humor and a seeing eye come to us from the ranks
of the “ generals,” and tell us all about the ser-
vant’s life and the lives of the families with whom
she has lived ? The reflection is called up by
an outburst overheard in a Melbourne railway
carriage the other evening. “Her husband's a
clergyman at Church,” said the flushed hire-
ling. “He gets big wages, and they’ve only got
a small family, and yet they’re that mean that me
and Sarah —the other girl—often sit hungry in
church, hearing the Rev. Bob preaching away like
old boots about lovin' charity and kindness to your
feller men. Look here, the missus will lock up
two strips of cold plum puddin’, and not good
plum puddin’ at that, for fear we should
eat it, and I’ve been in the house a
month and been hungry all the time. They’ve
had fifteen girls in twelve months, and her lady-
ship seems to think we oughtn’t to complain if she
starves us because they starve themselves. Two
mutton chops will be cooked for dinner, and never
more than four. The other day when visitors
called unexpected, the girls’ chops were sneaked
back again, and served up to the guests. The
girls went without. As for us, we try to- shame
the missus by going out and buying things and
eating them under her nose, but she only gets at
us for the sin of extravagance, and puts up the
Rev. Bob to mention the evil of over-eatin’ in the
prayers.” It seemed to me that Merely Mary
Ann must be a long-suffering soul to endure all
this.

“Poor Hawk”: The Governor-General
came to Bourke, and Bourke girded up its wasted
loins, donned its best 4s. lid. hat, and gave its
bluchers an extra dose of neatsfoot oil. The G.-G.
was met at the railway platform by the leading
lights of the district, while lined up outside were
the school children, who sang “Gorsave” in
fearful discord, and the local band, which played
“Gorsave ” even worse than the children sang it.
At night a banquet was held in honor of the
event, and some of the speeches would have done
credit to the House of Commons. Here is a
sample
Yer Ludsh ipi —\Ve welcome yer to our little midst.
We, as loyle citizens of this little outback ’amlet, we
welcome yer as we would King Edward VII. himself ; and
orl we’re sorry for is that yer ludship’s missus isn’t
aboard, that our loyle wives and darters could do ’omage
and ’onors to ’er. We drink yer ’ealth, me lud, in
Bourke’s best —not whisky, or beer, or wine; but Mr.
’s best champadgnee. Long life to yer, me lud,
and all yer addykonks.
On Monday, alter the train had gone, Bourke
dropped back into its usual tiredness, and its
conscience smites it somewhat for the extra wear
and tear of the new 4s. lid. hat, and for the wasted
neatsfoot oil.
THE SPRING VALE NECROPOLIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A brochure has been sent round explaining the advan-
tages of the Spring Vale (Melbourne) Necropolis. To
bring death within the reach of all, special, private,
selected, and cheap graves have been prepared at a varied
scale of charges.
Though some are for a burial that casts colossal gloom,
And some are happy with a monumental mason’s tomb ;
Though some prefer the lonesome bush, and some the
raging sea,
A little crib at Spring Vale looks quite good enough for
me.
I like the sceneree
About that cemet’ree —
The Spring Vale sanatorium exactly fixes me.
With canopies and rockeries and nicely-painted fence,
Upon this model lodging-house they’ve spared no dashed
expense;
To suit all tastes they’ve fixed a scale in this abode of
bliss,
You’re lucky if you get a berth in their Necropolis.
However could you miss
A chance so rare as this,
A private grave, for twenty bob, in their Necropolis?
For special sites they’ve special rates that men of high
degree
May keep in death their adipose respectability.
The Fat Man sleeps in richer soil than men like Bill and
Jim.
“ Sixteen by sixteen ” (fifty quid) ’s the area for him.
His ashes won’t be blent
With those who owe him rent;
He rots in state, aloof from Socialistic detriment.
The soldier or the naval man, they set him up in style,
With muffled drums and loud salute and stately marching
file ;
A special charge has been composed for burying the
brave,
Like Nelson or like Wellington he has a hero’s grave.
When bands strike up “ Gor’ Save,”
And grand old banners wave
(P’raps the African marauder would prefer a Chinese
grave).
Though some cry for the lonesome bush, some for the
raging sea,
I guess this Spring Vale tombstone shop exactly fixes me.
According to your banking-book and weight of brass or
bricks,
In special junks of hallowed earth your graded dust doth
mix.
However could you miss
A noble chance like this ?
The graves are priced like fancy goods in this Necropolis.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.

The disappearance of Preacher James of
Albany (W.A.), who is now alleged to have left
for. Canada under the name of Lee, with a
lady much younger than himself, looked
from the first like a carefully devised
vanishment; it also looked, on the whole, rather
too elaborate and there were weak spots m it.
James was a Methodist parson and 51 years of age
—both circumstances that might make any man
feel tired, and fill him with a sudden craving to go
whale-fishing or to set out to pursue the wild ass in
Persia. James started for Sydney on an Easter
holiday, and the story of the disappearance
divided itself into three fits :
(1) He reported in a letter that he had made the
acquaintance of Robert Hunter in the train, and was
careful to mention that Hunter had lost the use of his
right hand through writer's cramp.
(2) He reported that, owing to the hotels being full, he
had gone to share Hunter’s lodgings.
(3) Hunter wrote (with his left hand) from Suva (Fiji)
stating that Rev. James had fallen overboard while they
were boating on Sydney Harbor, and that he was still
overboard. Hunter had not informed the police, having
reasons for not running against that excellent but under-
paid institution, but he now communicated the facts, with
his left hand.
Apparently, it all hinged on Hunter having cramp
in his right hand. That accounted for 1 tunter (or
James or anybody else) writing a villainous scrawl
'of explanation with his left, hand—a scrawl that
couldn’t be identified as the work of anybody in
particular. The best kind of disguise in such
cases is to use the left hand ; it produces a consis-
tent scrawl, whereas an attempt to disguise the
ordinary writing done with the right hand is
generally a failure. And as long as the people
you are writing to know that the scrawl is done
with your left hand (the right one being disabled
by cramp) they don’t suspect the production of
being a disguise, nor do they wonder why a man
of education should perpetrate such an unspeak-
able fist.
Parson James seems to have provided himself
with money before he left. He is said to have
shaved off his beard, and got out of his clerical
garb at some stage of his holiday. He didn’t call on
any of his parsonical friends in Sydney. Neither
he nor Hunter left their names at the address he
gave. The meeting with Robert Hunter (who
had cramp in his right hand and so had to write
the letter of explanation with his left hand in a
fist which might be the production of anybody’s
left hand) was something of a coincidence ; Rev.
James is said to have had a parsonical friend
called Robert Hunter, and it might have been
better if the man with the disabled hand had been
named Bloggs. The body of the lost Rev. has
been assiduously fished for in Sydney harbor, but
so far it hasn’t been found (still a shark might
have got it). If Parson James proves to be dead,
despite all the appearances to the contrary, it is
certainly up to the law to find Robert Hunter and
ask him for an explanation—accompanied, per-
haps, with a deposit as a guarantee of good faith.
But if the rev. gentleman has simply revolted
against the monotony of life, and resolved to go to
Klondyke and see now the Polar bear is getting
on, it is difficult to altogether blame him. Life
is a dreadfully monotonous thing, and the most
sedate and elderly person is liable to be seized at
times with a fierce desire to go and personally
attack the rhinoceros in Sumatra, or wherever else
that animal may be found. The call of the wild
comes to all men at one time or another, and then
even a tame and gentle product of civilisation feels
an inclination to go forth and be primeval man
again—like Harold Hardraada, or Magnus Bare-
foot, or Olaf Coalscuttle, or any other rioter, or
pirate, or sea-king.
* * * *
The case of Knowles, the Palmerston
North (M.L.) wife-killer, seems, on more com-
plete information, to be directly traceable to
“ The Light of the World.” The ex-farm-burner
went with a friend to view that soul-stirring pro-
duction ; he looked at it fixedly, and then he
cleared the hall at two bounds, and ran swiftly
home as the fly crows, and demolished his wife
with a gun before she had time to ask him how
the picture had agreed with him. Apparently he
was a peaceful citizen till he saw the “Light,”
and then he immediately became a barking des-
perado. Which shows the strange effects that
may arise through a sudden rush of oil-painting to
the head—especially if a £lOOO oil-painting
rushes to a three-and-sixpenny head.
THE NEXT MORNING.
SORROWFUL ONE: “’Eavens! If I only had
thought I’d be as thirsty as this this morning
I’d ’are drank more last night.”
The law has been tying itself into hard
knots in Sydney over a “nurse” who did the
usual thing and was rewarded with six years’ im-
prisonment. Judge Fitzhardinge, who passed the
sentence, reserved some points of law for the Full
Court; and the Full Court upended the convic-
tion and the nurse fell out of gaol. Then the
High Court upended the Full Court’s decision;
and the Law Department concluded
that the lady shouldn’t have fallen
out, the Full Court having turned
the case wrong side up, so it
ordered her to be locked up again—
which incidentally inusthave given
his Honor Fitzhardinge some
pleasant moments, as the Full
Court had just been making sting-
ing remarks about his treatment
of another case. The ancient
nurse, who by this time was
badly bruised through being
poured backwards and for-
wards, next appealed on the
ground that, having been once
released, she was released for
good and all, and that the
High Court proceedings inter-
ested her in only an academic
manner of speaking—though
why the Full Court proceed-
ings shouldn’t have also in-
terested her in the same man-
ner of speaking wasn’t ex-
plained. Anyhow, the Full
Court decided that to all in-
tents and purposes she hadn't
been released at all, and that
she hadn’t been out of gaol,
and therefore there wasn’t
anything illegal in returning
her to gaol, seeing that in the
eye of the law she never had
been anywhere else. But just
at that point the law found
itself tied in another knot; the
woman had been sentenced to
six years’ imprisonment, and
for manslaughter, inN.S. Wales,
even an ancient nurse in a back
street can’t legally get more than
three years’ imprisonment, though she
can legally get six years’ penal servitude.
Whereupon the Full Court shut its unani-
mous eye and decided that the ancient
nurse should do six years’ penal servitude,
with the reservation that it shouldn’t be
penal servitude, which seems to be imprisonment
with more skilly in it than plain imprisonment.
And whether that is the end of the ancient nurse
out of the mean back street, Heaven and the old
stocking that Costs come out of only know.
