Thomas Brown, alias Anderson, learned at
the Warragul (Vic.) sessions recently the danger
of taking a man down for his boots. Near
Thomas there lived one Pestlin, who with great
trouble had made himself a pair of foot-coverings
without counterpart in the world. They were of
unique shapelessness and weighed about half a
ton. Thomas stole them, and none knew whither
they had gone, till Thomas walked one day to the
Drouin trucking-yards, and, cutting out a sheep,
slew it, and carried it (dropping its entrails by the
way) home to his camp. Pestlin’s boots, on
Thomas’s feet, left tracks like those of a devastating
giant. The police followed the huge indentations
in the earth to Thomas’s camp, and found mutton
bones, which Thomas in vain swore were those of
a large,rabbit. Pestlin, in his hut far off, heard
of the lagoon-like footprints, and knew that no
boots but his had made them. Coming to inquire,
Pestlin also found his coat in Thomas’s camp. So
Thomas, after getting nine months for the sheep,
got also 12 months for the boots and six months
for the coat —all cumulative. If Thomas grows
hungry for mutton again, he will travel bare-foot.
* » * *
Berwick (Vic.) has given up searching for
its lost boy—one of the unhappy Mason family.
Every Sunday for the past few weeks the whole
township turned out on horseback to hunt the
ranges for the youngster, who vanished in a gully
one Saturday night early in April. If this family
had lived 1000 years ago it would have been re-
garded as under a curse. The lost boy was tongue-
tied ; his eldest brother is blind ; and his 17-year-
old sister, in one of the Sunday search expeditions,
was thrown from her horse and killed.
’FRISCO—APRIL 18th, 1906.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Man builds a city marvellous,
With spreading towers and walls ;
O’er all the land at sunset’s hour
Its shadow eastward falls.
The tired old Earth her shoulders shrugs—
A ruined heap is all.
VIC.
E.C.
THE MAN WITH THE HOE,
Millet’s famous picture, destroyed in the San Francisco earthquake. The following
almost equally celebrated verses on the picture are by Charles Edwin Markham.
WED by the weight of centimes he
M 3 leans
B'V Upon his hoe and gazes on the
ground.
The emptiness of ages in his face,
And on his back the burden of the world.
Who made him dead to rapture and despair,
A thing that grieves not and that never hopes,
Stolid and stunned, a brother to the ox t
Who loosened and let down this brutal jaw l
Whose was the hand that slanted back this
brow l
Whose breath blew out the light within this
brain ?
Is this the thing that Lord God made and gate
To have dominion over sea and lawI;
To trace the stars and search the Heavens for
power ;
To feel the passion of Eternity l
Is this the dream He dreamed who shaped the
suns
And pillared the blue firmament ivith light ?
Down all the stretch of Hell to its last gidf
There is no shape more terrible than this — ■
More tongued with censure of the world's blind
greed —
More filled with signs and portents for the
soul —
More fraught with menace to the universe.
What gulfs between him arid the seraphim !
Slave of the wheel of labor, what to him
Are Plato and the swing of Pleiades l
What the long reaches of the peaks of song,
The rift of dawn, the reddening of the rose ?
Through this dread shape the suffering ages look ,
Time's tragedy is in that aching stoop ;
Through this dread shape humanity betrayed,
Plundered, profaned and disinherited,
Cries protest to the Judges of the World,
A protest that is also prophecy.
O masters, lords, and rulers in all lands,
Is this the handiwork you give to God,
This monstrous thing distorted and soul-
quenched 1
How will you ever straighten up this shape ;
Give back the upward looking and the light;
Rebuild in it the music and the dreams ;
Touch it again with immortality ;
Make right the immemorial infamies,
Perfidious wrongs, immedicable woes !■
O masters, lords, and rulers in all lands.
How will the Future reckon with this Man ?
How answer his brute question in that hour
When whirlwinds of rebellion shake the world /
How will it be with kingdoms and with kings
With those who shape him to the thing he is —
When this dumb Terror shall reply to God
After the silence of the centuries ?
HEART DISEASE.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
“For five years my heart was so weak that my
life was just hanging by a thread. All this time
I was crippled with Rheumatism. The three best
doctors in Launceston could do nothing for me.
After they failed, eighteen boxes of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills cured me for good. My case is a
downright miracle.”
_ That is the statement of Michael Clayton, who
lives at 9 Cypress St., off Elphin Rd., Launceston.
For 25 years he worked in the Waverley Woollen
Mills, and the facts of his case are well known to
Mr. Hogarth, one of the leading citizens of Laun-
ceston. Mr. Clayton himself has solemnly sworn
to the facts before Senator D. Storrer—and it goes
without saying that Senator Storrer would not
lend his name to a statement that was not true
in every detail.
“ It was in 1895 that I first felt the Rheumatism
in my shoulders,” said Mr. Clayton, going back
to the beginning of his illness. “It was a dull
gnawing pain that stiffened all my muscles. I
could not raise my arms to my head. Little by
little the pain worked down niy left side and
around my heart. My left arm was worst of all.
It was so swollen and sore that I could not button
my waistcoat. Many a night I did not get an
hour’s sleep, and my nerves were all unstrung.
Often I was sick on my stomach in the mornings.
I gradually lost weight and my whole health
went to pieces.
‘ One day when I was working in the factory, a
sudden smothering feeling came over me, and I
fell down in a half faint. When my mates found
me I was gasping for breath. They thought I was
dying from Heart Disease. After that, I never
knew when one of these attacks would come on.
Whenever my heart jumped or fluttered I was in
mortal dread. Day and night, I always had a
queer feeling round my heart. It was not exactly
a pain—it was just a stuffy choking feeling as if
the very breath were being crushed out of me. My
face used to flush, and then my head would swim
and my ears ring. A feeling of faintness would
come over me, and a cold sweat would stand out
on my face. Often I thought my last hour had
come.
“There are not three better doctors in the
whole of Tasmania than the three who treated me
all through this illness,” Mr. Clayton went on to
say. “ But they were not able to do me one atom
of good. When I was nearly mad with the
Rheumatism, they injected morphia into my veins
—but the pain was ten times worse afterwards.
And all this time my heart was getting weaker.
At last, one of the doctors found a lump on my
left side, just over my heart. Little by little it
grew and spread. I tackled the doctor, and he
told me I had Fatty Degeneration of the Heart.
When I asked him what that meant, he said that
the muscle of my heart was gradually turning into
fat. It was so soft that my heart might burst at
any time—for the fat might get so thick that my
heart would not have room to beat at all. From
that day on, I knew that I might drop dead any
moment.
“ The doctors tried to work the lump down by
rubbing it—but that only seemed to make it
spread. Then they tried to melt it with hot
flannels and oils—but that did no good either.
Every month I found it harder to breath. Often
I had to crawl home from the factory more dead
than alive. Sometimes I honestly wished that the
Heart Disease would put an end to all I was
suffering. The Rheumatism seemed to be all
through my system, eating into every joint and
muscle. The agony was something awful. My
face got all drawn and haggard with suffering.
I soon grew so helpless that I could not cut myself
a pipe of tobacco. At last I had to knock off work
altogether. For three months I was absolutely
crippled. The doctors could not even ease my
pain—so my daughter got me to try Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. She thought they might cure my
Rheumatism—but neither of us ever dreamed
that they would do my heart any good.
“At first I thought that Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills were going to fail like all the doctor’s
medicines,” Mr. Clayton added. “All I noticed
after the first box was that I had a grand appetite.
During the third box, I fancied ray Rheumatism
was a little easier—and before I finished the next
box I was sure of it. After the fifth box, I did
not want any more morphia. In a few weeks I
was able to button my own shirt and lace my boots.
Then I noticed that the lump around my heart
was going down. I could hardly believe my eyes.
Before long there wasn’t a sign of it left. I lost
that stuffy smothering feeling, and my heart began
to beat in a healthy way. It was a long uphill
fight—but, in the end, I was completely cured
with 18 boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
actually saved my life. I can jump about like, a
boy now, and my heart is as sound as any man’s.
I have never had a pain or an ache since Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills cured me five years ago—so
that shows I am cured for good. My daughter
here will tell you that every word I speak is
Gospel Truth.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills work these wonderful cures,
after doctors and common medicines have failed, because
they actually make new blood. That one thing is all they
do—but they do it well. They don’t act on the bowels.
They don’t tinker with mere symptoms. They strike
right at the cause of disease in the blood, and so cure
indigestion, kidney and liver diseases, palpitation of the
heart, nervousness, neuralgia, rheumatism, sciatica,
partial paralysis, and the ailments that fill the lives of so
many women with misery. Do not take any pills without
the full name “ Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People ”
on the wrapper round the box. Sold by all chemists and
storekeepers, or sent post free by the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Sydney, at 2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d.
Medical advice given free.*

This State insurance office, whose policies are backed up
by the entire resources and credit of Maoriland, has had
another moderately prosperous year ; though if Maori-
landers stuck a little more closely to their State institu-
tion, and didn’t need so much pressing to join it, and
after they had joined it didn’t allow their policies to lapse
so often, there might be a considerable reduction in the
expense rate ; and that is a movement for which there is
room. On the basis of total income the outlay shows
up like this: —
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1896 ..£l3 18 7 per cent. 1901 ..£»2 16 11 per cent.
1897 .. 14 17 2 „ 1902 .. 13 5 5
1898 .. 14 610 „ 1903 .. 18 19 4
1899 .. 14 2 11 „ 1904 .. 12 11 8
1900 .. 14 10 0 „ | 1905 .. 13 0 0
The last five-year period certainly shows an improvement
on the first; but it is Maoriland’s own fault that there is
not a much larger improvement; and, anyhow, some im-
provement would naturally follow upon the accumulation
of cheaply-managed interest-bearing funds. Considering
the rather small proportion of new business that is being
gathered in, the expense rate is much higher than it
ought to be. Adopting an arbitrary rule, because
there is no other sort, and charging renewal premiums
with 7 per cent, for expenses, and single premiums and
consideration for annuities with 3 per cent., and then
charging all the balance of the expenses to new business,
it is found that the latter, yielding £21,414 in premiums,
cost £43,137 to secure ; that is, it cost 201 per cent, of
the new premiums to get new business. This is expen-
sive progress. The National Mutual, with funds now
slightly in excess of the Maoriland State Life Depart-
ment’s, got its new business at a cost of 130 per cent, of
premiums, reckoning on the same basis, and allowing for
re-insurances. The Australian Widows’ Fund, which was
pilloried recently on the score of expensive management,
shows (still on the same basis) 218 per cent, of new pre-
miums as the cost of new business ; and the difference
between 201 and 218 per cent, isn’t so great as to make
the comparison over-pleasant for the Maoriland office,
even though the latter’s high rate is to some extent due
to the larger taxes it has to pay.
The Maoriland Life Department cannot expect
and need not desire any rapid growth ; for one thing- it
isn’t allowed to scour the earth for business ; for another,
bigness isn’t goodness. But if it doesn’t scour the eartli
for business—if it keeps itself within Maoriland—its
expense rate ought to be small to correspond. It should
be Mile to write quite as much new business as it is doing
without going to one-third the expense, relying mainly
upon the good sense of Maorilanders to take an interest
in a concern which has all Maoriland for its guarantee.
Instead, it seems that it has to battle for business just
like any concern that has little except good intentions
at the back of it. The Maoriland State office, backed up
as it is, ought to get thousands of pounds’ worth
of business from democratic Maoriland because it is
what it is, just as the A.M.P. has done in Australia ;
and its expense rate ought to be as low as the A.M.P.’s or
lower. Instead of that, considering the proportion of its
new business, it has one of the highest expense rates,
which is a pretty severe reflection on Maoriland’s alleged
democratic principles.
The lapse rate is also not as satisfactory as it
should be in a country of such prosperity as Maoriland.
Last year 3116 policies were taken out, and 1048 lapsed,
0r33’6 per cent.; and, in amount, the policies taken out
and lapsed bore exactly the same relation. Now, in the
A.M.P. the lapsed policies for 1904 amounted to only 14'9
per cent, of the new policies. Taking lapsed and sur-
rendered policies together, the proportion in the A.M.P.
was 26’3 per cent., and in the Maoriland office 47'7 per
cent. All the lapsed Maoriland policies carried among
them only £8272 of reversionary bonuses ; so they were,
on the whole, new policies ; and, in view of the heavy
cost of getting new business, this large percentage of
lapses is a further matter for regret. Democratic
Maoriland is not only not doing its full duty to the State
office by joining it; it to a very great extent fails in its
duty even after it has joined it. Then no doubt after it
has had to be pursued at great expense, first for a policy
and then for the premiums, it wonders why the bonuses
are not three times as big.
In the matter of interest earnings the Maoriland
Life Office stands well:

There is such a scramble for the business under
the new Workmen’s Compensation Act in Queensland
that one would think there were millions in it. This is a
list of co.’s which did not touch this class of business
until lately, but are rushing after it now : North Queens-
land Accident Insurance (new co.); New Zealand Fire In-
surance (which has taken over the N.Z. Accident Insur-
ance Co.); South British, Victoria, Standard (M.L.), and
Australian Alliance. The Colonial Mutual was already
in the field, along with the Ocean and three other foreign
offices ; and it is understood the Yorkshire Life and Acci-
dent, the Alliance, and the London and Lancashire are
“ making inquiries.” Already there is cutting and slash-
ing, and it looks as though there will be a repetition of
the experience of British offices, which made losses whole-
sale in the same sort of fight after the same sort of law
was passed in England.

Mar., ’O6 24,383 32 „ „ 16,232 186 7,965 165,710
As usual the co. offers some striking figures. Net profit
works out at £9 6s. 9d. per cent, on capital and reserves.
One large question, no doubt, remains to be answered—
though these profits are supposed to be net, is enough
written off for wear and tear ? Certainly, besides the in-
significant amounts shown above, profit and loss is directly
charged with £lB7B for depreciation of plant, &c. Even
that looks small; still plant which cost £64,918 has now
been reduced in book value to £33,716. The items that
may mean trouble by-and-bye are—Freehold brewery
lands and buildings, £69,798"; hotels, £154,040; lease-
holds, £1853 ; total £225,691, all at cost. In 1900 the hotels
stood in at £16,627 ; so except for that small proportion,
the purchases have been made during the past six years—
that is, apparently, since the big jump took place in Wes-
tralian values. The freehold rents show a return of £6BOl
(gross, no doubt); and on the average holding of hotel pro-
perties, that means almost exactly 9 per cent. On that
return it certainly would not be argued this side of Aus-
tralia that the property was over-valued ; still Perth has
its own conditions. But this looks certain : if this pro-
perty keeps its value, then the rental value will fall, and
with it the profits ; it returns 9 per cent, now because the
future capital value is uncertain. Therefore, the co. is
going to lose either some of its reserves or some of its
earnings.

All the shares have been for some time past paid up. The
extra dividend for the year is a triennial distribution, in
which borrowers also share ; their draw (£344) has in the
foregoing been deducted from the year’s profits, which
even then show £8 11s. 9d. per cent, on shareholders’
funds, after writing £687 off contingency reserve. Prac-
tically, therefore, the co. is paying shareholders a steady
10 per cent, per annum. But the co.’s risks are growing
steadily, too. Consider the growth of deposits, all repre-
sented by mortgages:—
A9Ol .. £93,553 1904 .. £127,476
1902 .. 94,338 1905 .. 138,211
1903 .. 108,277 1906 .. 152,350
At March 31st, 1900, the mortgages stood at £161,045 ;
six years later, at £227,390, an increase of £66,345.
That is to say, the co. has lent another £66,345 on
securities that are liable to slump ; yet out of current
profits only £5983 has been put aside to provide for the
slump, and even that £5983 is in the same sort of securi-
ties. So far as the depositors are concerned, though
their loans have increased so fast, the position is still
quite comfortable ; all these non-liquid securities, which
are yielding such a large return, could fall no less than 47
per cent, in value before the shareholders’ funds would
be wiped out.

There is nothing to explain the surprising jump in profits
during the past year. It is possible that this co. takes
credit for the whole of the corpus commission on an
estate when it comes in, leaving the annual commission
to meet subsequent costs, for which it is some-
times inadequate. A trustee co. may in such a
case find itself in the end carrying on business at a dead
loss ; and then the interests of clients are liable to suffer
through the cutting down of expenses—which would
mostly mean the employment of cheap and inefficient
officers. When, therefore, a trustee co.’s profits jump
about in this surprising manner, it falls under suspicion
—not only its stock as an investment, but the concern
itself as a trustee. In the end it may have to be made
illegal for a trustee co. to accept corpus commissions at
all, such an uncertain influence are they liable to have.
This co.’s funds are about equally divided between public
securities and mortgages; the interest earnings are
lumped with commissions, and that further clouds the
situation. Nobody should have anything to do with such
a co., either as investor or client, until he has more infor-
mation than the published accounts give. The trouble
about information furnished otherwise is that it isn’t
subject to public criticism, and usually carries no respon-
sibility.

lllawarra District (N.S.W.) Building Society
has now a capital of £9495, on which in the past year (to
March 31st) it made a profit of £706. Of this, the 6 p.c.
dividend credited to “ investors ” absorbed £517, leaving
£lB9 to add to the previous reserves of £l6OO. The ad-
vances brought in just 9 p.c., so there doesn’t appear to be
many “stiff” ones in that lot. And, anyhow, these se-
curities could slump 17 p.c. and still be covered by re-
serves, leaving the capital intact. The little society is
strong for two reasons—it works on its own money, so
that it hasn’t to provide for the slumping of securities
held against loans of borrowed money ; it keeps its ex-
penses down to a modest £250 a year, and sc far as the
evidence available shows, puts a decent amount by in case
of accidtnt.

But they are not true reserves that are shown above—
they practically never are in connection with a mining
co. At the most they are a fund for the return of
capital when the mine is played out, and the original
assets have disappeared. Meantime the disappearing
mine remains in the balance-sheet at original cost. But
Mt. Lyell differs from most mining co.’s in one important
respect: its so-called reserves have not been sunk in non-
liquid assets—plant and such like. On the contrary, its
surplus of liquid assets over current liabilities is £438,989,
even reckoning' the debentures in among the current
liabilities. As to those 5 per cent, debentures, which
were part of the North Lyell’s burden, very little shifting
has been done during the half-year—which, presumably,
hasn’t been the co.’s fault. From the outset the move-
ment has been :

Mt. Lyell expenses are moving up—in all
branches except converting (Bessemerising), where
economies are being gradually developed. A comparison
for three half-years :

the previous half), the outputs of metal varied this way :

The directors do not quote average price for copper for
their half-year. But it can be figured out that approxi-
mately £20,750 of last half-year’s increased profit was due
to the larger metal output, and about £45,000 to the
better prices. The profit was, as shown, £267,795, or at
the rate of £5B 13s. 3d. per ton of copper—which is an
amazing return, even keeping in mind the silver and gold.
From its smelting work Moonta and Wallaroo Co. was
happy enough with a profit of £lllBs. 9d. per ton for its
last year ; in fact, Moonta by itself showed only £7 7s.
lid. But then Moonta and Wallaroo have no silver and
gold to pay most of the charges. And how much of the
£58135. 3d. wouldn’t Mt. Lyell shareholders willingly
forego in return for the knowledge (if they could get it)
that their mine will be going strong 45 years after its
birth, as Wallaroo and Moonta are ?

Write-off £B7lO for depreciation of plant, and get £38,094
net. At 925. 6d. per share, this is at the rate of 16} per
cent, per annum—six years’ purchase, irrespective of any
profits that may come out of the sale of tailings to be
treated for zinc. And Block 10 will be able to sell its
tailings longer than almost any other Broken Hill co.—
just as it was able to sell tens of thousands of tons years
before any others got in the business. For the idea that
this is quite a new source of profit is all wrong, though it
has been of fine service to the crowd that has been boom-
ing Barrier stocks for 12 months past. Certainly, owing
to the high price of spelter, tailings dumps, that were not.
looked at till lately, have been put under option now, and
some have been sold outright. Block 10 itself lately sold
100,000 tons to the Gilles Ore Concentration Syndicate at
6s. per ton ; though this sale does not come into the ac-
counts just issued. But as against probable additions to
profits from the sale of zinc-bearing tailings, the much
more certain fall in the value of silver and lead must
be placed. Lead averaged £l6 os. 3d. per ton last half-
year ; in the previous half-year, £l3 Bs. In that previous
half-year the profit was at the rate of £5 6s. 3d. per ton
of lead in the concentrates ; in the past half-year it was
£7 os. 6d. per ton. In other words, though lead has
averaged £2 12s. 3d. per ton more, the profit has only
been £1 14s. 3d. per ton more ; which means that costs
of mining and extraction have been greater and (pro-
bably) that the foreign ore-buyers upon whom this co.
depends have grabbed a good part of the extra profit. As
a matter of fact, the past half-year’s increased profit on
the basis of a ton of lead should have amounted to con-
siderably more than the rise in lead, because silver
has gone up too ; and that, instead of being more,
it was considerably less isn’t a matter for shareholders
to feel very glad about. Approximately the matter can
be summed up this way : Given the present quality of
ore and present costs, with silver at 2s. sfd. and lead at
£l6, the co. can make £46,800 per half-year out of silver
and lead. Every movement of £1 per ton in lead means
about £6500 per half-year in profits ; every penny move
in silver means £1350. There remain after that the
future of zinc and possible economies in treatment to
gamble on. But past experience of sulphides has shown
that economies and improvements are counterbalanced
by the change for the worse in the character of the ore.

The Bast Greta Coal Mining Co. (N.S.W.)

Six months ago the directors recommended the usual 10
per cent, dividend ; but before the meeting was held the
co.’s Stanford-Merthyr mine had struck disaster, and only
5 per cent, was paid. The past half-year’s figures show
more of the consequences ; but all recent tests of the fire
area have been satisfactory, and there is probably
no fire at all left now. The co., besides its mines",
has a railway with extensive coal-hauling contracts,
some of them new. Its capital is now £150,000,
all shares paid; debts to the bank and public
total £7321, and besides the East Greta (freehold)
and Stanford-Merthyr (leasehold) mines and railway
and plant (£155,227), it has liquid assets (cash and sundry
debtors) amounting to £20,619. Even before the disaster,
it wasn’t quite earning its 10 per cent, dividend in addi-
tion to the amount written off ; and as it takes £6OOO to
write off 8 per cent, per annum from plant alone, the
amount written off was probably not a bit extravagant.
The 10 per cent, dividend appears, therefore, to have been
excessive. When work is resumed in Stanford-Merthyr,
the co. should, however, with new machinery and new
contracts, make larger profits than before; and, of
course, it needs to, for £25,000 of capital has been got in
during the past 12 months.
* * * *
Figures that follow show, more or less, the pro-
gress of the Balmain Co-operative Society, Ltd., the most
vigorous and progressive of the purely co-operative
N.S.W. concerns outside the mining centres. The pub-
lished accounts are complicated, and what many of the
figures mean can be little more than guessed at; and, to
make matters worse, the form of the published accounts
has been frequently altered. If the society wants it
members to understand the position, it had better lose no
time in calling in a consulting accountant, with a view to
simplifying matters, and reducing possibilities of error
and other things. As nearly as the matter can be made
out after a weary three hours and more spent on trying
to make figures tally that won’t tally, the accounts, put-
ting the results by half-years, show up in this way :
Dividends Writ-
(sp.e.)& ten Re-
Capital; Profit. Bonus. Off. serves.
Sept., 1902.. £291 £ll4 £B7 £2O £7

Sept., 1904.. 1492 370 298 57 149
March, 1905 .. 2173 462 406 54 151
Sept., 1905.. 2502 582 524 57 152
March, 1906 .. 2947 736 645 68 175
As may be judged from the foregoing, hardly any of the
interest (dividend) or bonus on purchases is drawn in
cash, most of it being transferred to capital, whereupon
it becomes entitled to 5 per cent.; and 5 per cent, com-
pound interest, additions made quarterly, isn’t an invest-
ment that happens every day. But the committee may
pay out any member who isn’t buying as much at the
society’s stores as it thinks he ought to; and that’s a
handicap on the thrifty capitalist who can crowd through
for a fortnight on three loaves of bread and a sardine.
Fines and some other trifles apparently don’t go through
profit and loss account, but are used to directly write
down stock and property ; with these small additions
the allowance for depreciation amounts to about a liberal
10 per cent, per annum. No progress worth mentioning
is being made in the matter of reserves. Reserves don’t
carry any 5 per cent; compound interest. But they are
mighty handy things to have about the premises for all
that. The society has few debts—a total of £176, in-
cluding a few small deposits.
• * * *
Owners of the Paringa, Kalgoorlie, after about
a dozen years of fruitless struggle, have made up their
mind that the thing is a frost. To try and save the
situation, the board has decided to acquire the Toora tin
mines in Victoria, which, at any rate, will give directors
their fees for another year or two. The Toora tin mines,
which, up to now, have hidden themselves mar-
vellously, appear to be a wonderful property. 8., M. and
00. sent an outsider to report on them. He found 20,000,000
tons of alluvial tin gravel worth lOd. per cubic yard,
which could be treated at a cost of 2}d. per yard, and would
leave a profit of 7}d. per yard, with tin at £l5O
per ton. The vendors are to receive £2OOO in cash
and 38,000 shares in a co. of 80,000 shares, £1
each. The Paringa co. has to pay the vendors £2OOO
in cash and to supply £20,000 working capital, for
which it will get the remaining 42,000 shares. 8., M. and
Co.’s borrowed expert says the co. will make an annual
profit for the next 20 years of £312,500 by erecting a
plant costing £15,000. Victorians must be slower than
the W.A. ’Groper if such a proposition has lain dormant
all these years. One query is: Why did 8., M. and Co. not
employ one of their own experts to sample the shows ?
Another query is : What sort of a proposition is it that
figures on tin "at £l5O per ton, when before last year it
hadn’t averaged that price for half a century ? Ten years
ago it averaged £59 9s. lid., which is some way off £l5O.
* * * *
Great Boulder, Kalgoorlie, is shortly to be the
leading mine of W.A. Ridgway’s slimes plant has proved
a success ; it will supersede filter presses, and, it is
stated, will make a difference of about ss. per ton—in
lower costs and better extraction. The plant is auto-
matic ; the pulp is sucked up, and, after being washed, is
discharged by air pressure. One man can look after a
dozen machines. Each machine can treat 50 tons daily,
and already 10 machines are practically completed by
Bennet and Co. There are 600,000 tons of residues now on
the dump.
* * * *
Some fair returns have been coming from the
patchy Murchison (W.A.). Peak Hill l and the Horseshoe
are both in a bad way. and Abbott’s is nearly dead.
Twelve or 13 years ago, Menzies, then connected with
Star of the East, predicted things of Abbott’s and a good
deal of capital went there ;• but only three or four satis-
factory crushings were got. Stampers can’t talk half as
glowingly as experts. At Meekatharra soz. and 6oz. crush-
ings are the average from the Fenian and the Marmont.
About 4000 gallons of fresh water per day have been tapped
in the North Commodore, and this will keep the State bat-
tery going. A dam like that at Menzies is needed. Bottom
and sides are faced with Trinidad aspbaltum, and it
holds. To give small parties a show, 200-ton
parcels will be the limit at the State battery
in future instead of 400 tons. At Cue the Duke
of York asked for 12 months’ exemption, and the
Warden actually granted six. Out at Black Range
crushings average up to 25dwt„ and prospects are
brightening. Understood Government will propose next
session to extend the Mount Magnet railway to Black
Range, about 100 miles. Morning Star, run by Joe
Bryant for the last 10 years in the interests of a Ballarat
co., keeps pegging away. Averages about Bdwt., which
pays and leaves a snack over.
• * * •
The high price of tin is giving Pilbarra (W. A.)
district a lift. About 200 men are there, and doing as
well as want of water will let them. At Wodgina there
are some likely-looking shows, on which the Government
geologist has just reported hopefully. An amalgamation
of 160 acres is under option to a Melbourne syndicate,
which is sending up a man. Option expires in July. But
it will have to be something extra to make Melbourne
bite. Outside finicking dredging shows the public will
take no interest in tin in spite of its price. Randolph
Bedford took a N.Q. tin claim down to the south
recently ; but he has gone back and there isn’t any news
of a float. The one biggish thing that the public did bite
at —on the strength mostly of John Moffatt’s name—was
Dalziell’s ; and, though that may make profits when the
mill Mr. Moffatt is leisurely putting up is ready, still it
hasn’t developed up to expectations.
-t * * *
East Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), floated on the
strength of its proximity to the Great Fingall, still
produces next to nothing but assays. At a depth of
413 ft. a mixed-up ore body was passed through in the
main shaft which was said to assay 3oz. per ton
for a width of 3ft. The shaft was continued to
527 ft., and crosscuts at 500 ft. showed that the
reef was absolutely worthless. Driving at 500 ft.
has been continued for some time, but nothing
of any value has been located so far. This mine is to the
east of the Great Fingall, and has the Trenton and Kinsella
to the south, and the Murchison Associated to the north.
The latter mine has started crosscutting at 800 ft., and,
so far, has got nothing of any value. This mine is said to
be working on the Great Fingall reef; the yarn may or
may not be true, but, as the Fingall shoot cuts off going
north, the fact of having the reef is of little consequence.
» * * *
Mt. Monger (W.A.), about 30 miles from Kal-
goorlie, is the next district likely to be heard of. A large
number of reefs and leaders running from a few inches
up to 6ft. have been exploited, but they are valueless
under existing conditions. The Firewood Co., which has
a concession to bring in firewood and mining timber, is
prepared to lay the necessary nine miles of line, but the
terms are not acceptable. If this field were opened, a
good many men would get employment at once.

Broken Hill Block 14 has made a profit of
£17,594 in six months ; but of that amount £B6OO (less
probably £9OO, being proportion of management ex-
penses) has come from carbonates ; and there is, so far as
is known, only 5600 tons more of this ore available in the
mine, which at present prices might give a profit of
£lO,OOO altogether. Practically, therefore, Block 14 can
only be valued as a sulphide mine. Apart from car-
bonates, the half-year’s profit was just under £lO,OOO.
The market value of the mine is £280,000. From this
deduct £28,364, the surplus of liquid assets, and £lO,OOO,
profits on carbonates in the mine, and the half-yearly
profit of £lO,OOO shows a return on the balance of just
over 8 per cent, per annum—and there is only about 64
years’ ore in sight! And it is no use going on perpetually
whispering “ Zinc ” ; for in this case more than half the
profits already come from zinc, and not all of it treated
in this half-year either. Look at it another way. The
profit per ton of sulphides was just over 6s. Allow a
shilling for improvements, and make it 7s. The best
price Block 14 can get for zinc tailings is 3s. 6d.=less
than 2s. 9d. per ton of ore. Total (at 2s. 9d. for
the zinc), 9s. 9d. Then 5600 tons carbonates at £2
profit=£ll,2oo; 200,000 tons (official estimate)of sulphides
at 9s. 9d. profit=£97,soo; or for the whole mine £108,700.
Yet at 31s. for ordinary and 255. for pref. shares, the mine
is valued at £280,000, or 260 per cent, above what looks like
the real present value. No douht it will be argued, as
usual, that (1) there are great possibilities in zinc treat-
ment ; (2) more ore may be found ; (3) the lead plant is
being remodelled. True ; and in reply—(l) the great zinc
possibilities are heavily discounted by the sales this co.

B.H. Block 14 does some interesting juggling
with figures in its last published accounts. Like most
mining co.’s it has not written off nearly enough for wear
and tear of plant; in other words, often when it has paid
a dividend (which hasn’t been often of late years) it has
to some extent paid it out of capital—the capital that
was put into plant. The co. therefore found itself with
plant, &c., valued in the accounts published in November
last at £97,006. Most of this value has long since disap-
peared ; the plant has been worn out, and the wear and
tear has not been written off, or the machinery has been
found unsuitable. But ingenious directors can easily get
over a little thing like that. They now write the mine
up by £86,221 (from £57,000 to £143,221), and that allows
them to write the plant down by £86,221. It is a new
way of paying old debts ; and everybody feels happy and
virtuous—till to-morrow. Even then they may feel vir-
tuous ; but some of them (who bought in at 315.) won’t
feel happy.
* -K * *
Broken Hill Junction North, the mine that is
always going to do things, made a loss last half-year of
£2OBl. Excluding fire-fighting and other non-recurring-
expenses, a profit of £952 was shown —assuming that
enough was written off for depreciation, which is very
doubtful. And lead averaged £l6 Os. 3d., and silver
2s. s|d. for the, half-year. Allowing that costs ought to
be reduced, and extraction improved, the results, in the
face of the abnormal price of metals, are extremely disap-
pointing. The best feature in the accounts just issued
is the disclosed surplus of £33,660 of liquid assets over
current liabilities. Of course, most of that is from the
recent sale of a block of shares.
A few months ago there was a curious
struggle between Broken Hill shareholders in Junc-
tion North and the Melbourne crowd. Former,
with Sydney’ and Adelaide help, got control
of a majority of shares, and prepared to shift
the management to Broken Hill. But a majority of
shareholders was also needed. The usual thing happened
—dummy shareholders were created. But the B.H.
shareholders didn’t play their cards well enough ; the
Melbourne crowd, with the rim of the office, smelt a rat,
and there was a sudden increase in the number of share-
holders there too; and so, the B.H. shareholders being-
pipped on the post, the control remained in Melbourne.
* * * *
Another movement underground in the Central ,
mine (the Sulphide Corporation’s), and for the comfort of
shareholders it is announced—“ It is nothing fresh. The
movement in this mine is almost constant, and all that
has happened is that it was rather more energetic than
usuaL” : Precisely. And that’s one reason why the
ordinary-share holders (Australian mostly) get no divi-
dends. But the. debenture-holders and the pref.-share
holders (whose name is mostly J. Bull) are all right. A
big new mill is just going- up —for which ordinary shares
will have to pay, as usual.
* * * *
A Barrier statistician has counted 117 plans
for the extinguishing of fires in mines.
* * * *
Homeward Bound, Wyalong (N.S.W.), mostly
owned in Sydney, has the tired feeling again. Some
weeks back, preliminary arrangements were made to
erect machinery. But it is said now that the reef
recently cut at 20dft. is small and patchy, and that pre- ,
sent developments do not warrant any immediate
change from the horse-whim mode of hauling. In a
trial crushing, the firsts, amounting to five tons, yielded
soz. 7dwt. to the ton, but the stone now paddocked—a
few tons—is inferior.
* * * *
Newcastle and Kembla (N.S.W.)and Port Pirie
(S.A.) were left in in the final run for the housing of the
Smelting Corporation’s works. Kembla won. The Cor-
poration’s; ideas with regard to the help to be got from
New Caledonia have, by the way, undergone a change;
thechances are that, in the end, the whole New Caledonia
proposals will be dropped, as some of them have been
already.
* * * *
White Cliffs (N.S.W.) opal miners are still dis-
satisfied with their marketing arrangements ; they live
on the rim of Sheol and gouge out the raw material and
sell it for £1 to a gentleman named Deidelstein or there-
abouts, who says it’s “ too plue,” and he knows he’ll lose
money on it. The stuff goes to Germany ; and the White
Cliffs gouger has the firm opinion that Germany sells
the j£l stone for £lO, after spending about 10s. on cut-
tingjand polishing. For 10 years past efforts have been
made to organise the gougers and command the market;
and theoretically the thing looks easy, because White
Cliffs produces over two-thirds of the world’s opal supply.
But the gougers are mostly poormen whocan’t wait, so they
have to sell on the spot; moreover, they are always com-
ing and going, and that makes organisation difficult. Just
now another attempt is being made to get better prices ; a
gouger who has been travelling reported that stone for
which he could only get £2 offered at White Cliffs he sold
in N’York for £l7. But that didn’t get over the practical
difficulty of financing the gougers. The latest idea is to
ask the English Wholesale Co-operative Society to take
up the business, and negotiations that way are now on
foot.
* * * *
A lump of opal, valued at £6OO, has been found
at the Lightning liidge field, outside Walgett (N.S.W.).
Interesting to know what the finders get for it. Several
other things are interesting ; for instance, reckoning the
£6OO lot in (it will probably fetch £200), what have been the
average earnings of the men on the field for six months?
They might work out at 10s. a week—not more. And
these are the figures for out-of-works to glue their eyes
to—not the £6OO.
* * * *
C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.), although deserted by
speculators since the drills told their sorry tale, hasn’t
slumped too much, if the drillassays are reasonably correct. ‘
But, as anticipated, the drilling has been useless work. Tub
Bulletin said, when it was proposed—“No matter what
the result of the drilling is, the main shaft will go' down
all the same; therefore,,why not sink the main shaft at -
once ? ” Now, after all the delay, the shaft is happening.
Also, just by way of consolation to shareholders, another
despatch of ore to Cockle Creek is happening. But
why hasn’t the C.S. A. made a push to erect even a single
reverb, or jacket, and so save heavy carriage ?
* * * *
Tharsis, Cobar (N.S.W.), which used to be the
Ivanhoe, and before that was the Bhoenix, which, pre-
sumably, rose from the, ashes of something still more
remote, is alleged to have struck something promising
that the Ivanhoe, the Phoenix, and the other things
missed. So far the “ something ” is very hazy and in-
definite ; and, in view of the claim’s vicissitudes, that
isn’t the sort of information that should lead to a rush of
speculation now. And there isn’t any rush either.
North Cobar, lately acquired by Australian
Mining' Estates Co., an English concern closely related to
the Oil Corporation, is (under manager L. J. Winton)
gradually equipping itself for the mission of recovering
the lost end (if there is any) of the Cobar copper lode.
Peculiarity of the Cobar lode is the occurrence of egg-
shaped lenses, and as three.of these have so far been dis-
covered in direct continuation, the Australian Mining
Estates presumably think there is no reason why there
should not be a fourth. So North Cobar is likely to undergo
the severest test of its life. Up to date it has proved
itself a veritable cabinet of disappointments,, and has
absorbed much capital—to which, by the way, the parent-
mine owners have not contributed, which shows what
they think of it. But, in spite of this, its position
demands some attention.
* * * *
Queen Bee (N.S.W.) showed wisdom in kicking
against proposed over-capitalisation. Good little mine as
it. is, the Bee, besides finding enough copper to equip
itself and pay irregular threepenny dividends on its
50,000 shares, has put a little into reserve; but the
chances of carrying a burden of 250,000 shares into the
dividend list are little better than Buckley’s. What with
improvements to machinery and duplicating the rever-
beratories, and other things that continually crop up, the
chances are that it will not pay dividends even on its
present shares for some months. The most solid share-
holders, at any rate, favor this course.
* * * *
Though N.S.W. seems waking only now to the
fact that it possesses first-class marble, there may be
seen in Sydney Museum two specimens of statuary
marble from Murrumbidgee and Yass, presented by
George Macleay just fifty years ago. Macleay at that
time maintained the excellence of Australian marble, but
his voice cried in a wilderness of importers.
* * * *
Bewick, Moreing and Co.’s new Australian
manager, Loring, is showing the Victorian public that
he knows how to drive a bargain, especially when the
other fellow is in a big hole. Poor Prentice and Southern,
after mopping up nearly £200,000, spent by one co. and
another, is handed to 8., M. and Co. for six months on
an option for which nothing is paid, with the right to
take a three-quarter interest in a 200,000 share co.
having a working capital of £30,000. This is even worse
than the old Gore-Hughes option, which shareholders
nowadays do not thank the Argus for stopping. Co.
went broke through relying on predictions about
the wash. Visick was no prophet. Gore was one when
he laughed at the promise of good yields. All
the shareholders’ stomach for call-paying has gone,
and so, with bowed head and due thankfulness, they
take a crumb from Bull’s table. Well, there is one thing
that 8., M. and Co. will now be able to say to their
Cohen friends at ’ome—“ We’ve got into deep lead wash
at last. The way may have been prepared for us, still
we’re there.” Having got there, it may be predicted
that they won’t stop long. If they do, it is equally cer-
tain they won’t be able to pay much out of the Southern
lead on a co. with a 200,000 register.
• * * * *
The, incursion of 8., M. and Co. into Rutherglen
draws attention to some late balance-sheets of the field.
Old-North Prentice and Prentice and Southern Co.’s, now
the. Prentice and Southern, spent jointly £150,000 on
their two shafts, and never paid a div. Present co. got a
loan of £20,000 from the State, and itself put twice as
much into the ground. Still no div. Great Southern
Consols has spent £44,000 of contributed money
and £176,000 of gold, and paid under £20,000
in' divs. Great Southern has spent £330,000,
of which £227,670 was from gold won, and has
paid about £20,000 in divs. Great Southern No. 1,
recently defunct, had an equally sad score up on the
wall. Chiltern Valley Consols, at the other end of the
field, is chasing these figures along very well, with no
sight of divs. ; and it is left, therefore, for John Cock’s
mine, the Chiltern Valley, to save the district with
£215,000 paid in divs. on a total expenditure of, roughly,
£BOO,OOO, most of which was got in the shallower ground.
Such figures explain why 8., M. and Co. are given a chance
to come into the field.
* * * *
England Consols is a Maldon bird. It had a
little flutter last week on the Melbourne Stock Exchange
on the news that 15dwt. dirt was showing in the bottom
level. Still the flight was not very' high, so when it fell it
did not receive many bruises. If it had gone to the
heights of Mt. Tarrangower, or sailed aloft to emulate
North of England, it might have had its chirrup ended.
As it is, the little fledgling is nestling close to mother
earth. Pending another flight, those who are helping it
along may be asked to particularise how the value of the
stone is estimated. Is it by systematic sampling, or dish
prospects ? On this everything depends.

Talk about the exclusiveness of Trades
Unionism 1 Why, members of the Stock Exchange of
Melbourne cannot take partners now unless they too
become members.
* * • *
The first bucket dredge to work Yackandandah
Creek (Vic.) has started, and local storekeepers shouted
a picnic, and buttered owner Donald Fletcher. Mean-
while workers looked glumly at three men and a couple
of lads doing a shift in place of 14 or 15 men on a barge.
Venture, while always losing some gold, will probably
pay in present position ; but up the creek, which
Fletcher has pegged for a mile or so, the country is all
slate, and buckets will be useless. In such country it
takes the pick and nozzle to search out every color.
Above Fletcher’s lease, Davison has just had 100 acres
surveyed. Lower down, at Allan’s Flat, one of Mrs.
Wallace’s barges has almost worked out. But the widow
has just floated another at Baranduda. Across at Bright
dredges are booming. The Tewksbury Freehold put up
the record of a£2 10s. div. for a seven weeks run. Same
co. is getting in a No. 2 plant, which will be at work in a
few weeks. The Tewksbury has returned 14s. per £1
share in twelve months.
* * » *
The temporary gold boom at Warrandyte
(Vic.), which has revived interest in the adjoining
diggings at Diamond Creek, recalls an old tale of the first
finding of gold on’“ the creek.” Henry and Fred Hurst
were squatting there in theearly days,” when one day
Henry, carting wood from a back paddock, knocked off
the cap of a gold reef and brought home a hatful of speci-
mens. He intended to return next day, but that night
bushranger Burke paid the brothers a visit, and, in an
argument, shot Henry dead.
•*/ * •
J. Bull in past years was at Toora, South
Gippsland, which the Paringa Co. has just tentatively
decided to take up on the strength, it seems, of F. W.
Trude’s report. And Bull was not only there;
he scorched his fingers badly as well. He
spent £20,000 in ■ bringing in water; but he
never got any tin back in divs. Present holders
are sufficiently modest to take all their consideration in
paid-up shares, provided a little drop (£2OOO cash) falls
into their bucket. This is to “cover their outlays
in procuring and investigating the option and
property.” It does not look as though they
have done mnch mining work. Even if they
have, the Paringa directors should think twice.
Trude’s report, anyway, has been read with gasps by Mel-
bourne people. These rub their eyes to think there is so
much wealth at their front door and they not able to see
it. Northe.v, Baudrv, Langford—where are they ?
* * * *
Melbourne solicitor and man-of-affairs Croker
has made terms with Bewick, Moreing & Co. to prospect the
Altona Bay alleged coalfield, near Newport, a Melbourne
suburb. This field was discovered 30 years ago by a local
brewer named Taegtow, and various shindykits have ten-
tatively tapped the alleged seams, which so far have only
yielded a poor quality of brown coal. Hitherto want of
capital to cope with the. water difficulty has prevented
anything like a thorough test.
* * * *
The kind of mining float which hurts mining is
taking place in connection with the New . Bird’s Reef
Co., Bendigo. The line was famous in the old days for
rich yields, and mismanagement is blamed for the fact
that it has been idle for 30 years. Eleven co.’s won
50,0000 z. from the reef, originally found by a man named
Bird. The promoters are taking 5000 shares, fully paid
to 35., and £125 cash, and selling 27,000 shares to the
public at 6d. Thus £5OO will be placed to the credit of
the co. What for? To buy an oil painting of old Mr.
Bird? 'To fill in the shafts and plant lemons? No—to
develop a Bendigo mine ! The farce of it.
*\* * *
Tin has touched a record—£2o3 per ton. For
four years its statistical position has been getting stronger,
and high rates have been foreshadowed; but not £203.
Two years ago the price was £126, and 12 months later it
was £136 ; so the big jump has been in the past year—
£4o within the last four months. Consumers, with im-
mediate orders booked, seem to have been caught in a
trap (the market, which lies in the hollow of the hand of
the Dutch, is one of the trickiest in the world), and any-
thing in the neighborhood of £2OO can only be regarded
as a freak.

Six months ago it was said that Lindesay
Clark, of the Briseis (Tas.), had reported that the Triangle
and other claims held by the ill-fated Ringarooma tin
mines would not suit his co. Six weeks ago the market
heard from London that the properties were to be
bought, and, within six hours after this last statement ha.d'
been confirmed by the directors, the shares were on their
w r ay downhill. It was natural, too, though it may not
suit Bowes Kelly, with his 60,000 shares, or Tom Luxton’s
little brood of chickens. Said Ringarooma blocks are.
chiefly remarkable for their capping of
Clouston tried to wash the red soil away with the pdmp
and race, but it beat him and also beat J. Bull, who
backed him. Briseis takes on the job because it has'
water, and cannot reckon on much of a life unless it
acquires other mines. A good deal of the profits will, go ,
into the Ringarooma tail race, unless the proposition is t
dealt with in a leisurely way. Even with the overburden
off, the wash will have to be elevated, and good dirt is
needed to pay on these conditions. The Pioneer may be
cited, but it has no overburden and the tin goes down
pretty well from the grass roots. Another factor to occa-
sion doubt over the purchase is that the lead widens as it ,
makes away from its source in the Briseis ; hence a doubt
as to whether averages will be high. Anyway, someone
has been shooting shares into the market sluice-box
whenever there has been a bit of a current to carry them
off. The question is, Who?

Just now Charters Towers is scarcely livelier i
than Gympie, which seems almost as dead as Noah.
Think of the tumble—in April, 1905, the mills belted out
£52,764 ;in April, 1906, only £38,546. And, of course, |
cyanide gave less too. For the first four months of this
year and last the field tells this dismal yarn :
. , • 1905. • 1906.
Gold £301,728 £257,915
Dividends ~ £67,785 £46,034
Calls 35,378 51,877
Surplus of dividends .. .. £32,407 >
Excess of calls .. ~ .. ~. £5,843 ,
This month there doesn’t seem as much as one solitary
dividend in sight. One day lately the few' who are left to
pick up a precarious living by buying Long Toms to-day
at 3Jd. and selling them to-morrow at 3|d. got wildly
excited over a report that mineral had been touched by a
bore in Brilliant Stockholm shaft. The “speckers”
lived on it for 24 hours ; then plunk ! fell the stock again ;
the men had made a mistake. Down south nothing
belonging to Charters Towers is dealt in now as a regular
thing, except perhaps Victory ; and the amount of sup-
port that stock gets is surprising under the circum-
stances. It’s the crowd, and not the mine behind the
crowd, that keeps the stock where it is.
* * » *
The path of the Carrington Lion P.C., Charters
Tow'ers, is still’clouded. The rich gold-stone of the sur-
face —and consequently of the early boom crushings—is
replaced at the lower levels by a body of very highly
mineralised stuff, carrying copper, galena, and a little
zinc, which is unfortunate (for amalgamation purposes).
All the same, those at present “ unfortunate ” metals
may save the bacon of the Carrington Lion, which now
looks much more of a concentrating-smelting proposition
than a milling-c Km-amalgamation job. Directors are
evidently nonplussed, and a small parcel of ore intended
for experimental purposes has left for Europe.
* * * *
Why this dead silence concerning the lately-
boomed fabulous wealth in the School Reserve at Charters
Towers? Seems as if the meek and mild reports in circu-
lation at present are the best that the property ever de-
served. The rise going up from Mills’ is to be continued
165 ft. further to connect with the School Reserve’s work-
ings. As it is already 25ft. into the School’s grounds, it
means that there is a patch of 190 ft. from the lowest
workings to the boundary ; 15ft. past that in Mills’, there
is 3ft. of stone.
* • * *
Premier Kidston (Q.), speaking at Cloncurry,
stated that, barring interference by floods, the railway
will be through in 18 months. The performance will
be creditable to the engineers, if this forecast is realised.
Kidston probably takes an optimistic view of the under-
taking, because he sees a reasonable surplus ahead, and
funds will therefore be more quickly available for con-
struction. A continuance of this state of things depends
upon further good rains, and further good rains mean
floods, and floods mean delay. Given a full purse, no
political eruption, and no floods, Kidston’s forecast may
be realised —three large assumptions for Queensland.
* * * *
Latest S. Australian “oilfield” is on the lower
end of Yorke’s Peninsula, where the salt comes from ;
and there’s money behind the venture. “The odor of
gases near the place of discovery is so strong as to
prevent persons approaching within a distance of several
feet,” a local man writes. It sounds like a whale —one
of the very dead whales that the “ Abo.’’-liars have been
writing about lately. They propose to bore, to see,
presumably, if the whale will spout.
'* * * *
Jim Wilkinson, one of the earliest battlers at
Silverton (N.S.W.), and, later, on the Murchison (W.A.j,
recently returned from Java, where he went hunting for
copper. Dutch authorities treated him well, and gave
him a concession of some likely-looking country ; but,
although he found a couple of lodes, they were too small
for profit, and he had to let them slide. He put in a few
weeks in mid-Java, looking down the craters of volcanoes,
some of them lively, and spent altogether a good time.
Also he visited Dutch Borneo, which he describes as rich
in coal, oil, iron, copper, tin, and gold—as fine a country
as any prospector could wish for. But the Mynheers in
possession offer no encouragement to syndicates. He
went up to Sian>, and penetrated to a native state some
80 miles from the coast; but, although the Sultan
received him kindly enough, his Maj. w'as not giving any-
thing away in the shape of concessions. Any mining
that was to be done he could do himself. So J. J.
Wilkinson returned empty-handed. ■

• * * *
The Phoenix Assurance Co., Ltd., of London,
had, on Dec. 31st last, assets amounting to £2,091,823,
against which there were liabilities of £154,037, leaving
£1,937,793 net. There is for the protection of policy-
holders also an uncalled capita* of £2,419,920. Last year
the co. made a profit of £269,588, of which £94,107 was
divided, or at the rate of 35 per cent. ; the profit was
equal to just over 100 per cent. The losses were 48'5 per
cent, of the net premiums ; the expenses (exclusive of
taxes) were 34’5 per cent, of net premiums. This shows
17 per cent, of the premiums available for profits (and
taxes) as against B’B per cent, in the New Zealand last
year, 11 "9 per cent, in the South British, 11-9 per cent, in
the United (Sydney). The Phoenix’s expense rate is
rather above, and its loss rate rather below, the Austral-
asian average.
* * * «
A Westralian who dealt largely in bark lately
found himself with about 10,000 tons over market re-
quirements. He had paid all his money away in wages
to the bark-strippers, and was hard up. A German bark-
shipping firm, being asked to make an advance, referred
him to a local solicitor, who set about raising an advance
of £ll,OOO, or something like a fourth of the actual value
of the bark at that time. A bill of sale was drawn up and
registered, but not so a collateral agreement, under which
Gent One was to receive £5 ss. per day for his services dur-
ing the whole periqd he had the matter in hand, also
travelling expenses, £450 commission, and a bonus of
£4OOO. Altogether Gent One provided that he should
receive £5383 as his first preference claim, and other
charges brought up the amount to be deducted from the
loan of £ll,OOO to £5691. Gent One let the thing go ,to
the Supreme Court, too ; and, though the agreement was
fired out there, the sale was ordered to stand.
During its last municipal year the Glasgow
(municipally-owned) tram receipts were £764,760 ; work-
ing expenses, £392,601 ; net receipts, £372,159; interest
charges and rents, £53,679 ; repayment charges, £46,920;
surplus, £271,590; depreciation and reserve fund
£246,590 ; contribution to rate fund, £25,000. From ’7i
to ’94 private enterprise ran Glasgow’s trams. It worked
its employees 14 hours a day for an average wage of 225. a
week ;it charged the public an average fare of - 89 d. per
mile, and it contributed £63,628 to the common good in
23 years. Then the Corporation took charge. It reduced
the employees’ hours to 10 per day and raised their wages
to an average of 275. per week, with two free uniforms
annually. It reduced the fares to an average "46d. per
mile, and in the last .three years it has paid nearly as
much to the common good as the private co. did in 23
viz., £62,500.
** f *
The Maffra (Vie.) beet sugar proposition, in
which the State sank £62,000 and the co. £31,000, is being
revived. The Bent Government will send canvassers
through the district to see how much beet the sturdy, in-
dependent farmers will guarantee, will provide experts to
study the question of manufacture, will pamper the farmers
with big railway concessions on the carriage of the roots to
the factory, and—though careful to give private “enter-
prise ” (!) the first chance —will, if necessary, run the
factory itself. And, so far, none of the Gippsland farmers
are howling about the horrors of Socialism.
* * * *
“ O’T.” from north-west Tasmania: Many of
the tin-concerns here are surely never intended to pay,
and are mere excuses for rooking the public either on
promotion or on the market. Take a typical instance.
Manager, Victorian engineer, done no mining : never saw
tin before, and doesn’t know it now. Blacksmith, Victorian
engine-driver, who never laid a pick in'ftis life ; gets 12s.
a day. Local blacksmith, who served his time, employed
on same job as miner at 7s. 6d. Carpenter, Victorian
tomahawk expert, 12s. Assistant carpenter, Victorian
miner, never tackled joinery. Foreman of road party,
Victorian ferry-boat stoker, Bs. As usual the locals only
are “rated”—that is, “paid what they are worth”*;
supremely fuhny in the circumstances! No “local”
knows what he is going to get until pay-day comes, when
he is handed as little as may be. The above motley com-
pany is building a dam that one wholesome night’s rain
would sweep into the Pacific if it had any catchment or
other supply, which it hasn’t. So it fairly balances the
mine, which equally lacks any permanent tin.
* * * *
Aerial tramways or conveyors undoubtedly
afford the cheapest means of transport, especially when
they are worked under a system which is entirely
automatic. The largest manufacturers in the world in
this particular line are Adolf Bleichert and Co., of London,
represented in Australia by W. and J. Lempriere, of Mel-
bourne. Delivering ore automatically from the mine to
the mill over almost any distance at a cost of about one
penny per ton, is far ahead of all other means of transport.
Automatic systems are in vogue for the cheap discharge
of tailings. Automatic weighing and counting of loaded
cars is also assured under Bleichert’s system, thereby,
nothing is left to assumption or imagination.*

It is interesting to note that the “Hornsby”
patent safety oil engine has sustained its world-wide
reputation for reliability and economy. During the past
year hundreds of squatters, recognising their adaptability,
have been using them for their shearing and general
motive power, and as they are specially suitable for
mining, electric lighting, pumping, etc., mine managers
have shown their appreciation by installing them on some
of the leading mines. The latest type of engine will run
at a cost of less than a farthing per brake horse power per
hour, and are manufactured by the well-known
“ Upright ” Water Tube Boiler firm R. Hornsby and Sons,
Ltd., Hay-street, Sydney and Spencer-street, Melbourne.*

* * * *
Two sets of improved plunger pumps, Cornish
type, are at present being constructed for the “ keystone ”
shaft of the Loddon Valley Goldfields (Victoria) to the
order of Bewick, Moreing and Co. Each set 'will consist
of two lifts of 250 feet, the total lift being 500 feet. The
plungers are 26in. in diameter and 10ft. stroke and the
discharge from the two sets will be 6,000,000 gallons per
day. The valve boxes are made in separate pieces instead
of forming part of one H piece, and the valves are of the
compound flap type. The total weight of the two sets of
pumps is 150 tons. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company has this work in hand. This company has
constructed and erected most of the big volume deep
alluvial pumping plants now working in Victoria.*

The Zinc Corporation has taken careful advice
in connection with the large plant required at Broken
Hill, and has ordered Babcock and Wilcox water-tube
boilers with superheaters and mechanical stokers. This
order has been followed by the great firm of mining
engineers, Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and Co., placing
orders with the same boiler company, in connection with
the South Blocks, also at the Hill. These instances
further prove the assertion that, wherever economy in
fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is •
installed. This company stock boilers at “Haymarket,”
Sydney.*

The strength, lightness* durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,-
and every discriDtion of cast or wrought iron work.* 1

Aboriginalities.
“Kulpara”: “C.X.G.” (5/4/’O6) com-
. plains of the heat and discomforts of Farina
‘ (S.A.). Let me invite him to Oodnadatta, a few
hundred miles further on, and also to some of the
cattle stations north of Oodnadatta. This year
we have only had a few points of rain, and north,
right through to the Territory, is in an awful
state. Along the Finke cattle are dying in
hundreds, and not a bit of feed is to be seen.
Even the spinifex is dead, and it takes a very
severe drought to kill it off. And how do the
residents fare ? With only a fortnightly train
(which takes three days to do the journey), and
no refrigerating car attached, and the ther-
mometer dancing a can-can between 120 and 125
in the shade, how can we get necessaries, let alone
luxuries ? No butter, eggs, fruit or vegetables are
to be seen, and only about half a cup of milk per
day. Certainly there was a market garden in
Ood., but owing to the waterholes drying, it had
to close, ior months we have been existing on
bread and salt beef, washed down with tea.

branding, referred to by “ Elmore ” (B. 3/5/’O6).
1 wo Maorilanders (Messrs. I>e Lisle and Luttrell)
nave a, painless branding composition on the
market, which they claim to be permanent and
oi° l ?ir OU& to , tbe b ' des - I branded four cows
about three months ago, and the result is a clean,
sharp brand. The composition is like tar to look
at, and is pressed firmly on a dipped surface
a r n T ery fine brand - (I used hoop-iron on
edge.) lhere seems to be some depilatory in the

makers claim that the cost is under £d. per heaxl,

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ There ain’t no place like it on earth ! *’
Bill made the statement in a bar
Up Queensland way. “It gave me birth,
And it’s Victorian ; there yew are 1
“ Yous tork of Sydney and of Perth,
How very rapidly they grew 1 ”
Here Bill nigh choked himself with mirth.
“ Well, yew should just see Bungaloo !
‘ And spread itself 1 Here, strike me puce,
A mushroom isn’t in the race,
It grows so quick—but, wot’s the use ?
Yew want to go and see the place.”
I know it well,” a stranger said,
And meditatively did spit;
“ 1 knew a bloke down there who’s dead,
And I don’t think too much of it."
“Not much 1" cried Bill. “Oh, paint me green,
With crossbars pink and spots of blue 1
How long is it since yew have been
In sight of distant Bungaloo ? ”
“ Three weeks 1 ” the stranger said. Cried Bill:
■“ What I Strike me strawb’rv as a cow,
H’yer think the place is like that still ?
Yew just go back and see it now ! ”

“Drifter”: “Mark” (B. 12/4/06) has
been struck by the total absence of bird life on
the N.S.W. Blue Mountains. “Mark” must
have turned himself loose among the scenery
during the drowse of noonday, when the feathered
songster is digesting the worm during a siesta amid
the foliage. Was camped in the Valley of the
Waters near Wentworth Falls recently, and was
awakened every morning by a full orchestra of
birdsong. Gaudy rosellas, king _ parrots, a hun-
dred varieties of honey birds amid the blossoms,
coach whips chasing through the bracken, wild
pigeons, thrushes, magpies, and lyre-birds filled
the hush with chatter, coo and whistling melody
as the sun lit up the dim valley. But as the day
advanced, the song of the birds dwindled gradu-
ally away till only the occasional note of the
coachwhip or the caw of the black magpie sounded
from the bosom of the bush.

“Janor” (whose letter cost 2d. for de-
ficient postage): In reply to “Musselburgh’s”
inquiry as to the truth of the legend that if a
snake is killed his mate will be found, 24 hours
afterwards, at that particular spot (8., 26/4/’O6),
I say “Rats !” (Pity he didn’t say it in a suffi-
ciently stamped letter.) During the past summe r
I travelled a bush track twice daily for several
months, during which time my average bag was
seven snakes weekly (if “Janor” had sent in
particulars of these snakes as they came to hand
the deficient postage would have been 14d.), but
I did not kill two in any one particular spot.
(Perhaps the other one was away buying stamps.)
The result of many years’ observation leads me to
the conclusion that snakes are always on their
own; there “ain’t no mate.” If they marry, as
“ Musselburgh ” (whose letter was sufficiently
stamped) suggests, why the fierce bouts of fisti-
cuffs, I have many a time refereed—with a waddy
—between two males over a lady ? Certainly one
of the combatants may have been a co-re., though
evidence is not forthcoming to support that
theory. Anyhow, “Musselburgh” is rightabout
their fondness for milk. They prefer it to music.
For instance, a black snake entered my dining-
room a while back, and crawled right over the
Jew’s harp, which was lying on the floor, without
any attempt to play upon it. But he rushed an
unopened tin of milk. When I killed him he was
busy opening the tin, which he held firmly in a
coil of his tail. He used the hole in his face to
propel the tin-opener.

“ Quan”: If no minimum number of pubs,
is fixed for the prize over-pubbed township compe-
tition, and have-beens are not disqualified, I
hereby enter a sure winner. It’s name is Bruns-
wick Heads (North Coast, .N.S.W.). When I
struck it in 1895 its population was only 39, and
it had two substantially-built hotels —not shanties.
Within a radius the population was only
51. I was the one, so can vouch for the accuracy
of my allegations. It was the most un-Australian
township I ever struck. Of the 15 men living
there, only five were Australians. The archi-
tecture consisted of a School of Arts, a brick
public school, police station, posb office, pilot
station, two stores, one ex-store (two-storeyed),
three private residences (one unoccupied), a ferry-
man’s hut, a goods shed, and the two pubs afore-
mentioned.
• • * *
“ Can Jeen ” paints a gloomy picture :
I am just home from a country concert. A choir of
children were “ singing ” glees, led by the schoolmaster
and accompanied by his wife. The schoolmaster was a
lanfcern-jawed man with black side-whiskers and eye-
glasses. Bread-and-butter dinners were writ large on his
countenance. There were four of his children present,
and the mother was wearing a much-faded, loose, three-
quarter coat. The youngest child (about a year old) sat
on mother’s lap, pasty and peevish, and not to be satis-
fied even by the proffered bottle. A struggling poverty
could be seen in the coarse stockings, bad boots, shabby
clothes, and the ill-fed hungry looks of the animated pegs
they hung on. And then I looked at the little choir, his
scholars. They were a mixed lot —the usual country
school. One or two had good looks; one or two were
rather pert or smart; none of them showed that bright-
ness of countenance which is the index to an expanding
mind suitably fed.
And how could they show it ? Their teacher goes to
school in the morning. The baby has had a restless
night. The mother, in delicate health, wants some held
in the house. He lights the fire and tidies up, and sees
the children get their breakfast —tea, bread and butter,
and an occasional egg. He is tired and peevish ; the
work is an irksome grind. Dinner is a bit of corned beef,
bread and butter (the butcher only comes once a week,
and potatoes are dear). The afternoon is hot, the
children fidgetty. What a relief when they can be
dismissed ! Even for those few hours in the day they are
starved of any refining influences, and refined thoughts
don’t come to empty stomachs and worried brains.
The holidays come, but the funds don’t run to a trip;
the only thing is to “ mooch ” the time away till the
grind comes round again. The Department pays this man,
perhaps, £2 10s. a week, and expects—or does it really
expect?—an intellectual influence on the youth of the
country in return. What do they get? Instruction,
perhaps, but no education, from a servile drudge who
sells his soul for a pittance.
The remedy? Pear there isn’t one, unless we euthana-
tise the whole Department—free it from the bonds of the
mercenary book-compiler and the ambidextral crank, and
get men in their stead who understand that their business
is the efficient education of our children, and who mean
to see it done. And that means, right to begin with—
money.

“ Wurrym ” : Ever hear of a night- well ?
Thirty or forty miles east of Wagin, on the
Southern railway of W.A., there is a hill, on top
of which is a small cavern. . Part of the floor of
the cavern is water-worn into a basin holding
about 50 gallons. ' A little after sundown a tiny
thread of water starts trickling from an inlet in
the wall of the cave, and continues till shortly be-
fore sunrise. During the day not a drop flows.
The water is of crystal clearness, and as pure as
Westralia’s Upper House. The intermittent
spring is no novelty; but why this particular
spring should only flow at night-time—it is shown
on the Government plans as “ The Nightwell ”
—has never been explained, or if it has I happened
to be away collecting a bad debt at the time.
“ Quidam ” : Re picture “ The Filial Aus-
tralian ” (8., Feb. 22/’O6). I was once at a race
meeting at Hill End, and saw an Australian youth
and his father there, the latter much under the
influence. The youth attended the race ball that
night, but his progenitor did not return till next
afternoon, when he was brought home, gently
cursing at rheumatism and a large bump on his
skull, in a spring cart, by a friend who found him
lying on the deserted racecourse. Pressed for ex-
planations, the fond son said that when he was
piloting his parent from the course the old person
fell violently against a stump with his “ napper.”
“ I thought he was stiff right enough, so I went
through his pockets and went to the darnce with
the beans.” The “old man” was laid up for a
week, but his bad language didn’t appear to be
specially directed against his enterprising off-
spring. He seemed to take the offspring as a
matter of course.
“Kulfara”: Guess “Janor” (B. 5/4/’O6)
makes a bloomer, or else the “ perforated
mounds” (white-ants nests) in his district must
be different from those up here (Central Aus-
tralia). Here we have them from 2ft. to 14ft.
high, and on going into camp, if wood is scarce,
they are very handy. You scoop out a hole from
one side at the bottom to the centre, and one from
the top down to meet it, making a kind of flue.
Then put your quart pot in the top hole, a few
sticks in the bottom, and in no time it’s like a
baker’s oven. On a cold night you camp fairly
close to it, and it will keep you warm all night.
It is marvellous how it retains its heat. The
only objection is that the smoke from the mound
is very unpleasant if it gets in the boiling water.
Once, when hard-up for wood, baked a damper in
the “ coals ” from the mound, but it tasted as if it
had been cooked in a cow-dung fire.

“Two Seas”: Be “T.G.F.’s” inquiry
(B. 3/5/’O6) as to why sheep at a distance some-
times look like bullocks “on a plain out Mores
way.” It isn’t necessary to go up there to witness
that phenomenon. Most sheep musterers have
observed the same tiling—particularly in the early
morning when sheep are standing on a ridge out-
lined against the sky. Some peculiar atmospheric
condition is the cause of the apparent enlargement
of the jumbucks. Re “ T.G.F.’s” second query,
as to why sheep “ half-a-mile away in an opposite
direction are decreased to the size of rabbits,” I
venture to suggest that he was looking at them
through the wrong end of his field glasses. As to.
the third query, “Why black lettering, three,
inches in height, can be read easily at a mile and
a-half with the naked eye ? ” I presume that the
letters read “Free Drinks.” That legend can be
seen a very long way off.
THE OLD SOU’-WESTERN WAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in the careless days
Of the old sou’-western ways
That we fell an easy prey to Beauty’s charms,
Flitted lightly here and there,
Dallied mostly everywhere,
Sipping nectar from the blossoms on the farms.
We had scores and scores of girls
(Tan and freckles ’neath their curls),
And they loved us for our trouble, more or less,
Ere we went in twos and threes,
’Cross the shrieking, salty seas,
Or along the Western track in loneliness.
But ah, often have I prayed
To the Fates that those who stayed
Far behind would share the same unmarred delights ;
The sweet old kitchen talks,
And the tranquil star-lit walks
Thro’ the bushland lone andlistening o’ nights.
And in days when discontent
With a thousand cares is blent,
Ah, I often fall to sadly thinking how
I might have settled down,
Far from city or from town,
Tending sheep or plodding on behind the plough.
How I might have trucked the wool,
Reaped the harvest bountiful,
And kept adding to my acres every year ;
How I might have been (but missed !)
Now a land monopolist,
In the place of writing yarns and verses here 1
But perhaps I will come back
When that lonely bridle track
Is macadamised and labelled up-to-date ;
When the dreamed-of bridge is built
’Cross the swamp of sand and silt,
And the track is grassed or gravelled to the gate !
Yes, perchance we’ll all return
When our hopes have ceased to burn—
In the drab and dreary twilight of our lives ;
And we’ll sadly, sadly find
That the girls we left behind
Are but memories—or other fellows’ wives.
N.S.W.
GILROONET.
TRIOLET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young Autumn comes across the hill,
A-stepping in his robes of red ;
With boisterous winds to do his will,
Young Autumn comes across the hill,
And leaves of gold his coffers fill,
A-dropped by Summer as she fled.
Young Autumn comes across the hill,
A-stepping in his robes of red.
S. A.
ALICE GRANT ROSMAN.

Here behold a Right-thinking Fat-
man, his eye in a fine frenzy rolling,
tearing a copy of The Bulletin to. tatters
and dancing on the fragments.

with the proper spirit.
