THE IRREPRESSIBLE.
The Bulletin had a dream the other night. It dreamed that
the house was on fire and Bruce Smith was being rescued
with difficulty from a top-window. Half-way down the
fire-escape he seized his opportunity and stopped ito.
make a few remarks to the cirowd.about Empire Day.
' ‘ ■' .. ‘ ■-■ ' : 'tv p ’’
In Asiatic quarters the enthusiasm is
quite spontaneous. “Hullabl (also
Banzai!) our Empire I ”
Now, children, this is Empire Day. And you are here to celebrate the
glorious Empire. You will all have a bun and a bottle of ginger-beer. First you .
will sing “ Rule Britannia,” and give three cheers for Me.
- This isn’t a criminal in a fit. It is
only a suburban alderman rehearsing
his empire Day speech in the back yard
before he discharges it at the miserable
school kiddies.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Reported from England that Joseph
Chamberlain’s health is failing. He is 70, and the
weight of his political sins and his orchid begin to
drag his head forward and downward.
John Nankeville Thomas, aged 77 years,
who died in the Alfred Hospital, Melbourne, and
whose remains were cremated at his own wish,
had been for 22 years in the employ of an under-
taker. He knew.
Some birthdays : Speaker Holder was 56
on May 12; Chief Justice Madden was 62 on May
16; Henniker Heaton will be 58 on May 18:
Missionary J. G. Paton, 82 on May 24.
Macnamara, one of the earliest battlers of
the N.S. W. political labor movement, is now a
victim to phthisis, and in very poor circumstances.
.The Bulletin will gladly forward to him any
help that old friends may care to offer.
Captain Neitenstein, formerly in charge of
the Sobraon, then Comptroller-General of Prisons,
and finally now appointed to the Public Service
Board, sets the Service thinking. First he was
engaged in reforming those who had taken the
first step towards, becoming criminals; next lie
took the proved criminals in hand; now he’s got
his eye on the Public Service.
It is hard to understand why the Westra-
lian Cabinet found it necessary to go to England
for a judge. The experience of the Eastern States
puts the Australian-trained man right on top of
the imported law-twister. On the Vic. bench
for example. Madden, Hodges, Hood and Cussen
are all Melb. University men—like every leader
of the Vie. bar, except Purves.
Stated in the press that Senator Staniforth
Smith may be appointed Governor of Australian
Papua. The appointment, on the face of it, would
be an excellent one,, as Mr. Smith seems to know
more of the Possession than any other Australian
now in the public eye. Certainly some change
the present London-Johnny administration
should be made ; and that immediately.
THE CRY OF THE PATIENT.
Majorities are often wrong... The country is ruled
by a minority.—ARGUS.
"Poor soul! Raving again."
“ Yes, goes on like that orl day."
Edward Spicer, who died this week in
Adelaide in his 90th year, was reputedly the
richest ready-money magnate in the State. He
was a good Methodist, and knew as well howto
stmk to his cash as to make it. His grandson
dmd on the same day, and they were buried
together in the same vault. Hard to wait so lone
and then to miss the big bequest left him in the
old man s will.
Dead in Adelaide, aged 82, Miss M.
Aldersey, a veteran educationist, who in oast
years taught a large number of S. A.’s youth and
beauty to spell cat ’ and other things. A decade
or so ago Miss Aldersey’s school at M'Laren Yale
was the foremost female cramming institution in
South Oss, and past pupils tell that the old lady
was the most delightful and popular school-m's-
tress ever invented.
• I , llave to report that I was last
night at Flinders-street railway station (Mel-
bourne), and saw T. Bent running for the train
with two Frankfort sausages and a roll. Later I
saw him consuming the provender in a dimly-lit
second-class carriage. I like Tommy. He is off-
hand, democratic (in many things), and devoid of
trill. He does twice as much good work as Car-
ruthers, yet who ever saw Joey rushing for a train
with one bread and two meats in his hand, or con-
suming a pound of corned beef in a public vehicle ?
Died, at Barcom Glen, Sydney, aged 71
years, T. J. West, city alderman, who was born
° p ~ t Pt,r Bpot where he died. He was the son of
Übed West, who was born in Pitt-street, on the
site of Hoffnung’s warehouse, in 1807. Obed’s
father, Thomas West, arrived in 1800, free, and
was the first man in Australia to start a’mill
worked by water power. Grandfather West
seems to have been the only one in the family who
put up anything of a record.
Frank T. Bullen sold the “ Cruise of the
Cachelot” (which paid the publishers hand-
somely) for £5O, though later on the publishers
voluntarily increased this to £250, Bullen left
home at the age of nine to “find something to
eat. ; kept a stiff upper lip through many
vicissitudes, which included jobs at billiard-
marking and scraping potatoes ; educated himself,
with the Bible, Shakspeare and Milton for text-
books ; and proudly looks back upon a day when
he was “converted.”
Keith Ramsay, who died in Dunedin on
May 4, aged 62, belonged to a mysterious class of
Scotchmen very common in that town. He had a
little murky office like a two-roomed blot down in
the neighborhood of Crawford-street. He started
as an agent when he was 21, and kept on agenting
all his days. He had something to do with coal,
and his premises looked it. He was connected
with two or three small steamers which seemed to
have very little moans of support—they mostly
lingered about looking as dusty as the office. Yet
Keith Ramsay, son of a hard Perthshire member
of the Presbyterian persuasion, was piling up
money all the time, and had a hand in almost
every enterprise that was worth having. He
leaves much siller, a family of nine, and a square-
built Presbyterian reputation.
Died, last week, Henry Maxted, one of
a Sydney family which used to thrive on its
fellow-creatures misfortunes. Sydney Maxted
was for years Director of the Government chari-
ties and asylums. Edward Maxted was manager
of the Sydney Benevolent Asylum, and Henry
now deceased, was formerly head of the Bank-
ru P tcy Court. Poverty, old age, and insolvency
kept the Maxteds afloat. They were all clever
writers, and Henry used to more than dabble in
journalism.
One Rankin, who was mentioned rather
gloomily in Sydney Bankruptcy Court last week,
seems to be a person who is sanguine even in his
insolvency. _ He has failed twice. The first time
he valued Ins assets at £950 135., but all that the
assignee managed to fish out of the troubled
waters was 2s. 3d. On his second failure he valued
his assets at £77,040 17s. 9d., and the assignee
contrived to rescue £llO 12s. Id. in actual cash.
® court, after deep consideration, found a ver-
dict that Rankin was an incurable optimist.
Profit Dowie, when he heard, in the place
of exile where he had gone to recuperate, that
Zion was slipping, made a final effort to assert his
divine mission, and telegraphed about how he had
just healed himself of ten or twelve incurable
diseases:—
Overseer-in-charge, Zion City, Illinois.—Psalm Ninety-
one. Week of wondrous blessing'. Details reserved for
letter. Serious symptoms suddenly appeared Lord’s Day
last. Prayer for guidance offered and direction sought.
Liven. Obedience brought immediate blessing. Many
open sores had appeared. Legs and feet were rapidly
decaying. Could not walk a step. To-night not an open
sore on my body. My feet and legs are becoming nor-
mal. Have just walked around my verandah, a distance
of two hundred twenty-five feet. Restoration being
realised. I praise God for methods He has employed, and
for the joy and peace imparted. I sleep, eat, and
assimilate food, and am on the way to perfect health.
My joy at the prospect of once more being among you in
strength to engage in the extension of the Kingdom of
God throughout the world is intense. Love to Zion
everywhere. Mizpah. First Apostle.
But Zion was tired, disgusted, and broke, and if
it had been told that Dowie had just cured him-
self of two wooden legs and an amputated head,
it wouldn’t have cared.
Henry Tennent Donaldson, who was killed
through slipping down the lift-well at the Sydney
Gr.P.O., had several close
calls before the last one.
Once the overhead tram-
wire snapped and fell
around him like a serpent,
dragging him from the
top of a ’bus to the
ground. Had it been the
“live” end Donaldson
would have been an elec-
trocuted man. On another
occasion, when travelling
from Bathurst to Sydney
in the mail-train, the door
against which he was lean-
ing flew open, and he went
in great haste down an
embankment. Some time
afterwards he tried to
board a tram-car which
got off the mark too sud-
denly, and he was bounced
off the wood blocks in a
violent fashion. It was
through changing his mind
that he met his death.
He usually ascended to
his office by the stairway.
The day when his call
came he was seen to climb several steps, then,
noticing the elevator waiting invitingly on the
ground floor, he returned and stepped in. Ten
seconds later it happened.
The deceased James Ebenezer Tonkin,
M.L.A. for East Macquarie (N.S.W.) in days
gone by, had a remarkably ingenious grand-
father. The old man’s name is associated with
early efforts to raise sunken ships. His apparatus
was adopted by the Admiralty when the Royal
George went down, with Kempenfeldt and 600
persons, off Spithead, in 1782. The salvage of
the Abergavenny, an East Indiaman, was another
great feat. The apparatus on which Grandfather
Tonkin spent his life was not only intended to
save cargoes, but to save sunken vessels bodily,
and was reckoned a most ingenious device in a
period when ships were smaller than they are
now. Grandson Tonkin died after a weary ill-
ness at Forest Lodge, Sydney, aged 71.

lecturing under R. S. Smythe's auspices in
Sydney.

TWO SAMPLES.
JOHNNIE : “ Been to the circus yet!"
BOUND HEBE: “ No , we have circus enough here all day long-mules, donkeys, and and amount
of ciowns. You never saw such a variety show as this is.”
Mr. Arthur Hardy, the oldest member of
the Bar in South Australia, passed into his 91st
year on Thursday. He still attends business daily,
and is as keen after the 6s. Bd. as any of the
young members of the profession.
Ebenezer Thorne, who said “Hear, hear,”
or an endorsement to that effect, in regard to the
statements made in London Daily Mail about
the degradation and immorality of Australian
youngsters, is just remembered, and no more, in
Queensland. He claimed to be a journalist, and
used to attend temperance meetings, where he
would fire interjections at the speakers. Publicly
speaking, he was of no account, and it astonishes
Bananalanders that the cable-man should bother
about sending us his alleged opinions. The
views of any London bargee on current politics
would be just as interesting to the people who
dimly recollect Ebenezer.
Victorian Rep. Crouch’s unpopularity in
“officah circles” seems to be of long standing,
mainly owing to the fact that he always stuck up
for the men, and particularly so for his Queens-
cliff constituents. The recent camp episode
brings to recollection the famous “ fix bayonets ”
order, on the gory battlefield of Albert Park. On
that occasion, Crouch’s company of the 6th (then
the 2nd) Vic. Infantry Regiment was charged by
cavalry, who were evidently anxious to show
“Hastiness” towards their foot comrades, by
riding almost on top of them. Crouch stopped
the circus with the order to fix bayonets.
Out of the students at the Edinburgh
University ; this year 18 per cent, were
“colonials”; yet in the science section they
gained 13 out of the 20 medals or 65 per cent, of
the honors. Marshall Allan, of Brisbane, secured
a medal in practical chemistry, amongst 400
students. Last summer session he got honors in
botany and zoology : and has now excelled in
anatomy, sixth on a list of 250; while he gained
first-class honors in physics. A Maorilander led
in this section with 87 per cent, of marks, with
Allan, of Brisbane. (84 per cent.) next, and Miss
McLeod, also of Brisbane, third.
If the volcanic Voliva succeeds in blowing
off sufficient denunciation to smother Dowie’s
protests, and drives the whiskered seer out of
Zion, it will only be a case of paying Profit ’Lijah
in his own coin. From the beginning of his
ministerial career, Dowie had a yearning to jump
the other fellow's claim if the reef looked at all
payable. In Melbourne he first became notorious
through his performance in undermining the Rev.
C. M. Cherbury, while the latter was on a health
trip.. Dowie was temporarily filling Cherbury’s
pulpit in the Collingwood Congregational Church,
facing the back of Wren’s tote premises. When
he failed in his attempt to appropriate the church,
• ?" lir } exe d a great part of the congregation, and
with them started a kind of miniature Salv’army
of his own. The fallen Elijah can hardly expect
sympathy, now that Voliva has forcibly succeeded
to the mantle and the tail feathers of the pro-
phetic billet.

Barries, Miner (Broken Hill) suggests
that should the local S.M., (who is regarded as an
Outback Apostle of Peace) desire to embellish his
notepaper with a coat-of-arms, an appropriate
design would be two clasped hands holding an
olive-branch, surmounted by the words, “ Can’t
this matter be settled privately?” Even the
local lawyers like to lose fees rather than disturb
the calm of the sanctum where the holy man urges
that the matter should be fixed up out of court.
And he takes as well as gives the advice. He and
the Miner “settled privately ” a little disagree-
ment they had over an allegedly libellous letter
recently.
Recently died, David Reid, one of the
pioneers of Riverina (N.S.W., at present). He
came to Sydney in 1823 with his father, Dr.
Reid, R.N., who, having delivered a consignment
of convicts in more or less good order and condi-
tion, settled near Goulburn. David was one of
the first overlanders, and helped to take 500
cattle to Port Phillip in 1838, what time the site
of Albury was part of Mungabareena station,
owned by C. H. Ebden, afterwards a Victorian
treasurer. Reid’s first station was Carrariagan-
gße, on the Ovens River, above Wangaratta
(Vic.), the property including the sites of the pre-
sent townships of Tarrawingee, Reid’s Creek and
Beechworth. In 1844 he acquired Murramur-
rangbong and Yackandandah, and on the latter
property established the first flour mill in those
parts. Later on he owned Barnawartha run. Then
i a iu Vic. Assembly as member for
the Murray. Afterwards he had squattages on
the Lachlan (N.S.W.) and Warrego, which seem
to have panned out none too well and when Jock
Robertson’s Land Act came along he took up a
selection and hung on to it till he passed over.
It is calculated that the names of the properties
he owned at different times ran to 100 syllables.
“ Murramurrangbong ” was a good instalment in
itself.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 15, 1906.
My Dear Arini,—
Australian women abroad have got the Falling
Sickness very badly. They want to fall down
before Royalty, but they call the disease Presen-
tation at Court. It should be Prostration at
Court. Wives of the Agents-General and influen-
tial women in the Australian colony are beginning
to feel embarrassed and to hedge when asked
to sponsor their countrywomen for the Grand
Curtsey. Curiously enough the affair doesn’t do
them any good in their own country when they
return to it. If they don’t already know the
Best Butter here, the 8.8. doesn’t hasten to melt
before them. And their friends are not a bit im-
pressed—only curious about their dresses, and how
they felt, and if the Queen really looks like her
pictures. The two Misses M‘Micking, of a
squatter family on the Victorian side, are to be at
the next Drawing Room, but they needn’t get hot
looking for sponsors, as they have near 1 relations
with names in modern Holy Writ. Which, as
everyone knows, is “ Whitaker’s Peerage. ”
, The reception given to Grace Palotta bv the'
Willoughby-Ward-Geach trinity, at the Australia,
was a great fur show. Every second woman had
ermines or Russian sables. Miss Ben-Yussuf
who is about to lecture on. Japan, was half hidden
in mongoose—a short bluish grey fur. Madame
Degen’s ’possum furs looked very possumus. A girl
who determined to do something desperate had
put a sable necklet with a tail around her simple
sailor hat. Mr. Willoughby’s bride was present-
tall and fair, with a conventionally-good, fashion-
plate figure, and wearing a Princess robe of
pink cloth. The debutante daughter of E. P.
Simpson, a Frenchy-looking girl, carefully
finished off as with a fine drawing-pen, was the
smartest-looking youngster present. Grace herself
did her hand-shaking in a black chiffon velvet-
corselet skirt and white chiffon bolero with wide
gold embroidery. 1 *'**
At the last hour Premier * Seddon and daughter
Mary changed their minds, and delayed their
departure from Maoriland. They .will really and
truly come to-morrow (Wednesday) provided the
eternal sea does not bluff them in the Warri-
moo. A lot of Maorilanders will wallow
down outside the Heads just to let the long-
|»ckled Premier know that his language is spoken
Collections for the memorial—a cot in the
Children s Hospital— to the late Lady Rawson
have only just started, yet over £lOO has been
subscribed. The sum required is £lOOO. It is a
good form in which to honor the memory of a
much-liked woman. I
• Mr o Emanuel, the cattle king of, Westralia,
is in Sydney. If I told you how many billions of
acres he possesses—l suppose it is billions, but I
was always loose on figures, and still do compound
subtraction on the thumb of my left hand—or the
number of his bellowers, you wouldn’t believe me.
What is worse, I probably wouldn’t believe
myself, for I have quite forgotten. It was for
his house at Perth that Sid Long painted
three overmantels—“A Bush Fire,” “Flamingoes
xt . Sunrise,” and “Cockatoos at Sunset.” Talking
?f Bong—that clever artist has started classes
tor drawing and painting from still, life, the
antique, and the model. So now, any day you
may run against Sid guiding High School girls in
the flutter stage between short and long frocks to
some landscape patch suitable for transference to
canvas.
Late millionaire Peter Russell, who did so much
for our University, is having a bronze statue of
himself wrought by Mackennal. Correctly speak-
ing, it is his widow who has ordered it to keep his
memory brown at the Uni. Mrs. Russell, a lass

It was a beautifully combed audience. Never
before did I.see so many artistic back combs
under, one roof. Diamond combs, black enamel
comlA with a tracery of dead gold, gold combs
with brilliants, and one of tortoiseshell with the
name of “ Carrie ” done in peridots. Fancy wear-
ing your name m your.back hair ! Talking of back
nair: Miss Linda Byron Moore’s hair (she is a
Melbournian) made picturesque the box she was
in. It was an airily-coiffed pile of golden fairness,
and set the circle asking at interval time, “ Who’s
the Girl with the Hair?” So many women do
their hair to look like a chaplet of distended
sausages.
A parson lias been saying that there is a great
revival of religion in Berlin. All owing to the
Empress, who “dearly loves to build a church,
give it a bible and choose the text for the dedica-
tion sermon.” So piety is fashionable in Berlin.
One may imagine the Imperial Bill’s moustache
brushing the doorposts as he goes to listen to the
dedication sermon which he could have preached
so much better. Louis XIV. used to have pious
intervals when he was about 40—in later years he
took to piety as a steady job—and whilst they
lasted the court had a sad, sour time, dressed
drably, and discussed the merits of different
preachers. But when the King came out of his
attack the court went back to bon mot making,
gay clothes, and neighing after its neighbor’s wife!
After seeing “ The Fatal Wedding,” I am in-
spired to write the following :
The Worst Woman in Wodonga.
A night of murk and mist, and out of a sky like a horned
malediction a half-moon looked down on Alys Effingham
hurrying down the deserted street. She drew her beau’-
titul furs more closely around her, and almost fell over a
body lying across the path. Stooping, she peered into
the face on the footpath, and, starting back, shrieked
My God ! My husband ! ” A hundred yards off an Irish
policeman, coming stealthily out of the side-door of a
hotel, heard the shriek, and recognised it as that of a
society lady for whom he had been searching. Coming-
up at a run, he forced a citation for divorce into the icv
hands of Mrs. Effingham. “ But death has divorced us,”
moaned the misjudged wife as she pointed to the corpse
on the kerb.
“ Make no mistake, ” hissed a voice, that of Gladys Doone,
at “ e r elbow, 4 Laurence Jago is as bad a shot as he is a man
—fortunately for me. Your husband will live to be my
husband. Gladys Doone’s black eyes flashed. She was
dressed from toque to toe in costly crimson chiffon and
satin. The shrinking Alys sobbed “ But I never did you
any harm. Before the Worst Woman in Wodonga could
reply the report of a revolver rang out, and the police-
man who had been going through the wounded man’s
pockets dropped dead, shot from a window of the hotel
by the housemaid he had betrayed.
The famous divorce case of Effingham versus Effingham
Laurence Jago co-respondent, had been called for
hearing. A woman was selling violets just without the
doors. She wore a sun-bonnet and a long cloak.
B. Effingham, the petitioner, pacing sadly into the
court, groaned at sight of the flowers. “My wife’s
favorites, he said ; “give me a bunch.” Alys handed
him the violets with one hand and with the other tore
off her disguise. “Why,” he asked coldly, “have
you clone this?” “So that I might speak to you
once more,” she sobbed. “0, Philip, my husband, I have
been true to you in thought and word and deed.
You are the father of your children, and I am the victim
of a conspiracy. “ False words, false woman. Did you
not meet Laurence Jago on the gangway of the Orontes
half an hour before it left ? An assignation ! The proofs
of your guilt ? You drew down your veil as he raised his
licit. But I was coming off, Philip, after saying good-
bye to mother. He was going away.” “But he didn’t
£°-, Your signal brought him back.” “No, no, my
husband, the police launch brought him back. He was
arrested for forgery.”
At that instant Gladys Doone, dressed in orange and
gold radium silk, came out of the court and, taking the
broken-hearted man’s arm, said, “Congratulations,
Philip, your decree nisi has been pronounced. And the
children »a ltlnB' t o know if you want the custody of the
~ resumed her disguise, but her lips were set tightly.
He and she shall never have my children,” she said,
and turning, saw the funeral of the murdered policeman
passing down the street. In one vehicle Mrs. Effingham
recognised her faithful nurse, butler and cook, and they
recognised her despite the ugly bonnet and cloak. Gather-
ing around her they exclaimed against the injustice of
her divorce The three of us,” said the butler, “over-
heard Mr. Jago and Miss Doone planning to get you
divorced. And she was to give him £2OOO on the day
Mr ; Effingham married her. She signed a paper, and he
put it into a tin box and buried it in a vault in the very
cemetery we are going to.” J

Besides being responsible for the above, “ The
Fatal Wedding” was the cause of Florence
Young s accident on Friday. She was rehearsing
the burlesque on the melodrama that has been
introduced in “Florodora,” and fell and cricked
her spine. A report that her neck was broken
whirled around town like a maddened motor-car
Florence was out of the ‘ ‘ Florodora ” cast on Satur-
day, but back in the scented garden on Monday
night. Once I knew a mummer lady who acquired
a reputation as a wit by replying to questions re
her health, “ Well, you know I’ve never been quite
well since I broke my neck.” She really had
broken it (some) by a fall from a horse, but Sur-
geon O’Hara, of Melbourne, glued it together
afresh.
Mrs. (General) Finn and her debutante daughter,
who are living at the Wentworth Hotel, are great
contrasts in coloring. Mrs. is like a Spanish
dame, Miss like a Norwegian lassie with a weight
of yellow hair.
Tuesday afternoon is the real macaroon time at
the Australia Hotel these days. Tea is drunk to
an accompaniment of vocal and instrumental
music. Well-known singers and players take part
under the direction of Mr. H. N. Southwell.

Parkes family and the offshoots of that old ecclesi-
astical pioneer, Archdeacon Scott.

Consider again the relative importance of
things. At Burwood (N.S.W.), last week, a
woman who was convicted of fearfully ill-using a
State child of 14 was fined (“ heavily fined, ’’ the
Telegraph called it) £2O. The same day a man
who ill-used a plate-glass window got two months’
“jug” without the option ; and a man who used
a horse got six months. The last man didn’t ill-
use the horse; if he had it would probably have
cost him 10s. He only used the animal —but. then,
it wasn’t his horse. And, as already mentioned,
the horrible maltreatment of a defenceless girl
only ran to a small fine.
From the Argus report (5/5/’O6) of Frank
Bullen’s lecture on “ The Way They Have in the
Navy”:—
The British navy stood for the peace of the world, and
he advised Australians to support its necessarj- increase,
for if they had not the defence of a sufficient fleet they
would be in a parlous place.
Age report, same day :
Mr. Bullen demonstrated his up-to-dateness by a strong-
appeal to Australians to have a navy of their own, and so
ensure their own safety without being dependent on out-
side aid—a sentiment which met with the heartiest
approval from his hearers.
Now what did Bullen really say? No visiting
lecturer will be able to shake the majority of
Australians from the determination to have an
Australian fleet, _ but as a matter of curiosity it
would be interesting to know Bullen’s real opinion.
A 14-year-old Melbourne nurse-girl was
lately charged with larcenies of loose cash and
articles of jewellery from the home where she
looked after the baby. The prosecutor brought
the case into court in order to recover a brooch
from the pawnbroker with whom the youngster
had pledged it. The pawnbroker’s assistant,
explaining how he came to take the brooch with-
out making inquiries of the girl, said she was
“ well-known to the Mont de Piete ” thereabouts.
The more often a small and early nurse-girl takes
things to the “ pop shop ” the less likely she is to
be commended to the earnest attention of the
police, seemingly. Pawnbrokers are an artless,
unsuspecting people.
“You make a lot of noise about Walter
Ward, the boy who was transferred on sale forms
from proprietor to proprietor in Victoria,” said
the dyspeptic, with an injured air. “Why, I’m
a middle-aged man, and have been so trans-
ferred 17 times this last 10 years. I was
sold with the boarding-house every time—
myself and as many as ten other boarders, with
piano, coalscuttle and sundries, going for £l7O one
time and £45 another. We could leave, of course,
but that’s not the question. The point,” and he
placed his hand over the dyspeptic part, “is that
we were sold—passed over to the purchasers with
the fire-irons and milk-jugs.”
The tale of “D. Harum ” (8., 10/5/’O6) of
how he founded long ago “ The Australian Press
Cutting Agency,” and how it died immediately
with a subscribers’ list of 0-—how, in fact, it tied
its own duck-egg round its neck and hanged itself
therewith—reminds that there is an existing
“Australian Press Cutting Agency.” It lives
and moves, and has its paste and scissors at
Lincoln Chambers, 341 Collins-street, Melbourne,
and it has no intention of pegging out. Perhaps
the management is different from that of “D.
Harum s ” show. The Bulletin has pleasure in
making this explanation. It would have; a lot
more pleasure if there hadn’t been 4d. fine for de-
ficient postage on the Australian Press Cutting
Agency’s letter. That is cutting it rather fine.
BE BEAUTIFUL!
Imitation may be the sincerest form of flattery,
but at times it must be very annoying-, as in the case of
“ Valaze,” the wonderful Russian skin-food. Three years
ago it was introduced to the public of Australia by Mdlle.
Helena Rubinstein, and so speedily were its good qualities
recognised that quite a big- demand sprang up almost
immediately. Instantly dozens of imitations of “ Valaze ”
appeared, got up to closely resemble the Russian skin-
food. Often the food bore a name so nearly resembling
1 Valaze ” that it deceived buyers. All this was very
flattering, but at the same time decidely aggravating, for
often not only worthless, but positively harmful so called
skin-foods were trading on the reputation of “Valaze.”

of the genuine “ Valaze ” are widely recog-nised. Price,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 15, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The Fitzpatrick-Armytage wedding even
eclipsed that sensational pink Oliver match The
Armytage clan broke into extravagance," and
added any amount of frill and silver to complete a
wholly theatrical effect. The bride was almost
lost to sight in a sea of exquisite fal-lals —a,
foundation of heavy glace with a series of over-
dresses and a five-act drama of interesting and
almost exciting decorations ! Accordeon-pleated
gauze, Brussels net, floss silk, bebe ribbons
pleated chiffons, ducliesse satin (any one of which
would have composed a small person’s dress) were
leagued together in one resplendent whole Won-
derful laces draped the pretty 15-carat head and
veiled the omnium gatherum. Guests and specta-
tors peered like second-hand dealers at the laces
of great value, lent by the relatives and intimates
Two children in theatrical rig helped to bear
some of the great wilderness of snowy train. A
team of attendants, harnessed up in silver cords
and blue and cream trappings, followed that
gorgeous creation down St. John’s aisle and
swarmed round when the Voice breathed.
The two plain, untrimmed figures in that over-
powering clothes picture were the ’groom himself
and Captain Guy Madden who barracked solus
for the he-prmcipal. Captain Arthur Fitzpatrick
ex-aide, ex-fighter, ex-many other things, wore
the smile on his grim jaw that he displayed only
when he intentionally kept an Ex. waiting for the
Gor-save signal. At such times a saucy grin
softened the jaw’s outline and made the' much-
canvassed private secretary a likeable captain
Ihe Clarke and Talbott!e Exes, liked him none
the less, and went to quite a small trouble
(Governors are not given that way) to set
a public blessing on his fortunes. This
blessing helped them considerably. Wed-
nesday’s match crowned them. Miss Army-
tage is one of a large brood with a local history
that started with the teething days of the State
A big bachelor brother gave her away He is the
Hercules of the Melbourne Club, and little bride
sister was always a pretty shrimp beside him.
Marms relate with bated breath how Miss
Armytage—the bride of the week—took lessons in
cm > p-grilling and egg-poaching before entering
tne double state. Also, how ‘ ‘ she stitched all her
own initials on.” A big thing if, like the Girl from
Kay s, she “ had a dozen of everything.” They
put down all the virtues of the canonised saints to
the gilt-edged bride. It seems too much perfec-
tion for any one marm to acquire.
The cheques received by Captain Fitzpatrick’s
bridelet run into four figures, while the silver and
electric plate gifts will furnish the future domicile
five times over. When toast-rack and mustard-
pot No. 1 wear out, reserve supplies from the
matrimonial store-room will take their places.
As for the clocks ! Half Toorak thought of sup-
plying the time to the newly-linked pair. When
all are on tick, something like two dozen clocks
will be marking the age of the domestic day for
the Fitzpatricks.
A political scandal is on the brew, and the
witches of the sex squat round the cauldron, wait-
ing breathlessly for a boil-over or at least a singing
of the pot that may be interpreted aright. The
politician in question has been showing some
favor to an individual undeserving in the public
eye. The man himself reckoned he was only
getting his due, and allowed his missus to do all
ttie thanking necessary. That, it is alleged, was
m the politician’s programme. But the dame is
said to have overdone her thanks. Divorce is
threatened and a scandal of an extremely sordid
nature.
Complications have arisen over a big medico in
another city who leased his practice. The
lqcum who secured the post had a charming mate,
the Cream called. Then it transpired that the
pair lacked the formal tie of wedlock. It wasn’t
that they had forgotten. They couldn’t, each
haying a partner already. Decrees nisi were
being sought by the original mates in separate
suits. Meantime they lived in hope. So the
Cream has crawled back into its jug.
A very large, brainy audience took off its hat
(most of it had its hair off already) to a Browning
discourse, delivered last Thursday by Rep. H. B.
Higgins under ’Varsity auspices. The Athenaeum
Hall never held an audience with a bolder show of
brow and bump. Even a Toorak dame of high
degree—Mrs. Albert Miller—coming late, had to
crush her elaborate grey and pink chiffons in a
small space left beside a destitute-looking poet on
a back bench. Earnest-looking girleens, who
could be prettily pictured playing kiss in-the-
ring with sunshine in their cheeks and the wind
in their flying tresses, sat forward intently and
strained their bearing to catch something of the
long dull polemics of the bard who dealt in high
conundrum thoughts and overwhelming poly-
syllables. Rep. Higgins gave the idea of having
written the lecture in his best Equity hand and then
proceeded to read it out with costs. It sounded
like a demurrer, and was a dull—most dull—pro-
ceeding.
The one good lecturer on the Poets is, after all,
Prof. Tucker, though he is given to aesthetic
velvet coats and an occasional haw-haw mannah.
He has sufficient sympathy with an audience to
respond to its moods and to consider its intellec-
tual lapses. For instance, if, in lecturing, he finds
the technicalities of poetic lore oppressing the
listeners, he introduces, without a violent wrench
from his subject, some incidental story, some flash
of humor, some quip or crank that picks up the
wearying mind, refreshes it, and carries it full
steam ahead again. The other night, when Rep.
Higgins had finished droning of Browning, Prof.
Laurie, with even more pink dome than the
shining lawyer-politician, raised a squeaky treble,
which ruined the effect of some light stories,
aiming a gentle bolt of ridicule at the poet of the
wrapt-up sayings. Then Dr. Walter Murdoch,
poetaster, and ’Varsity litterateur , stood up with
an untrained thatch and added some real gold
[“-carat) to the subject, but so awkwardly that
the bulk of the audience failed to catch and prize
The true and tried lecturer is never com-
pletely lost in a subject. He must have a kindly
eye on the crowd and learn to coquette just a little.
There must be a large number of Benedicts eon-
templating a bolt with the girl they met too late.
(How that does soothe the conscience of a stained-
glass window Miss ! No amount of flattery or
persuasion can equal that pathetic gag, “ The
(xirl Ive met Too Late.”) All this apropos of a
recent big elopement—or threatened elopement—
at mention of which seven furious and highly-
excited Benedicts have rushed to this scribe to
announce in a thunderbolt fashion that they are
not going to elope, and whoever said so must be
prepared to settle with them (a terrible shake of
the top-piece at this juncture). What a great
number of these old married Melbourne citizens
must be contemplating the terrible step ! They
were all middle-aged reproachers and announced
that they were not the eloping party referred to,
with a “Consider my age!” expostulation of
nands and shoulders that wasn’t in the least
intended to be funny.
Talk about “The Song of the Shirt!” The
masculine garment isn’t in it with certain piece-
work millinery. One Melbourne firm pays nine-
pence a time for the complete manufacture of fur
toques making the shape and covering it in
approved fashion with fur. Some hands find it
hard to finish one toque per day.
Every cold season hat is a sort of glorified
catafalque for a dead bird. And there’s such a
difference in the arrangement of the silent brood.
In some cases the hat-brim seems good enough for
the last long rest. But a few deceased twitterers
of gaudy plumage appear to make a final flutter
and curl and coquet and even frisk. And just one
or two varieties sit up on the hat crown, and perk
and glare defiantly at the feminine population for
whose beautification they were struck down in the
days of their early bill and coo. One draper
states that 700 dead birds stand to the credit of
the local dressers as a result of an early winter
sale. Seven hundred dear little twitterers
butchered and laid out on the millinered
catafalques! It’s a barbarous business. Why
doesn't an Association start a fashion of wearing
rabbit dresses, and covering a hat with a quaint
design in codlin moths or some other pest the
destruction of which would be of practical benefit?
Those who ought to know assess the portion of the
principal bride of this season at £20,000. It seems
these days that the eligible young man takes a
girl for better or worse per Troy weight.

The Marmora bore away on its last trip outward
the most curious lot of globe-trotters possible to
herd together sardine fashion in the height of the
shipping season. A she-tripper sends along pic-
tures of the floating population :
Mrs. N. Grimwade, from the St. Kilda-road mansion,
does the deck as she would the Flemington terrace, with
a grand air, giving a garden-party smile to the leavened
few, and condescending to a word or two only on very
rare occasions. Her husband has thawed to the extent
of running the sports committee, but the girls treat him
far more distantly than he was ever prepared to treat
them. Prof. Rentoul has a forbidding air. They may
talk of fighting Larry and his platform larks. Aboard,
he looks like a very vinegary Dissenter. His clever
daughter looks dull. Miss Connie Godfrey, a St. Kilda
chick, has a capital 'board-ship style. She dresses
cutely, and looks in the picture. Senatah Matheson
rambles about airily, butterfly-fashion, sitting down only
when there’s some smart coterie in vein for airy per-
siflage. Miss Winter, who recently flashed it at Menzies
with a great display of diamonds, admits a few to her
smiles. She’s a fair trifler, spoilt by too much cash
dignity. There are a Mrs. and Miss Morgans, from
Sydneyside, who are getting heaps of fun out of our
mixed crush aboard.
An ocean-traveller sends word of a very slow
English officah who aide-de-camped it in one
of the States for a time and threw up the
office to go forth into the wide, wide world to
wed a titled globe-trotteress. “He looks slow
and particularly gloomy, but I hear the spinster
in question suffers from rheumatism so badly
that often she loses the use of her natural
supports. These collapse, and the fair one folds
up like a punctured concertina. Probably the
officah knows this, hence the gloom. However, I
hear, too, that when the touring spinster isn’t
from rheumatism she’s bright and
amiable. Fancy sitting on deck for pretty well
five weeks with the picture of the occasional
collapsed-concertina lady for a future mate in
one s mental focus.

MRS. BOOTES, recently shot by her husband at

goes everywhere, laughs, chaffs, and knocks round
the land generally. When in the Holy Capital
itself, he walks abroad on his own, when and how
he lists, bangs a cheery boxer on the side of
his bump of veneration, and has all the appear-
ance of being a real live good sort instead of a
deputy-imperial paper-weight. Sometimes (an
Adelaide girl tells me) he inserts his head into a
bazaar group fixed for the camera bolt and gets
snapped with a crew of unconscious girls. He
uses up a fund of energy and good spirits, and
makes six foot three of geniality in the land.
This State glories in a good sort of V.R. private
secretary at last. The Hon. Vic. Hood turns out
to be a right bower, and the best card in the
whole V.R,. pack. He may lisp at times of great
agitation or botheration, but he’s a man with a
decent sense of dry old duty, and none of the
usual caddy affectations.
Several exciting pictures of she-anglers pub-
lished in a local weekly suggest the exact relative
position of the Social Miss to the deep sea of
ehgibles. One view of a fisheress getting a
bite is eloquent of the local Beatrice kindling to
enthusiasm at a probable catch, and another,
showing the sport actually landing a two-pounder,
has a glow of eye and wholesome look of rapture
that the fashionable Miss wears only once in her
Jiff 3 when the eligible is branded, sealed, and
labelled hers. The sex is always fishing—it is a
recreation peculiar to the species. If it isn’t fish-
ing for everyday compliments, a little flattery, a
husband, or a scandal, it is looking for a row or a
bangle.
At a Melbourne emporium a holidaying dame
from Launceston (Tas.) recently bought and paid
for an enormous sideboard. Then she realised
that her house was not big enough to fit the piece
of furniture, and the door wouldn’t let it in. So
it has gone into a store, and she has gone back to
Tasmania to economise for a year or two, by which
time she hopes to be able to send for the mon-
strosity, and move into a house to match. She
was quite free and contented before. Now she is
a slave, driven hither and thither by a bloated
piece of furniture, which threatens to put her into
an early grave.
The ’Varsity flag had hardly completed its last
flutter when the Bent State put itself into its
official best bib and tucker for the coming of the
Chrysanthemum fleet. The brown Admiral and
co. shine like well-polished mahogany furniture
and their smiles are flanked by a double file of
flawless ivories. Otherwise their little monkey
heads and flat features are same as any other boy
brownies’ from Mikado-land. But the local girl,
or some of her, looks with a kindly eye on the
little chocolate boy in blue, and wants badly to
take him home in her satchel and feed him on a
diet of milk-lasses and cream puffs. In Collins-
street every brownie is peered at, ogled, some-
times even embraced in a warm, comprehensive
feminine glance. And the little man hurls his
chest out more than ever and goes on with an
eloquent air of reciprocity. Envy of the brown
stranger is the white man’s burden just now.
His nose is put out of joint, and he gets even to
cursing in his bitterness.

have been distributed by the Bent Government!
well-nigh bursting its waistcoat in an effort to do
the brown squad proud. The hoardings in ques-
ticm make their hospitable attentions known in a
mixed manner, by stating in a high headline—
‘ their Exes.”, &c., and in small type
underneath the formal rigmarole—“ Reception in
the btate 1 arliament House,” &c. ; and in another
Of Admiral Shimamura,” &c. Altogether
seven varieties of type are in use to ask the Smiths
to the Jap junket.

Melbourne Caledonian Society dragged half-a-
dozen of the Jap naval officers to its smoke night
and marched them up the Masonic Hall with the
society s bare-legged pipers making awful noises
in front of them. Ihis deplorable demonstration
brought its own punishment upon the assembled
Scotsmen. Having heard the bagpipes, and feel-
mg confident they could do nothing worse than
that the brown visitors in due time mounted the
platform, and sang a war song. Anyhow it
served the Sandies right. The musical utterances
of modern Pagan civilisation undoubtedly are
worse than bagpipes.

TRYPHENA.
A burglar, aged 68 years, on conviction at
Wellington (M.L.). said that he spent the best
part of 40 years in gaol in N.S. Wales, and that
on receiving his discharge in 1901 the authorities
at Sydney paid his fare to Maoriland. That is
reciprocity. Maoriland did the same thing for
Sydney in the case of the Maungatapu murderer.
With the approach of Empire Day the
air of N.S. W. State schools is becoming charged
with jingoism. The pupils are to be heard bel-
lowing “Gorsave,” “ Rule Britannia,” and other
'fahicking songs, at all hours of the day. Jose’s
“Growth of the Empire” is being studied by
teachers as carefully as if they were working it up
for an exam., and aldermen—especially the sub-
urban variety—and members of public school
boards are rehearsing speeches with whicli to
enlighten Young Australia. And for all their
trouble in singing the jingo songs, and their
patience in listening quietly to the labored utter-
ances of teachers and the vapid effusions of
parish-pump nobodies, the poor kids will be
rewarded, in most cases, with a gassy bottle of
lemonade and an indigestible bun, probably an
unsaleable stomach-ache remaining over from a
baker’s Good Friday stock,
Adelaide has had its first motor fatality
under unusual circumstances. A country doctor’s
driver was found dead early one morning, under
his overturned car. When it became known that
most of the hotels around had been visited by the
car during the night, the police interviewed two
other men who had been in deceased’s company,
but they said they had left him while he was still
alive and in good order, and walked home. They
were then invited to explain many bruises
and a broken collar-bone, and at last
they admitted that they had been out on a
garish spree in the doctor’s car while the medical
gentleman was absent, that there had been a wild
hurrah of liquor, and that the deceased had started
for home trying to drive three cars—a real one
and two imaginary ones—along five roads simul-
taneously. Finally they collected themselves out
of the ruins of the father and grandfather of a
smash, and, finding the driver dead, they left him
there and crawled home. The coroner’s court
spoke severely about their conduct in not sum-
moning help— getting first aid for the dead, in
fact—but, except as a matter of'sentiment, it is
difficult to see what good it would have done.
Melbourne papers are just now filled with
long letters of protest against the “brutal and
degrading” display of boxing by Squires and
Murphy, several being written by “sportsmen”
who run cronk horses, a few more by ghouls who
delight in shooting pigeons released from traps,
and some more are from the pens of people who
have been prosecuted under the Factories Act for
sweating their girl and child employees. Which
recalls the fact that, in Sydney, some years ago,
there was a man who, whenever an execution was
imminent, used to shake the earth with his
demands for tickets of admission. Somehow he
always succeeded in obtaining these, and never a
strangulation passed off without his being in the
front, row to listen to the dull, depressing thud.
There was a big fight on the evening following
one execution, and, as they left Darlinghurst gaol
together, a Telegraph man asked the visitor if
he was going to the contest. He shuddered.
“No,” he replied, “I should think not. I sup-
pose. in your business, you have to attend many
sickening spectacles, but I should be sorry to
think that you visited such scenes of brutality for
your own amusement. In the interests of the
community, the Law should suppress such horri-
fying displays. By the way, do you think you
could get me a ticket for the hanging of , at
Bathurst, next week ? I’d take a trip up there, if
you could.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
‘ ‘ The chief tariff anomaly to be rectified
is that of Protective duties which do not protect.”
—Prime Minister Deakin.
Transpires now that the Australian and
Maoriland Governments both cabled to the British
Government affirming Natal’s sacred and inalien-
able right to hang its own nigger.
Bananaland prepares to build 20 locomo-
tives at the Ipswich shops. The engines built
there have given much more satisfaction than the
imported article, and the Government reckons it
is worth while to keep the local industry going.
Warships seem to go as a matter of course
straight from the Australian station to the bone-
yard. The recent sale, at Chatham dockyard, of
the cruisers Mildura and Pylades practically com-
pletes the “scrapping” of our erstwhile heavily
subsidised Australian squadron.
The Jap squadron, on its visit to Sydney,
is to be given a celebration—the usual flag-
flapping, fireworks, oratory and booze. A more
fitting celebration would be to lay down the keel
of a first-class battleship in an Australian ship-
yard, and thus by imitation sincerely flatter the
strenuous Brown Man.
If Alfred Deakin really wants to get
through a mass of business in the short Federal
session, his first act should be to abolish the ad-
dress-in-reply and the utterly useless debate
thereon. There is almost time to pass a new
tariff in the period usually wasted over that un-
interesting babblement about the universe in
general.
Hughes, M.P., at Forbes (N.S.W.), referred
to American monopolies, and stated that Yankee
industries were practically in the hands of from
eight to a dozen men. Yes, and the worst of it is
that those queer anomalies, Australian Labor-
Freetraders, are apparently quite satisfied to let
those eight or a dozen Americans work their sweet
will on Australian industries as well.
THE FAMOUS NECKLACE.
KING GEORGIE PORGIE : “ A necklace of nega-
tives ! How absurd. My people know me
better than that.”
One of the paltry tricks of the Victorian
Government is docking the old age pensioner’s
dole what time he is in hospital. If the hospitals
were Government institutions there would be a
thin excuse for doing so, but as they are run prin-
cipally on money subscribed by the charitable
public there is none—not even an excuse that is
thin enough to pass under a door without disturb-
ing the carpet. The forfeited amounts are not
paid to the hospitals that shelter the old chaps,
either. They go into the surplus.
Natal is trying to raise £500,000 to shoot
its niggers with. Natal has about 97,000 white
inhabitants, 101,000 imported Asiatics, and
900,000 Africans, who object strongly to pay
taxes. The handful of white people—not much
more than half as many as there are in Tasmania
—added £2,000,000 to the debt in 1902, £1,500,000
in 1903, and £2,000,000 in 1904. They now owe
about £lBO for every white inhabitant, and unless
some one will lend them more money with which
to go and shoot at the bulk of the country’s
alleged taxpayers it appears as if almost anything
might happen to Natal.

N.S.W. City Council proposes to join in
the national jog to the pawn shop. Several of the
city streets want re-blocking and the wood paving
is to be extended to others, and in order to find
funds the Mayor is to obtain authority to borrow
£60,000. It wants a lively faith m the local
hardwood (even if the Mayor is a timber mer-
chant) to regard wood blocks as a permanent and
reproductive work.
S.A. Labor party is wisely abandoning its
former rigid manual-labor test for Parliamentary
candidates. The 14 men picked by plebiscite to
stand at the next Federal elections include Paris
Nesbit. K.C. ; Allerdale Grainger, who was lately
the State’s Agent-General in London; and
Vaughan, a youthful lawyer and brother of the
Single-Tax M.H.A. There were 61 candidates.

Lade, Scottsdale, Tasmania.
Scottsdale is celebrated as the only place in the
Commonwealth where the anniversary of the scrap
between St. George and the Dragon is celebrated.
There are some celebrated old fossils about there,
and Bill Lade is their prophet.
San Francisco is to be rebuilt a city of
steel. Millions of tons of steel will be used in the
task, and every ounce of it will be manufactured
in the States, under a tariff which is a tariff. Sup-
posing Sydney were destroyed by fire or earth-
quake, where are the Australian foundries and
workshops with which to supply the rebuilding
material? How many millions of tons of steel
would be dumped on our shores to cope with the
demand ?
The great Owe’Sullivan, when he was going
strong, had a mysterious scheme which started
with the erection of a costly and magnificent
chimney at Miller’s Point, Sydney. When the
chimney was finished the bottom fell out of things
and the rest of the scheme never materialised.
Now Carruthers proposes to turn the Mint out of
its old quarters, and a new Mint is wanted, and
Owe’Sullivan urges that it should be built on to
his chimney so as to take away the nakedness of
the unproductive landmark, and prevent that item
of loan expenditure looking so like an insane
brick hole in the air.
Victorian Protectionists have to decide
whether they will take the decision of the Labor
Leagues, to fight to the death even
against those members of the Pro-
tectionist party who have stood by
Labor in this Parliament, lying down.
In politics it is not wise to take attacks
of that sort lying down. The Protec-
tionist organisation in self-defence
should hit back and make the nomi-
nation of the first Labor candidate to
oppose a member of the Isaacs wing
of the party the signal for opposition
to every Laborite in the State. Such
a course might result in the loss of a
seat or two to the Tories. That would
be a harsh price to pay for teaching a
lesson ; but, as the lesson is necessary,
the price, whatever it is, should be
paid. With faith-breakers it must be
war to the knife.
Charles Atkins, the hard-work-
ing President of the Victorian Chamber
of Manufacturers, who shouts Protec-
tion all the time, is to be backed by
Melbourne Age in his candidature for a
Senate seat at next Federal elections.
Of the three retiring Victorian mem-
bers—Fraser, Zeal, and Styles—the
obsolete Zeal has decided to leave
voluntarily instead of having depar-
ture thrust upon him. Fraser, having
lost the countenance of Dawvid Syme,
will perhaps get the Argus support.
But he was lately on the sick list, and,
now that his forlorn colleague Zea.l is
dropping out, old Simon the Sec-
tarian may follow suit. Fraser’s re-
tirement would improve the chance of
victory for the three Labor candidates.
The old man commands sympathy out-
side of the sash-and-badge clan. His
violent Orangeism is so pathetically
sincere.
The Greater Melbourne Commission, ap-
pointed some four—or 40 —years ago, got out of
its grave last week to report in a weary whisper
that nothing could be done without the concur-
rence of the interested councils. The “ interested
councils ” ! Apparently the interested people, the
people who are misgoverned at great expense by a
vast number of unnecessary, puffed-up aldermen,
who mostly speak for Proputty—apparently they
don’t count.
Shaw Lefevre, in the February number of
the Contemporary Review, has an article on
“Rival Navies,” and mentions a thing or two
worthy of note. This is one of them :
It may be as well to subject the existing navies to a
critical estimate of their comparative effective forces as
tested by the experience of the recent war in tfijFar
East Turning now to the British fleet, we must, in
spite of the holocaust of 150 useless ships condemned by
the recent Board of Admiralty, strike off as ail but use-
less six battleships of the second and third class, one of
the first class—the Collingwood, launched in 1882 ; one
coast defence ship—the Rupert ; and four armored
cruisers of the Orlando type. We must also exclude no
less than S 7 protected and unprotected cruisers of the
first second, and third class, including the Powerful and
Terrible which it may be confidently maintained that no
Board of Admiralty will ever venture to send to sea in
time of war with 800 men on board.
Shaw Lefevre has been connected with Admiralty
management in the Cold Country, and should
know what he is writing of. This is what we get
for our share of the precious naval arrangement—
our very flagship picked out from the list of dead-
beat tramps which the real British Navy is casting
off!

Governor Talbottle, of Victoria, carries
Jap worship to the extreme point of adulation,
when he says the alliance between Britain and
japan is not merely a union of Governments, but
of “kindred nations.” This brings nearer the
time when a Mikado will suggest that the tail of
his son and the hand of a British King’s daughter
shall be joined in marriage, and when that same
Mikado will receive a shocked refusal from a
potentate who suddenly realises for the first time
where his country has been drifting to.
“ X.C.P.” : Hop’s striking cartoon of the
Little Boy at Manly striving to roll £83,000,000 of
globular debt uphili leads me to the idea of form-
ing a party with one plank only on its platform—
“ Never £90,000,000.” With Carruthers’ Cabinet
calmly proposing to borrow £2,500,000 for the
North Coast railway, and considering the attitude
of the State Labor party towards the productive
loan which never products, this one whoop would
be worth all the rest of the politics of N.S.W. put
together.
Sydney Telegraph was terribly upset
about the Federal Commerce Bill, ft had a
leader headed “ The Great Federal Meddle ” and
a letter signed “ Butter Fat,” both prophesying,
amongst other disasters, the loss of Australia’s
export trade as a result of Government grading
and branding. And in the very same issue it gave
a list of proposals adopted by the London Butter
Exchange to be supported by that body before
the British Parliamentary Committee on adultera-
tion. Proposal No. 6 read :
That the genuineness of all butter should be guaranteed
by the Government of the country of origin, and no
butter should be allowed in (to Great Britain) unless so
guaranteed.
Will someone please give Vic. Minister
Swinburne some ginger? Else that long person,
who has a habit of doing valuable and interesting
things, promises to get tired and give the habit
best. He has battled long with the Carrum
Swamp Irrigation Trust—really a drainage trust
has got the boundaries fixed, has had valuations
made and rates graded, has put an Act through
arranging for the State to do all the work and for
the landowners to pay interest and a very gradual
sinking fund, and now—now when he thought the
whole thing was fixed—these precious Carrum
landowners want the interest reduced, the valua-
tions lowered, and the period of repayment
extended immeasurably. Swinburne was half-
inclined to be angry with the deputation, but a
wave of weariness came over him and he promised
to see what he could do.
Higgins, who was Attorney-General in the
Watson Ministry, is to be opposed this year,
it is said, by one Rain of Collmgwood,
an ex-Mayor, and once a burning and voci-
ferous light in the Sal Army. Hopes are
entertained by the Tories that Rain will
Sour down on Higgins in a devastating manner.
iut Higgins is hard to shift. In 1901 there were
8496 votes polled at North Melbourne, and he got
4958 of them, the balance being divided between
two desolate persons named Barr and Selby. In
1903 there were only two candidates. Higgins
got 11,595 votes, and S. Painter was just visible
fleeing in the distance with 4897. In the new
arrangement of things, Higgins proposes to stand
for Clifton, and the ex-Sally Army man is vaguely
supposed to be powerful thereabouts, but the
chances are that Higgins will beat him, Sarah
Army and all.
KINDLY.
SATURNINE OLE GENT : “ Now, supposing I was
to give you a pound, eh! What would you do?"
CADGER : “A pound, sir! S’help me, I’d drop
dead with pure gratitude.”
S.O.G.: “ Ah! I’m afraid I’ll have to save your
life.”
A letter from South Africa, the country
which is saved and made prosperous by cheap
black and yellow labor—“these splendid workers,
as Spruce Myth calls them :
This is a country of black and yellow people. The
odor of them is in my nostrils. Vegetables are at fright-
ful prices, and apples are 3d. each. The biscuits we got
for 5Jd per lb. in Victoria we pay Is. 3d. for here. A loaf
of bread costs 10d., and all meats are Is. per lb. and
upwards. A five-roomed house costs £5 10s. a month.
The place is down to zero.
There is great joy in the idea of inviting
all living members of bygone Parliaments to the
jubilee celebrations in honor of the establishment
of Responsible Government in N.S.W. The event
should see a notable gathering. What yarns the
old brigade should have to tell—tales of how this
railway came to run like a letter S to keep So-and-
so sweet; tales of how many uncles and brothers
and cousins and other relatives, friends, acquaint-
ances and constituents So-and-so. and Such-and-
such got into the service ; choice tit-bits about the
old-time prices of portfolios, the quid pro's for dis-
honored pro. notes, the land-grabs, and the
contracts. They managed to make a penny
or two without the beggarly £3OO a year, did
some of those old time irresponsibles. But no
doubt they would prefer to talk of what a lot of
honest, disinterested patriots they were.
In Maoriland all agricultural implements
are now exempt from Customs duties and come in
free. The result is, that the once thriving
machinery industry, which was the support.of
thousands of people in Christchurch and Dunedin,
has been brought to such a state of weakness that
the firms are glad to come to terms with the
International Harvester Co. of America. Morrow,
Bassett and Co., have given up the struggle, and
disposed of business and goodwill to the Rocke-
feller tentacle, and Reid and Gray (a time-honored
name in Maoriland), and Booth and Macdonald
(also an old and much-respected firm which has
deserved well of the State) are understood to be
going the same way, only sticking out for slightly
better terms. The taking over of these firms doesn’t
mean that the industry will go on just the same as
ever under Yankee management; it means that
the works which once beat pig-iron into agricul-
tural implements will now become mere fitting
shops, in which the manufactures of Chicago and
Toronto will be put together by small staffs of
fitters. Also, watch the prices, and notice how
long the fool farmers, who howled for freetrade
in agricultural implements, and got it, will get
the benefit of the prices which local industry
insured to them. When the local firms have been
bought out, the International will proceed to
make the Maoriland farmers pay the cost, and
interest on the cost, of buying up the local
industries —and serve the fool farmers right.
Seddon has been moving in the matter of Protec-
tion for agricultural machinery, but he hasn’t
moved fast enough.
