DOWLING ST., REDFERN, SYDNEY.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The meeting of the N.S.W. Cricket Association
on Monday night last revealed M. A. Noble as a man of
much nerve. Summed up, Mr. Noble and the players
object to the Board of Control, which represents
the Victorian, N.S.W., and Queensland Associations, for
the following reasons :
(1) That the Board does not represent all the best
interests of Australian cricket. That it is not sufficiently
representative. That it does not contain representatives
of the Melbourne Cricket Club, the players, and the S.A.
Association. For this reason he and the players will
assist the Melbourne Club, a proprietary concern, which
represents nothing but its own monetary interests, in an
endeavor to assume sole control of Australian inter-State
and international cricket.
(2) Because it (the Board of Control) proposes to deprive
the players of the management of their own business —
viz., the English tours. Mr. Noble regards this action on
the part of the Board of Control as tyrannical, uncalled
for, and calculated to bring about the ruin of the game.
The sole control of English tours by the Melbourne
Cricket Club, a proprietary concern, does not shock Mr.
Noble’s soul. He apparently imagines that it will foster
international cricket.
(;•>) Because the Board of Control will not listen to any
proposal that the players should have direct representa-
tion. The Melbourne Club doesn’t propose to give them
any representation either, but that makes no odds in Mr.
Noble’s philosophy.
The one really interesting remark that he could
have made he carefully refrained from making. On the
subject of how much the Melbourne Club proposed to
give the players he maintained a close reserve. He
explained that it was customary for the Melbourne
Club to write to players at the end of one season
asking if they would play against an English team at the
end of the next; and incidentally threw a vivid
if unintentional sidelight on some of the past
selection mysteries, and produced an unexpected argu-
ment in favor of the immediate establishment of the Board
of Control to do the thing better. Finally he offered, if
the players were granted direct representation on the
Board and allowed to do as they liked with regard to
English tours, to notify the Melbourne Club that
this was the last time the players would play
with the M.C.O. unless the N.S.W. Association
allowed them, or, if the Association was very nice
to them, the players would intimate to the M.C.C.
that it was their royal will the Club should approach the
N.S.W. Association. Mr. Noble was evidently laboring
under the delusion that he and his followers were masters
of the position. He received his answer when.one speaker
said the Association would go down fighting, and play
with third-rate men before it would give in to the players
or the Melbourne Club, The Association then carried
unanimously the motion that the players be allowed till
Wednesday'to withdraw from the arrangement with the
Melbourne Club. After that, the axe.
* * * *
Of recent years, since the players got fat,
there has been increasing trouble over the ex-
penses. The gentry who have practically lived
upon cricket have been hungering for bigger fees,
and some of them worked the “expenses” racket to
such an extent that the Association was compelled to ask
for details. The details were startling. One “ idol of the
public” had his breakfast in bed when travelling, and
charged ss. for it every time. Another man’s trouble was
that he couldn’t walk round the corner —he had to have a
cab Whenever he went abroad, and “ cab 55.” ran up to
quite a considerable figure in his statement of accounts.
A third willow-wielder used to ask a choice collection of
kindred spirits to join him at supper or dinner in the even-
ings while on tour, and had the front to charge it to
“entertaining.” As a rule there were about,three
cricketers present, and a dozen outsiders in no way
connected with the game. This gentleman was just
“outraged” when the Association said it didn’t see
why it should pay for entertaining his friends. There are a
few more items of the same sort, but these will do for the
present. If our players like to come out boldly as
professionals and announce that they will play for anyone
who puts up the money, the position will be under-
standable, but “expenses” are too elastic.
* * * *
The troubles of the Melbourne C.C. are only
just beginning. Granting that it can keep its house of
cards standing a little longer, it will certainly strike
trouble when it proceeds to redeem its promise to the
Vic. League to bring about a system of district, or elec-
toral, cricket. Under such a system it is hard to see how
it is going to maintain its preponderance. If its electoral
or district system is anything more than a name it must
lose an enormous number of its members, and the sub-
scription list will fall ofi frightfully. If it devises some
weird arrangement by which a man may belong to a dis-
trict body and the M.C.C. at the same time, the League
will discover that the loaf the generous M.C.C. promised
it is a brick imitation. What the Melbourne Club is
fighting for is the control of the treasury chest, and if
the M.C.C, gets the cash, the League clubs won’t. The
trouble just now is that the League clubs are mostly in
debt to the M.C.C., and can’t afford to oppose it.
* * * *
Melbourne Cricket Club and the recalcitrant
players struck a bad snag in W. P. M‘Elhone, the secre-
tary of the Board of Control. That fiery little solicitor,
whose position, by the way, is quite honorary, worked
with tremendous vigor, and it is due to his exertions
that the scheming of the Melbourne Club with the
recalcitrants was so promptly brought to light. He
anticipated every move, and was full to the
chin of facts and figures, so that the half truths
of the other side never got much of a start.
He even had a copy of the constitution of the English
Board of Control, with which to knock out M. A. Noble’s
amazing talk about the players’ right to representation
on the Board. Melbourne Club has heaps of money, and
could hire first-class assistance, and the players were
playing their own game ; and neither the Melbourne Club
nor the players ever reckoned that any honorary official
would be likely to take his coat off and rage round with
an axe after the fashion of secretary M’Elhone.
* * * *
The blastiferous impudence of the Sydney
Cricket Ground Trustees in demanding that the Govern-
ment should not appoint any trustee who was not accept-
able to the rest of the members of the Trust roused
Minister Ashton’s “ Irish.” Jimmy promptly kicked the
Truston the watch-pocket, so to speak, with the intimation
that if the stand it took Was in accordance with the law,
he would see the law altered. This shook the Trust to
the very centre of its fat, and on Monday night it was
learned that it had withdrawn its objection to the appoint-
ment of Mr. Trickett. Now we should soon know what
really took place between the S.C.G. Trustees and the
Melbourne Cricket Ground—as far as the press letter copy
books disclose it.
* * * *
In the roar of the Melbourne C.C. versus the
Board of Control match the Burwood-Glebe struggle for
the N.S.W. premiership has been well-nigh forgotten.
Glebe, it will be remembered, had made 188 in the first
innings, and Burwood responded with 207 for five
wickets, thus winning - the championship. There were
only a few hundred people present, and Sunny Jim
Mackay and Diamond, who are accustomed to be loudly
cheered when they step into the popular gaze, were
greeted with stony silence. A few personal friends, who
argue that Mackay, when he pledged himself to the Mel-
bourne Cricket Club, was an inexperienced youth who
was led away by old and designing citizens, tried to start
a demonstration by cheering him, but it did not eatch on.
Considering that Mackay had just made 2064 runs
for Association matches during the season and had
put up several records, this stony silence was very signifi-
cant.
* * ■» »
When Darling opened his mouth about the
“ treatment” of Victor Trumper he didn’t put his foot in
it, but fell into the cavity bodily. Trumper wrote to the
N.S.W. Cricket Association, barracking for more
custom for the firm of Trumper and Carter. If
this could not be given, well, his services were still at the
disposal of the Association if it paid for them. The Associa-
tion took him at his word, and promptly sent along a
cheque for £5 for Victor’s “services” against S.A. Then
came the organisation of the Queensland tour by
Trumper, regardless of the best interests of the
clubs. Just, at present there is ho rule to deal
with profit-seeking excursions of this kind, but it is
understood that the Association will shortly arm itself
with power to give anv imitators the axe.
* * * *
It looks as though the outcome of the Melbourne
Cricket Club’s revolt may be an attempt to establish
purely professional cricket in Australia. In Melbourne,
on the Melbourne Club’s fine ground, such a thing might
be possible, but in Sydney there is no place outside the
Sydney Cricket Ground where the game can be properly
played ; and the spectacle of Noble, Mackay, Trumper
and co. playing openly for boodle on inferior or matted
wickets seems too great a downfall.
* * it *
The magnetic pole of Sydney Rugby on Satur-
day was the match between South Sydney, last year’s
premiers, and Eastern Suburbs, with an exhibition of
“ line-bucking ” by the Yankee footballers from the Balti-
more as a curtain-raiser. The line-bucking is another
story, but just here it might be remarked that it is more
like mud-larking than football. Nobody expected that
Eastern Suburbs would have any hope of victory, but the
team earned fame last year as a brave crowd which
played a losing match with all its heart and a stiff upper
lip. So when, immediately the kick-off was returned,
Eastern Subs, came away with a rush down the line and
jammed the South Sydneys right on their own goal line,
the public was struck dumb, and even the barrackers for-
bore to yell. South grew desperate, and things were
done to clear that line that call for the disqualifica-
tion of some of the Southern men. Souths bullocked
the ball back to half-way, but the Subs, carried
it down to the 25 again, and were only just prevented
scoring. It was not that South had deteriorated ; it w r as
a case of the Eastern men improving, and they never
slackened the attack one moment. Charge after charge
was stopped, only to be resumed at the first opportunity.
At last the South got its machine to work spasmodically,
and the leather flew down to the Suburbs’ 25, where the
mighty Messenger, who is a whole team in himself, got it,
and, with a tremendous kick, sent it almost to South’s
goal. So fast did he follow that the South backs had no
time to get going, and were forced to kick hurriedly
before getting clear of their own 25, and Messenger
snapped a mark. The shot failed. The Souths got a rush
on, but it was blocked, and the tide swept back towards
their goal line once more. They grew reckless,
and many illegal things were done. Messenger
was a marked man, and some of the South men
showed that they didn’t care much how they
stopped him, but any' preference they had was for some
method which would incidentally break his neck. But
the best of men make mistakes, and it was a blunder by
Messenger that gave South Sydney its only chance to
score. Messenger picked the ball up while off-side, and
kicking it, gave the Souths a penalty kick right under
the bar. They scored 3. The second half saw Messenger
brilliantly kick a goal from a distance of 40yds., and so
equalise the score. The rest had done Souths much good.
Once more the machine worked, and carried the ball into
the Suburbs’ 25, where Messenger stopped it. Another
penalty kick just missed supplying a second goal.
Messenger made an amazing run, charging right through
the South pack, evading the three-quarters, and
jumping clean over the full back when he stooped
to grab him. But he was overtaken and pulled down,
and South Sydney carried the ball out of touch.
Noses were bashed, and two opposing forwards fought a
stand-up fight while the rush carried them along. Com-
bination told, and very slowly South worked its way down
the field. Then there was a blundering kick, a Southern
man getting there first kicked into the arms of D’Alpuget
of the Suburbs. Like a flash he was away. Half-a-dozen
sprang at him, but he passed deftly to Rose, who never
altered. He was at half-way, and the team was left
behind, and, with a solitary South man in close pursuit,
Kose crossed the line and scored in a good position.
Again the shot failed, hut the former Premiers were
beaten by 6 to 3, after a most strenuous game.
* * * * *
The line-bucking game, as displayed at the
Sports Ground on Saturday, is a dreary scramble, a sort
of perpetual scrum, in which the ball can be picked up at
will, and in which there is no off-side. It is full of mag-
nificent resources for playing off personal grudges, and to
prevent murder, the players are so cased in defensive
armor as to be unwieldy. Their ears are covered with
armor plates, their noses are protected by guards which
give them a horrible appearance; shoulders, elbows, knees
and ankles are covered with great wads of quilting, and
it is said they are heavily padded round the hips and
groins. Finally the leather helmet has a thing like a life-
buoy (pocket edition) round the crown to minimise the
effect of collisions. All of which makes for slow play.
The Yankees, after their match was over, stayed to watch
Eastern Suburbs and South Sydney put up a struggle
that was almost a record for energy, and one Yankee,
with his eyes glued on the spectacle of a pair of forwards
unfeignedly fighting as they were swept along in the
pack, remarked : “ Great snakes, look at that! Why this !
game’s ten times rougher than ours 1 ”
* * * *
Manly’s second appearance, against the Uni-
versity Rugby team, was even more disastrous than the
first against Newtown, and this time the Villagers were
beaten 32 to six points. Some of the incidents were:
amazing. In one case a Manly man charged right down
onto the Uni. line, where the blue and gold full back
took the ball from his hands and, running the full length
of the ground on “ his Pat Malone,” scored right between
the posts. It was not football, but a joke—a burlesque.
At Rushcutters’ Bay the St. George individualistic club
went down before Sydney District, which plays a
somewhat more Socialistic game, the Socialists winning
by eight to nil. Newtown trod on Western Suburbs by 11
to five, and Glebe and North Sydney put up an amazing
game at North Shore—a game which was full of
illegalities. Glebe won by 14 to eight. The competition
list now shows Eastern Suburbs, University and Newtown
all abreast with four points each. South Sydney, Glebe,
North Sydney and Balmain have two each, Western
Suburbs and St. George one, and unfortunate Manly',
which has lost both matches, still labors along under the
weight of its nest-egg.
* * * *
In the ’Soccer game Glebe, Balmain and
Pyrmont have forged ahead with four points each, all
three, having won both initial matches. Kozelle and
Granville occupy second place with two points each,
Ultimo, Sydney and South Sydney have not yet scored.
On Saturday Ultimo put up a good fight against the
conquering Pyrmont until its condition gave out, and
the hardened Pyrmonters raged like wolves. The
score (five goals to one) would have been much heavier
but for the strong wind that fought for the exhausted
Ultimo men in the last quarter. Balmain and South
Sydney played well, but the Balmainers had the better
combination, and carried off six goals to one. Glebe
defeated Sydney by eight to nil, but the Sydney team was
deplorably weak, or the indifferent Glebe shooting would
have made no impression.
* » * *
The Australian League’s premiership table
shows Newtown and Sydney at the head with four each,
and the next four teams have a couple of points, while
North Shore and Paddington are wearing the sign of the
bird. Y.M.C.A., after a straggling sort of game, was
beaten by East Sydney, which scored 51 points to the
Good Young Men’s 16. Sydney, which was premier club
last season, met the North Shoremen at Sydney Cricket
Ground and inflicted severe punishment upon them, win-
ning by 61 to 18. But the debacle of the day was New-
town’s flattening out of Paddington. It was like Bill
Squires falling on Tim Murphy. Paddington never scored
a point, while Newtown grew sick of goals and behinds
after 145 points had been scored.
* * * *
There was a surprise or two about Melbourne’s
football last Saturday, the principal astonishment being
at South Melbourne, where the locals downed Colling-
wood by 77 to 57 points. “ Skinner’s hirelings ” have
acquired considerable new' blood—notably the best player
in the Association last year—and they have stolen and
improved on ’Wood’s famous system. Also, they have
learned to kick straight—witness their score of 12 goals
five behinds to ’Wood’s eight and nine. But writer is not,
so far, greatly mashed on them ; the selectors, appa-
rently, put too much trust in height and weight, which
advantages won’t count for so much at the end of the
long, heavy season. Melbourne put up a fair
fight against ’Roy, and looks like repeating its
last year’s early promise and subsequent falling-off.
Carlton went to St. Kilda’s ground and methodically and
placidly notched 100 points to the other crowd’s 35. It
is a pity Bent or somebody couldn’t be persuaded to take
an interest in the Saints’ team —it might thereby be
induced to get a move on, instead of being a mere League
passenger. Some 10,000 spectators watched the ’Wood-
South match, and about 6000 the West-North ditto
(Association). The other Association and Junior matches
meandered along more or less discrepantly, the results
being posted up in the usual pub-windows. By the
way, a telegraphic error in these result sheets caused a
lot of language among the weedy youths who drop into
the tobacconists’ on Saturday morning and back a double
—one League and one Association team—for highest
scores. The aforesaid sheets gave Carlton’s score
wrongly, whereupon the backers of the assumed winning
double straightway “ bust up ” their £l, only discovering
when they saw Monday’s paper that there wasn’t any £1
to bust up. As to these doubles, the laying Nosey seems
to stand on the usual pile of velvet. There being eight
League clubs and 10 Association, the backer has one pick
out of 80 combinations. And Ikey lays £1 to Is.
* ♦ * *
Adelaide’s biggest racing double, the Goodwood
Handicap and Cup, has been won by local horses, J.
Rovven’s Step Out and Allan Macfarlane’s Dynamite.
Bookmakers were not anxious to do business on the Cup,
everything engaged having been backed with Step Out in
doubles, but the local mare, Phaedra (which had beaten
Pifeness, Red Streak, and Avalon at weight for age on
the Saturday), was made a warm favorite on the totalisa-
tor. She ran a poor second to the light-weighted grey
horse, Dynamite, and her backers, under the new
arrangement by which a dividend is paid on the second
steed, got back 18s. for their pound.
* * * *
The totalisator gave the bookmakers a rare
showing-up over the Adelaide Cup. The evening before
the race 2 to 1 was offered against the favorite ; then
came a 5, two 7’s, and an Bto 1. In the machine there
were two equal favorites, each paying rather less than
2 to 1, then came a batch, all returning nines and tens.
But—and here comes in the vital difference —the tote
(under the new rule which gives a quarter of the money
in the machine to backers of the second horse) provides a
chance of “saving.” The winner would have paid, instead
of his £lO 3s. dividend, one-third as much even if he had
been beaten, and the thousand people who backed the
favorite and saw her finish second got practically their
money back. Fancy Moses returningyour money because
you backed the second horse 1
* * -)r Ik
Three men were lately- charged at Perth (W.A.)
with having obtained £125 under false pretences. There
is a difference of two hours and some odd minutes between
Sydney and Perth times, and aWestralianpaper, in work-
ing it out, made an error of some 20 minutes. The result
was that the bookmakers were cheerfully laying the odds
after the race was lost and won. Somebody is alleged to
have sent a message from Randwick with startling sud-
denness, and about the time that such a message might
have arrived a party of young men rushed the various
bookmakers and liberally backed Noreen. There would
have been a considerable downfall if all the bets had been
paid, but apparently, apart from the £125, the book-
makers refused to part up. Arrests followed, and at the
time of writing the chief hitch seems to be that no one
can connect the alleged conspirators with the receipt of
any telegram, and unless that link can be traced the case
presumably goes by the board.
* * * *
The vitality of the racing game— £4s,ooo was
paid for thoroughbreds in Sydney within two weeks
recentlv.
* * * *
There was slaughter at the Upper Manning
(N.S.W.) J.O. races Saturday. First event saw a jockey
and his mount “sent up” for 12 months. The third
brought similar trouble to another horse and rider, and in
the fourth the owner, the man who saddled up, the
jockey and the mare were shut out of the racing business
for two years. In this last case the nag won, but
weighed in 21b. short.
* * * *
Name of Quorrobolong (winner of Canterbury
Park, Sydney, Handicap, last Saturday) broke Ikey Mo
up so badly that he laid any odds asked against him.
Though Quorrobolong ran a duffer in each of two races
recently, Saturday’s performance justifies the belief that
it is in him to win in even better company when the
money comes from the right quarter.
* * * *
Most promising nag seen out for some time, and
one already labelled “ worth watching” by shrewd people
is St. Patrick, full brother to Cockney Boy, by St. Swithin
from Grand Flaneur —mare Liguria. St. Patrick won
Canterbury Park Stakes very easily Saturday last, and,
like a thorough racehorse, landing a good load of coin for
his “stable,” who had the “good thing” so much to
themselves that they got long odds first, and kept putting
the money on till better than “evens ” was difficult to
procure.
* » * *
The race between the Baltimore sailors and the
Sydney Fire Brigade crew lasted about two minutes—
after that it was a procession of two, the Australians
paddling quietly home winners by about 20 lengths. The
funny part of the business was that on the U.S. warship
the leading boat was mistaken for that propelled by the
donkey-licked Yankees, and wild Red Indian whoops
scared the birds round North Head, and the warship’s
syren blew off into blood-curdling shrieks. Then suddenly
a look of horror came over the face of one dignitary.
“ Stop that goldarned row !” he thundered ; “ that’s our
lugger bringing up the tail, sure!” And the row fell
down dead. The victorious firemen will try conclusions
with a crew off the Britannic warship Challenger bn
Saturdav.
* * * *
Apparently there are no other games that th©
sports of the U.S. war-whoop (that is to say, war-ship)
Baltimore can play. Their visit to Port Jackson was a
howling failure. They went down at boxing; Sydney’s
baseballers beat them, and at rowing they were not in the
same street as the firemen. And then, as a last straw,
the little Japs came along and took the girls who always
flock after visiting marines right out of their hands.
• * * *
The motor-cycle racing at Sydney Cricket
Ground on Saturday produced some excitement. In the
first heat there was a sensational collision between
Witham and Murray. Murray ran up the embankment,
and Witham dashed past on the inside. Down came
Murray with a mighty swoop to regain his lost position,
but he had misjudged the distance, and all in a flash they
had collided. Whether Murray rushed on to Witham, or
whether Witham, making a wild spring to keep in
front, dashed into Murray’s front wheel, no one
could tell. The only thing that was certain was
that all but about six spokes were ripped out
of Murray’s wheel, and it was mere accident that
both men were not seriously injured or killed out-
right. Just before the final started, the rain came down.
with a swish, and the track gleamed like an p ''^
just about as slippery. Man after man d ' e "
Finlay went skidding wildly, sliding 20jds. , nj-
sion. Witham’s machine was niore fitted ~ r i ar ; n , r
on the wet surface, and,, in spite of the ns , S
wheelist reeled round the track followea y .
enthusiast, Lynch, of Newcastle, as reckles . , '
Witham won from Lynch by half-a-dozen lengths, the
third man, Murray, being 30yds. further hack.

C. B. Kellow and H. B. James put up 556
miles in that 24 hours run—an average of 26S miles an
hour for the whole distance; good roads and bad
and no roads at all-done in broad daylight, fading
twilight, and inky darkness. It was done as a test and
onlv a lunatic would expect to derive any pleasure out of
such a run. On second thoughts someone did, and
against the Dunlop Tyre Co. it must not count for mental
weakness. The co. has been whooping ever since for thg
Australian-made Dunlop tyre sailed through the .test
without a scratch. On still further thoughts this paper
also finds itself deriving satisfaction out of the run
Anything that demonstrates- the fact that the Australian
article is as good as (and a darned sight better than) t
imported always causes an outbreak of joy in this office.^,
Died, a day or two since, in Sydney, Alec;
Small, who, in the boom days (20 years ago) of ( foot
running, when fortunes might have been won on a Car-
rington ”or a “ Botany,” had a stable of peds, and
backed them largely. Writer knew Small to make a pre-
sent of £lOOO to one Botany winner of the period, and
has heard that he acted even more liberally with others.
Smail’s figure was familiar at the corner of litt and
Market streets for some years until a few days before his
death. „ „
* * * *
There is a general impression that if the
unsophisticated child of the bush comes into the city he
is as clay in the hands of the potter when the smart city
gentleman gets hold of him. But there is a crowd of
eminently respectable gentlemen (by Act of 1 arliament
and—some of them—by design of God) who now hold a
different opinion. It happened this way A number of
bushmen came down from (say) the Northern Territory
to arrange for (say) an alligator-riding show in Sydnej%
Then someone said that the chief rider tied his legs
under the alligator’s belly, and the boss sacked him for
getting such things said about himself, and a row took
place over money. Later on the angry, unsophisticated
bushmen met in the office of a quiet, eminently-respeet-
able, church-going solicitor to settle matters, but instead
of listening to him they drew revolvers, used horrible
language, and threatened to blow one another’s livers
out. Why they should have concentrated their attention
on the liver is a mystery, unless it is large and not sq
easily missed. The solicitor slid out of his chair on to the
floor, passed hurriedly under the table, and left the office,
unobserved, on all fours. Outside in the passage he up :
ended himself and rushed into the offices of brother soli-
citors, and begged them to come and help him disarm the
lawdess ruffians who were threatening to spoil his carpet
with one another’s blood. But the legal gentlemen, with'
one accord, hurled themselves out of their rooms and
took refuge across a little wooden bridge that led to a
lavatorv, and shouted loudly for an axe with which to
destroy the bridge, and keep the awful bushmen away.
Then the solicitor heard a crash of furniture, and thought
of his bric-a-brac: This was an outrage on the rights of
proputty. His gore rose. He rushed across the bridge in
a howling fury, and burst into his office. “Confound
your impudence, you (dash) hogs. Stop it this moment,
or I’ll get you all 10 years. Curse you ! look at my carpet,
look at my pictures.” Ringing the telephone: “That
the police station ? Send down 10 constables; two howl-
ing lunatics in my office fighting with revolvers. Hurry,
or I’ll be murdered, and all my pictures, destroyed.—lt’s
all right, th’ey’ve gone.” And so they had.
There is a sensible glaze on the surface of the
drafted rules for governing the world’s sculling champion-
ship. The stake for future championship events will be
not less than £5OO a-side, and the “ gate-money ” will be
shared out even to the last penny ; while the holder of
the title must accept a challenge within three months,
and be prepared to race within six months in Australia or
nine months in another country from the time the chal-
lenge is issued. The decision that, should the champion
retire or die, the immediate ex-champion will pick up the
title, simplifies matters a great deal.
* * * *
War in Sydney ponydom rages as fiercely as
ever, and reduced prices attract bigger crowds, including
people who never went to pony meetings before. Ascot
Club, whose genesis caused the trouble, boasts of making
considerable money. All the same, associated clubs keep
on giving rich prizes and tipping that they’ll have the
business to themselves again soon.

The question of the value of the marriage
or birth certificate on which melodramatists so
confidently rely turned up last week, in Parra-
matta (N.S.W.) Police Court. An elderly com-
mission agent, who said he made about £1 a
week (there is a world of faded respectability in
being an elderly commission agent and making on
an average £1 a week), was sued for maintenance
by a lady, who said she was his long-lost wife.
The separation had lasted over 18 years, and at
the end of that long time she wanted her faded
apnt returned to her. The defendant replied
that he once possessed a wife who drank, but had
lost the run of her. ITe had heard of her death
many years ago, and had no reason till now to
suppose she wasn’t dead. He didn’t recognise the
applicant (who wanted £2 a week maintenance)
from a .herd of wild door-posts. She might be his
wife or she might not. But what he revolted
against was the supposition that she must be
nis wife because she had his wife’s marriage
Certificate in her hand. And just there—whatever
jnay.be the merits of the case otherwise—the
rather faded man kicked against a superstition
that badly wants to be kicked. On the stage the
possession of the certificate is always accepted
without hesitation as proof of the identity of the
lost wife, yet it would be just as logical to assume
that the cow was the lost relative because it had
the certificate impaled on its horn, or that the
villain had been married in early life to the door
because the certificate was gummed on the door.
The same objection arises when an aged person
dies in the Home for the Decayed and Venerable,
leaving under his pillow a yellow document setting
forth that Michael Grady was born at Roscommon
PL. , 8- That may suggest that the deceased was
Michael Grady, aged 108, but it doesn’t prove it
any more than the finding of the same paper
under a tank would prove that the tank was born
at Roscommon in 1798. There is a certain
amount of free drink and notoriety attached to
being 108 years old, and given the papers, which
are often easy enough to find, the borrowing of
the name is easy. When last heard from, the
Parramatta Court was thinking over these things
and had postponed its decision.
Messrs. Craig and Aitken, of 654 George-street
Sydney, record, with pardonable pride, that although
they have advertised Capillarine for over 20 years and
sold many thousands of pots they have never had a single
complaint during that period. Capillarine is advertised
to grow hair on any face. It is compounded of absolutely
harmless ingredients yet strong enough to give a man a
heavy, luxuriant moustache. Capillarine is sent under
sealed cover free for 10s. 6d.’'
Adelaide Local Court looked very hard at
“Statute 29 of Car. IL,” last week, and then
decided to ignore it. Defendant had engaged a
trap on Easter Sunday to take him to the Oak-
bank races next day, and when it came along it
was not (he said) fit to go the distance. When a
]<hw-suit arose out of the matter, he was advised
to strengthen his case by leaning on Charles IT.,
who forbade contracts to be made on a Sunday.’
The Court, however, presumably considered
that monarch a poor sort of person to lean on
(what Charles himself is leaning on now it is hard
to say but there are suspicions that it is some-
thing hot), and the hirer had to pay.
When Malcolm M‘Eacharn was Gaud
Mayor of Melbourne he entertained the Japs
with such zeal that they have ever since reckoned
Melbourne Town Hall the capital of Australia,
and the Gaud Mayor its Mikado. Little Mayor
Weedon tried hard last week to live up to the
M'Eacharn tradition, and though he failed he
and his fellow councillors provided an entertain-
ment that will live for years in the memories of
those who saw it. The Jap officers, despite their
boasted proficiency in English, are almost incom-
prehensible when they try to talk. Then Weedon
and Ins friends had the common English impres-
sion that any foreigner can understand the
British language if you yell loud enough, and they
bellowed like the fall of Jerusalem. It was better
than an old age pension to see a burly alderman,
with his eyes starting from his head, his face red
and convulsed, and his arms violently semaphor-
ing over the room, while he shouted into the ear
ot a little yellow statue that never moved a
muscle. All the Mayoral crowd helped them-
selves liberally with “You savee 1 ” and it is said
that one alderman several times called the
J apanese admiral ‘ ‘ John. ”

Winter Trips to North Queensland
*;•/" ' • v *:■. ’ A
’M-V
By Mag'nificent Steamships.
FOR the months of May, June, July, and August,
tourists or those seeking an equable climate and mag-
nificent scenery can be thoroughly recommended to
take the trip to Cairns, North Queensland. The scenery on
the way there and round about Cairns stands unrivalled. The
famous Cairns-Herberton Railway, a marvel of engineering
skill, runs from Cairns over the ranges past the celebrated
falls to Chillagoe, with its wonderful copper mines and lime-
stone caves. The Cairns-Mulgrave Tramway Line also
takes you through some typical Northern scenery. Cairns
is an ideal resort for the invalid, for the tourist, and pleasure-
seeker. Australia has not any winter trip to compare with
it. The round trip from Melbourne to Cairns and back can
be done in 27 days, Sydney to Cairns and back in 20 days,
Brisbane to Cairns and back in 14 days.
S.S. GABO.

SUCH is now the demand for BEALE AUSTRALIAN PIANOS
that within three years we have had twice to enlarge our factories. In two
years we have doubled our output. Our branch retail supply houses have
had to be increased in number to cope with the ever-growing demand for BEALE
PIANOS. We have established branches in every State of the Commonwealth,
notwithstanding energetic antagonism by foreign agents. From all parts of Aus-
tralia, Tasmania, New Zealand, from the Islands of the Pacific, India, South Africa,
Mauritius, and even Japan, inquiries and orders for BEALE AUSTRALIAN
PI AN OS continue to pour in upon us. This wonderful rise in popular favor has never
had a precedent in the music trade anywhere in the world. We have always relied for
our success upon the solid merit ot the goods we offer, and therefore our chief aim has
ever been Superiority in Material, Workmanship, System of Construction, and
Finish. It was in order to gain control of these essentials that we started manu-
facturing in Australia, and, once having begun, we have without check gone on to
our present position as the manufacturers of the HIGHEST GRADE PIANOS
IN THE WORLD. COME and INSPECT the Instruments for yourself. We
invite comparison with any imported pianos. Your verdict will be the same as
that of nearly seven thousand people in every department of life, who have pur-
chased from us, and scores of thousands of their friends who have seen and heard
the BEALE AUSTRALIAN PIANOS.
We have been granted Patents in England, France, and other manufacturing countries, and Australasia, but it is always accounted a special certificate of merit
to obtain a patent in Germany. In the German Empire it is a criminal offence, punishable by fine and imprisonment, to use another man’s patented invention. The
Government, therefore, is particularly careful in investigating the merits of inventions in respect of originality, priority, and efficiency, before granting patents which
protect to such an extent. After cautious, special inquiries which we had to answer, the Imperial German Government granted us
LETTERS PATENT for our Steel Tuning' System.
Complete Pianos may be seen at any of our Showrooms or small specimen sections will be sent by post to any intending purchaser. There are now thousands
of Pianos with these patent wrest-planks in use with families in stock, and in course of manufacture. During months there is no change in them whatever. All the
expected advantaged have been attained, and some which were not anticipated.
If you want a Piano which will last a lifetime and can always be kept at a proper pitch, as a true musical instrument should be, then we invite you to inspect and test the
BEALE PIANOS
before buying elsewhere. They are made in your own climate to withstand the
climate, of the best materials, by thoroughly competent workmen. We give
guarantee and submit proofs which you cannot obtain elsewhere. Why not give
Britons a chance, instead of demanding the label “ Made in Germany V We sell
800 Pianos a year in this State, and there would be no sense in making them here
at all unless we made them better, because we, can get all the labor and skill at
much lower wages in Germany. We use the same kind of mechanism as Julius
Bluthner, made by the same supplier. We use the same kind of steel strings as
Carl Bechstein, from the same supplier. Those two eminent makers may be
reckoned as the best in their own country. Yet we claim to be better situated—
with our patents and with access to a better timber market (California) —to supply
Australian buyers direct from factory to consumer. Our workshops are large and
new, fitted with steampipes throughout, and the most modern electrically-driven
machinery and apparatus. The climate is all in our favor. In Germany, for
many months in the year, the workshops are tightly closed, whilst often icicles
hang from the roof. Inside there is a close atmosphere at about 62deg. The
rooms are moist with* breath and the steam from glue-pots. The Pianos are
jointed up, sounding boards and all, in such conditions, inevitably. Then in the
same atmosphere they are finished and packed. Yet in three months’ time such
pianos must withstand, if they can an, Australian open-air, dry temperature of
llOdeg. in the shade. Which is likely to endure those conditions best ? Pianos
made in Germany or Pianos made here by skilled workmen from those very shops,
with adequate experience, and every possible appliance at hand, in this climate ?
We have our own foundries and ironshops, adjuncts that until recently not one
single German Piano-maker possessed. Thus alone can full control of the quality
of the metal parts be attained. Even the very pins and screws are made in our
works, on automatic machinery, which is not the case with any Piano-maker in
Europe.

“Lil” writes:—
What a pitiful sight some of the anti-Socialistic dames
present as they speak their pieces at a meeting. If they
could only see a biograph reproduction of the perform-
ance they would never ascend a platform again. Often
enough most of the lady orator’s voice has oozed with her
courage and memory before she rises. She stands staring
with frightened eyes, just able to mumble nervously after
some politician who is prompting her —“ Home purity—
marriage tie —domination of the Labor party—dangerous
apathy—-all women vote.” Then she totters nervously
back to her chair. Heaven only knows what frightful
calamity the politician aforesaid must have prophesied to
make the poor misguided woman face such torture.
Representatives of trades-unions and em-
ployers’ associations are meeting in conference in
Sydney to discuss the Arbitration Act. Labor
wants Capital, as a preliminary, to admit the
principle of the peaceful settlement of disputes by
arbitration, and so far Capital has refused. This
leaves no doubt as to which is the party of law and
order.
Still another demand for a send-off :
Dear Bulletin.—l should like to enlist the sympathy
of your readers in the case of a promising youth who is
studying for the bookmaking profession. He has learnt
all that local professors of his art can teach him, and if a
sufficient' und can be acquired to enable him to study
paddock voice-production under its leading European ex-
ponents—Bellot, of Paris, and Bull, of London—l feel
confident that his pure basso racecourse voice will
yet do Australia credit. I hope to interest some
of our leading citizens in arranging a benefit race
meeting (at which the juvenile genius will appear)
for this purpose. One reason why Moses Bull-
doza (my young friend) wishes to go to Europe is his
desire to find out whether Continental experts can pos-
sibly improve on the fine shop-betting methods of our
Australian professors, which he has already thoroughly
grasped. A meeting of citizens will be held in the Town
Hall next Monday, when a committee to further the
above-mentioned objects will be formed. Mo (as his in-
timate friends call him) humorously says that if only a
few hundred citizens would try and back dead Tins with
him at an ordinary meeting, he would not need the bene-
fit. But may we not hope that Moses will thus be given
what he calls a “ thkinner,” even in conjunction with a
benefit meeting? Yours faithfully, Terence O’Fipp, sec.,

Public Notice.
The Clyde Engineering
Company, Limited
(Incorporated under the Companies Act).
INCREASE OF CAPITAL.
OURTHER Capital being required to purchase addi-
* tional plant, machinery, tools, and material in con-
nection with the contract for building 60 Locomotives for
the Government, the Directors have decided to offer to
the public the balance of the unalloted shares at 20/-
each, payable 10/- per share on application and 10/- on
allotment.
Shares already issued and on which 20/-
per share has been paid in cash .. 75,479
Shares now offered to the public at 20/- 24,521
Making Total Capital .. .. £lOO,OOO
The 24,521 Shares will carry a Preferential Dividend at
the rate of 6 per cent, per annum.
The Assets, as per Balance-sheet for
1905, amount to £117,617 19 5
The Liabilities, as per Balance-sheet for
1905, amount to 30,000 15 9
£87,617 3 8
The Land and Buildings have been
valued by Richardson and Wrench,
Ltd. ; and the Plant, Machinery, and
Tools by Norman Selfe, M.1.0.E.,
M.I.M.E. The valuations show a
sum in excess of book values of .. £43,662 17 6
Total Assets in excess of Liabilities .. £131,280 1 2
The New Capital would further increase
the Assets by 24,521 0 0
£155,801 1 2
The amount of Profit available for future
dividends is .. * £12,138 3 8
The Dividends paid during the past 7 years average
81 per cent.
Although the business is well established, and the pros-
pects of extension most promising, no premium is asked
for goodwill.
The Contracts, amounting to about £380,000 at present
in hand, should keep the works profitably employed for
some years.
Further particulars may' be obtained at the Company's
Office, Mutual Life of New York Building, (2nd Floor),
Martin-place, Sydney.
Applications for Shares addressed to the Secretary till
May 31 will be dealt with in the order received.
JOHN J. WOOD, Secretary.
Legal Notice.
GRACE COULSTOCK, Deceased.—Pursuant to an Order
of the Chancery Division of the High Court of
Justice, dated 12th day of February, 1906, and made in
the matter of the ESTATE of GRACE COULSTOCK,
Deceased, Sarah Annie Springett (Spinster), and Wilson
Stuckey plaintiffs, Thomas Coulstock defendant (1906,
C 267), whereby the following enquiry is directed : An
enquiry who were the persons entitled by virtue of or
according to the Statute of Distribution of Intestates’
Estates to the estate of the above named testatrix, Grace
Coulstock, living at the time of her death, and whether
any of them are since dead, and, if so, who are their
respective legal personal representatives. Notice is
Hereby Given, that any person or persons claiming to be
next-of-kiri of the said Grace Coulstock, or the legal per-
sonal representatives of next-of-kin, are, by their solici-
tors, on or before the 24th day of October, 1906, to come
in and prove their claims at the Chamber of Mr. Justice
Kekewich and Mr. Justice Joyce, at the Royal Courts of
Justice, Strand, London, England ; or in default thereof
they will be peremptorily excluded from the benefit of the
said order. Friday, the 26th day of October, 1906, at
twelve o’clock at noon, at the said Chamber (Room No.
267), is appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated this 3rd day of April, 1906.
WILLIAM BINNS SMITH,
Master of the Supreme Court.
Note. —The said testatrix, Grace Coulstock, died a
spinster, on the 10th March, 1905, and was late of
Brighton, in the county of Sussex.
NASH, FIELD, and CO., 12 Queen-street, London, E.C.,
England, Solicitors for Plaintiffs.


“ W.P.8.” on a personal matter :
You should be sued for libel. On the front page of
issue 3'5/’O6 I recognise Deakin, but who are the other
men ’ ’ I don’t know them well enough to follow any
resemblance, but I know Deakin well. Deakin s not a
bad-looking chap, and 1 think you might give him a
better-looking face than that—it is quite criminal (the
face, I mean). I thought it was a picture of Deeming at
first You make a great error in showing such faces to
the universe if you want to encourage immigration, No
one will come out here if he thinks he’s going to see faces
like that blotting the landscape. Also, in spite of
barracking (for explanation of this term vide Aborigi-
nalities” column) like Hades for Australian defence, you
decry the soldier bloke and give him a face as bad, if not
worse, than Deakin’s. It isn’t fair.
But that isn’t what I was talking about, and maybe you
will put me down as irrelevant. I’m not. I started
about Deakin, and I’m coming to him again in a nnnute.
I want you to understand he is a friend of mine. 1 hat s
how I knew his picture on the front page. Leave him
alone or give him a decent face, and also give that soldier
man a chance. Not that I’m very particular about the
soldier man, but don’t forget about Deakin. Make him
better-looking next time. He’s not such a bad chap, and
can be quite firm in his views. 1 know him, that s how I
recognised him on the front page.

■A- TRIUMPH lIXT ULTIMO.
The Case of Mr. A. Walker.
(By a Special Reporter.)
In all matters affecting the well-being of the community it is our duty to exercise every effort
and influence in the direction of promoting a still happier state of things, and if we only utilise the
chances that offer of helping our fellow-man we shall do much to brighten the hearth of many a home.
In this direction such assistance as that given by Mr. Alfred Walker, of No. 68 Macarthur-street,
Ultimo, is most valuable, for it deals with a subject which claims the attention of everybody.
Answering a reporter, Mr. Walker said: —
“I served my apprenticeship to engineering in Yorkshire, and have been at the trade all my life,
a large proportion of which has been spent in this country, for I came here in the year 1876.”
“ Do you find the climate agree with you ?” was asked.
“ Pretty well, but about six years ago I had very bad luck indeed, which was brought about in
this way. My work had taken me to a place situated between Douglas Park and Picton, where I had
an engine and boiler to repair. In putting those things to rights I put my health all wrong, for whilst
I was on that work I lived in a tent, and caught a severe cold, which went right through me. For two
or three months I could not get rid of it, and in the meantime the cause of much suffering had been
taking root, for rheumatism attacked me so badly that my legs, arms, and shoulders were almost use-
less to me. And the pain ! I can’t find words to describe it. My neck, too, was so stiff and sore that
I could hardly move it, and by degrees I got so infirm and helpless that I had to lie up altogether for
several weeks.”
“ Which would mean considerable financial loss to you ? ”
“My word it did ! but that was nothing compared to the misery I was in. My right shoulder got
worse and worse, and my sleep became scarcer than ever. I had become so cross and restless that I
could not settle myself down to sleep at all satisfactorily until about midnight. Then I would sleep
anddream till two or three o’clock, wake up suddenly, and then there was no more rest for me till the
next night. But these were not my only trials. There was one very important drawback, which was
that I could not relish any kind of food owing to my appetite having left me in the lurch long ago.
When I ate it was more by force of will than anything else—a determination to keep up my strength as
well as possible under the adverse circumstances ; but there was a stronger element than my own will
at work, and so my strength dwindled away in spite of me. The internal derangements that had got
hold of me were having a detrimental influence in every conceivable way. My eyes felt twice their
proper size, and my sight got bad, for when I started to read I’d soon have to give up, all the print
seeming to run together, and nearly every day of my life I was persecuted with the most violent
headaches. Oh ! it was an existence deserving of compassion, and I pray to God I’ll never experience
the like again. It was strange how giddy I used to get, too, sometimes so severely that I felt quite sick;
but, at that time, I was an awfully bilious subject, and it took nothing to upset me. I would only
require to take two or three mouthfuls of food when a feeling came to the stomach as if churning was
going on. The chest also had a stifling feeling about it, which made me short winded, while about
the back, just across the lower part here, the aches were something dreadful. It would be almost
impossible to endure all this without the nerves giving way, and I assure you mine broke down with a
vengeance. In point of fact, I was a complete wreck at the time a friend of mine came to see how I.
was doing, and strongly advised me to give Clements’ Tonic a trial.”
“ What medicine had you taken beforehand ? ”
“Well, I had been under the treatment of several doctors, each of whom paid me great attention
and prescribed medicine, but for all the effect it had I might just as well have taken so much water.
Seeing it was useless continuing with them, I tried my luck with many of the medicines I saw adver-
tised, only to meet with mere failures. Yes, dozens and dozens of bottles of physic I took before
Clements’ Tonic was recommended to me, and yet everything was left for the last remedy to do. I
may as well tell you that some of the chemists tried to palm their own mixtures, but I said ‘ No; 1
have spent enough money already, I’ve been advised to take Clements’ Tonic, and that is what I am
going to have.’ My word, I was soon thankful I had acted on the advice given, for after using a few
bottles of Clements’ Tonic I felt so much better that I hardly knew myself.”
“ In what respect did you notice improvement ? ”
“When Clements’ Tonic had had a fair chance I experienced benefits in many directions, but
what I was particularly thankful for at the time was a distinct falling off in the rheumatic tortures
that all other medicines had been incapable of touching. It was marvellous. Naturally, I was
encouraged to persue this latest treatment of mine, and, by-and-bye, I could hardly restrain myself
with the joy I felt at the progress made. I soon started taking my food as eagerly as if nothing had
ever been the matter with me, and in due time, to my surprise, I found that my internal organs were
actually performing their proper work again, which meant that I gained entire freedom from the old
tortures of indigestion, and began putting on flesh again. It was a new thing for me to retire to rest
at night and not wake up again till morning, and that’s what I have to thank Clements’ Tonic for. It
was also new to rise from bed with a clean tongue in place of the fur which had formerly covered it,
and no headache to distress me or no giddy turns during the day, and I assure you I often thanked
God that at last I had got a remedy which could restore vigor to my enfeebled system, and steadiness
and strength to my nerves. Yes ; these are some of the good things Clements’ Tonic did for me, but
I need not enumerate them all, as it is sufficent for me to say that my rheumatic pains were thoroughly
driven away, and I’ve never had a touch of biliousness or indigestion since.”
“Not for six years ? ”
“That’strue, and if my story will help other afflicted people you may publish it in any form
you please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, ALFRED WALKER, of No. 68 Macarthur-street, Ultimo, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, consisting of five
folios and consecutively numbered from one to five, and that it contains, and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements’ Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements —which I give voluntarily without receiving any payment; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) ALFRED WALKER.

WORK WAS A PLEASURE
To Mr. Sullivan—He foil ill of Indigestion
and Mother Seigel’s Syrup Cured Him.
“The sweetest thing in all the world is love,
And, next to love, the sweetest thing is hate.”
We never quite agreed with this couplet of
Longfellow’s. The first line may pass, but at the
second we kick vigorously. It is doubtless a
satisfaction to some natures to have their foot on
their enemy’s throat. But surely that is not the
next sweetest thing to love, and it would be
sweeter to have no enemy at all. Had the poet
written :
The sweetest thing in all the world is health
(health invites love, and leads the way to wealth)
we would have agreed with him heart and soul.
But he didn’t. He left that for us to do, and un-
fortunately our power of poetic expression falls
somewhat below that of Longfellow. However,
we stand by our opinion, and could back it up by
many practical illustrations, but shall employ
•only one, that of Mr. John Sullivan, of 140,
-Campbell-street, Surry Hills, Sydney, N.S.W.
For years Mr. Sullivan was as healthy, strong
and active as any man in the land. “I am a
wood-block layer by trade.” Mr. Sullivan stated
on sth May, 1905. “ Work used to be a pleasure
to me, and I took pride in out-pacing all my fellow
wood-blockers. I could sleep like a top, and my
■appetite would have scared any boarding-house
keeper. But about five years ago a change
gradually crept over me. From being smart and
active I became dull and heavy. After eating, I
felt as if a big knot had been tied in my stomach,
or I had swallowed an inflated football. Every
week I grew worse until it was as much as I could
do to struggle through the day’s task. At night I
could not sleep unless I dosed myself with some
powerful stimulant, and the morning would
generally find me with a bad taste in the mouth,
my tongue thickly coated with fur, and with no
appetite for breakfast. After eighteen months of
this I was on the verge of complete breakdown,
and dwindled away to a shadow of my former self.
My mother, who for years had been in the habit
of taking Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, urged
me to give that medicine a trial; but I had
always dreaded doctors and physic, and it was
long before I could be persuaded to do so. How-
ever, at last I resolved to try a course of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup and Operating Pills, with the
happy result that by the time I had taken half-a-
dozen two-and-sixpenny bottles of the Syrup and
four boxes of the Pills I could eat without suffer-
ing subsequent pain, and sleep naturally, unaided
by narcotics. In short, my health and strength
were thoroughly restored, and I have remained
well to this day.”

The Scientific Cure for Indigestion.
From Mr. James Joshua, Oatherine-street, Leichhardt, Sydney, N.S.W., 6th March, 1905.
“ For fully a year I had suffered acutely from indigestion, headache and biliousness. I could
scarcely eat anything solid without having to endure great pain. My rest at night was greatly
disturbed, and this made me irritable. I was under medical treatment for some time, but derived no
real benefit from it. Having heard of the good effects of Warner’s Safe Cure, in the cases of other
sufferers, I decided to give it a trial. After taking the first two bottles I felt considerably better, and
a few more bottles thoroughly cured me. lam now in the best of good health.”
From Mr. W. H. Sampson, Haydonton, Murrurundi, N. S. W., 11th January, 1906.
“I suffered very much from indigestion for a considerable time through always being in the
bush, and, of course, living on rough food. Many a time I sat on the edge of my bunk at night unable
to lie down, the pain being so great in the pit of my stomach. I felt as if there was a great lump
there. Hearing that Warner’s Safe Cure was a cure for indigestion, I took a few bottles, with the
result that all pain left me and I was again able to eat, sleep and work well. Some of my mates also

Business, Robbery, etc.
Meg” : The decision of Justice Hood, re-
cently acted on by the Port Melbourne bench, that all
time-payment sales are absolute after first payment, has
hit the piano shark hard. One of these gentry takes the
loaf and all the rest of the food for meanness. Girls are
engaged as apprentices at ss. a week, with increases loom-
ing in the dim beyond. One of the conditions is that
each girl must buy a piano on the time-payment system,
so as to become a proficient pianist in the future. The
benevolence of this idea is somewhat discounted by a
stipulation that half the wages go as weekly payments on
the box of whistles. After the budding Paderewski has
got up to 15s. a week (7s. 6d. for the piano), she is sud-
denly found incompetent. Dismissal leaves her without
a situation, and with a piano that she can’t pay for. So
the implement is humped back by carrier to the ware-
house, to be sold to some other guileless maiden, and the
boss gets his piano and servant for next to nothing. I
heard of one girl who got even. She had paid about
£lO off, when the usual dismissal came. But her Adonis
was a clerk in a solicitor’s office, and knew a thing or
two. She at once sold him the piano for £5, and spent
the money on her trousseau. There was a fearful inter-
view with Herr Spreckelstein. Lord knows what will
become of the other 50 pianos sold in a similar way when
the time-paymenters find out that they are sole proprie-
tors and only liable as debtors for the balances. The
prospect of recovering the value of the machine by suing
a discharged infant-in-the-eye-of-the-law who never had
more than 7s. 6d. a week to bless herself with at the
best of times is too dreadful.
• * * *
“Sano”: Do painters always charge £10? I
wanted some painting done, so called in Smith and asked
him what it would cost to have two rooms painted. Smith
looked round and said it would cost £lO. Happened to
pass Brown’s, another painter’s, and thought to get
another estimate, adding the hall to the two rooms.
Brown offered to do it for £lO. I was getting interested,
and consulted Jones about painting two rooms, the hall,
and the back wall. Said Jones, after inspection : “ I’ll
have to charge you £10.” In the end I got the whole
house done for £lO, but I firmly believe that if I had only
got the dog-kennel kalsomined it would have cost £lO
just the same.
* * * *
Women —or the men who pay for them—are
sinfully victimised in their drapery purchases. Take,
for illustration, the two old-established London drapery
stores of Peter Robinson and Jay’s, now run as limited
companies. Peter Robinson’s latest dividend was 18 per
cent., and the Jay’s balance-sheet records ah increase in
the net profits (over last year’s) of something like £9OOO.
The British aristocracy of the near future will be mainly
composed of the descendants of pushing softgOodsmen
who sold the shop to a co., leavened by a number of
impoverished blue-bloods who are seeking riches in the
bonnet-and-costume business. The brewer will have to
make way o>r the draper in upper circles. Men are drink-
ing less begr than of yore in order that the women may
buy more ribbons and things.
* * * *
“P.G.H.” : Apropos the recent row as to
whether made-in-Australia hats are ever branded “ Made
in England.” Rubbish! Every experienced hat-man
knows ic is done—if not by one firm, then by others. I
have handled hats for over a dozen years, and know that
Australian goods are frequently fitted with imported
leathers and linings branded “ Made in England.” I have
handled one lot branded “Made in U.S.A.” I don’t
blame the maker or retailer (naturally perhaps) as much
as Ido the public. The shopman doesn’t lead ; he only
reflects. All the same, I would give the fraudulent-
marks man gaol. I would even build him a special gaol
all to himself if necessary, with dark cells and skilly in it.
The romance of Australian gold mine flotation
did not begin, as is held, with McGregor, or Hargraves,
or any of the commonly cited celebrities, but with Dan
Daly when Botany Bay was still in swaddling clothes.
Dan, who had come out in irons for trying to pass bad
bronze coins in England, fell in love with Jane Reeby,
who was supposed to have purloined handkerchiefs
from the pockets of spectators of an execution at
the Old Bailey. The pair after a short time married in
Sydney, and Dan grubbed and Jane scrubbed
for a living. A longing to get back to England
seized both, but Daly’s head was a very small and soft
one, if the history of his plan of return is to be credited.
He thought to take down hard old Captain Phillip with a
few brass shavings melted with copper and embedded in
granite. He maintained the stuff was gold, that he knew
where mountains of it were to be found, and he offered
to tell the Governor where the Golconda was if the latter
would pardon him and his wife and send them back to
England. Phillip, who had never seen gold in conjunction
with rock, and who had nobody about him able to
analyse metals, showed doubt. Daly, to convince him,
alleged he had sold some of the stuff to an expert
on board the Golden Grove convict ship then lying in
the harbor. “Who is the man?” demanded Phillip.
“ A black-complexioned man,” was the only reply. But
there was no black-complexioned man on board the
Golden Grove. Phillip ground his teeth. He called
twenty men. “Daly,” he said, “show these men the
mountain of gold forthwith.” Daly set forth. After a
ten miles’ walk he gave the men the slip, and, returning
to camp, told the Deputy-Governor that he had settled
the twenty'on a junk of gold, but wanted another twenty
to help them to carry it. There was great commotion at
this, twenty men being immediately ordered to start.
But they had only just started when the first twenty
came back. Daly was flogged till he acknowledged that
he was a liar, though it took 300 lashes to clear up the
matter. He was hanged soon after for another offence.
That was the first wild-cat flotation, and it ended, where
many cats begin, up a tree.
* * * m
“Wal. D.” : I feel as savage as a meat-axe.
Just sent a messenger to a leading grocer s shop for a
bottle of chutney to help the cold meat down. Told him
to ask for Australian. He came back with the reply that
they only stocked English, price Is. 9d. I thereupon
made a personal onslaught on the establishment, and
after a well-directed volley of comment, discovered that
South Australian chutney was stocked, price 6£d., and
very good stuff, too. I liked it better than the Cold
Country product, but I did feel D—d Wild that a Arm
dependent upon S.A. for its business should hide the
State’s products from customers, to try and force the
sale of foreign substitutes at three times the price.
* * * *
“T. O’Fipp ” : Why this rage for tagging bits
of dead language on to articles of clothing ? My hat is
branded with the words “ Dum spiro spero,” and a
heraldic device that looks like a poor-conditioned hen
waving a gum-branch. (It is also ambiguously marked
“London and Melbourne —English finish,” though I have
every reason to believe it is Australian-made.) When I
go to buy collars and shirts I sometimes find them re-
marking “ Domine dirige nos,” or “ Multum in parvo,” or
something else out of a dictionary appendix. Even my
trouser-buttons are stamped with “Tenax propositi,”
and when I glanced at my new boots, sure enough they
were of the “ Ora pro nobis ” brand. I hate to be dressed
in all these dead languages. It feels like wearing a hanged
man’s clothes.
THE CALL OF THE SEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Who are the men that rattle in this sort
The sounding kerosene-tin, this dull noon,
In this far-inland suburb ? What their boon ?
Mussels they vend, one penny for a quart!
Gods ! that once more like them I could resort
Where I first saw the waves beneath the moon—
Yea, even to Port Melbourne’s spiced lagoon,
Where lurks the shell-fish on its pier-pile fort.
I! A shop-walker ! Even Blank and Oo.’s!
Thus to be—This way, madam ! —envying
A common hawker ? Yearning thus to chuck
Well-creaskd pants and superfine half-hose,
And hunt coarse mussels ! Ay, and even to sling
You, chaste frock-coat, for moleskins and a truck !
vie.
TERENCE O'FIPP.
FALSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw a Face a-shining in the dark.
I stooped ; I said, “ Ah, damn !” and flung it down.
False it was—false and leering like a shark ;
Bloodless, lifeless—a bad half-crown.
N.s.w.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.

“Viator”: The San Francisco Overland
Monthly last year had a soheme by a lady for
reconstructing San Francisco. Curious how
writers alight at times on such uncanny things—
not exactly prophecy in this case, but perhaps a
little of the sub conscious. Anyhow, here is the
chance. Wren had a most sensible plan for
rebuilding London after the fire, but the stupid
citizens insisted on a reproduction of their old
Strand, Cheapside, and other cracks and alleys.
A few months ago, one Dr. Stark, a
Yankee tooth merchant in Launceston (Tas.), was
making laughing gas, when an explosion took
place that was no laughing matter at all. The
surgery was blown to atoms, and the doctor
escaped by the tails of his coat. After this he
invented something—perhaps the extraction of
teeth by dynamite—and started for ’Frisco to
take out a patent. Another Yankee molar-
man, his partner or something, went before
him. The partner reached ’Frisco in time for
the earthquake, but was saved with his
womenkind. The man who had been blown up
in Launceston turned up at the Golden Gate
fifteen days too late to be blown up again, but
in time to discover the rest of the firm waiting
for him among the ruins.
The London magistrate’s decision that most
whisky isn’t whisky, and that nothing is whisky
that isn’t pot-still whisky, hasn’t, pending an
appeal, knocked out the market in whisky-
distillery shares. The difference between the
whisky that is alleged not to be whisky and pot-
still whisky, which is whisky, is the "difference
between all-malt spirit and the other sort —grain
spirit, potato spirit, %c. Ali-nialt whisky is made
from malted barley ; the best of the other whisky
(“grain whisky”) is three-parts the pro-
duct of unmalted stuff, and is made in
a patent, instead of a pot, still. There is
very little all-malt whisky on the market,
the output is limited, so what there is isn’t adver-
tised, and practically no well-known brand is all-
malt. And it wouldn’t have much hope of win-
ning popularity, anyhow ; for even the caSe-
hardened whisky drinker generally finds it too
heavy for his digestion. Probably no jury would
ever agree on the ideal whisky; but a majority
verdict would most likely be obtained for a blend
of, say, 33£ per cent, of pot-still and 66§ per
cent, of old, matured and unadulterated grain
spirit, which the London magistrate says isn’t
whisky. It is the new, raw grain or potato spirit
that is Sheol.
An ex-M.L.A. (probably he is dead now)
was once sold out under a court judgment, and
among the goods seized by the bailiff were thirty
Government blankets branded as for the use of
aborigines. Obtaining blankets for the aborigines
was once a thriving Parliamentary industry, some
members drawing as many as 200 pairs in the
course of a winter.

A Magazine for Elderly Ladies was a
printer’s idea discussed in Sydney during the
week. Saturday’s Herald, however, was found
to fill the bill.
In Freetrade England! A woman the
other day told the Lambeth (Eng.) guardians that
she “finished” volunteer trousers for payment
which worked out at less than a penny an hour,
and investigation showed the story to be true.
It was found that for years the woman had risen
at 3 o’clock in the morning, worked in her room
till seven, and then gone to the factory, where
she was engaged until half-past seven at night.
She worked also on Sundays, and had never
earned more than 9s. a week. To add to the
tragedy of it, she was 63 years of age. Asked by a
Daily Express interviewer what she had for
dinner, she replied: “Anything I can get fora
penny. Sometimes a bit of bread and some
water. I always have a cup of tea at tea-time,
though.” And the old face brightened at the
thought of that solitary luxury.
“Melb.” : Miss Thorne is here, with her
consumptive cure. She was a medical student at
Sydney University, and propounded it. The
patient inhales hot, dry medicated air to kill the
microbes. The idea was somewhat laughed out
of Sydney, but Miss Thorne went to England.
Her apparatus is now used in Brompton Hospital.
She is going to Berlin. Meanwhile the Vic.
Board of Health will experiment with her cure at
the Eehuca Sanatorium.
Melbourne Australasian published this
item recently :
The apotheosis of the slave trade was revealed in a case
(in New York, U.S.A.) where a negro had for years re-
cruited the vicious dens of his own race with young white
girls, abducted from the vicinity of schools, and kept im-
prisoned in depdts until their degradation was complete.
Shocking, certainly. Yet the Australasian still
prints articles asserting that a White Australia is
impossible and undesirable. If it only looked up
the Court records of its own city it would
find that cases in which girls of school
age have been debauched by loathsome
colored aliens are by no means uncommon.
It only needs enough nigger to make this horror
an everyday occurrence in Australia, and it is no
fault of the Australasian that there isn’t
enough nigger. Meanwhile the paper shows its
strict impartiality by being very angry at the de-
praved nigger—in New York.

BRONCHITIS AND PLEURISY.
A SEVERE CASE CURED BY TWO BOTTLES OF
HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE.
AFTER OTHER TREATMENT HAD FAILED.
Mr. Hearne, Chemist. Geelong.
Dear Sir,—Some momms ago, in Sydney, I suffered from
a severe attack ol influenza, and was confined to my room
for about a week, at the end of which time, feeling some-
what better, I got up and tried to transact my business
as usual. But I got up too soon, for the very next day I
had a' relapse, and suffered tortures from what the doctor
told me was pleurisy and bronchitis. The pain from the
former in my chest and shoulders was frightful, and for
four long weeks I was confined to my bed, under the care
of a well-known Sydney doctor, and all the time his medi-
cine gave me hut temporary relief. The landlady of the
hotel (the Cleveland) where I resided told me of a medi-
cine—Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure—from Victoria, which had
cured her of a bad attack of bronchitis and pains in the
chest, and begged of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks
and gratitude to you, tell you that, after the second bottle,
my cough had ceased ; but, what is more astonishing, the
Fains from pleurisy entirely left me, and, in about a week,
was able to attend to my duties as usual.—Yours faith-
fully, J. BRAHAM,
Melbourne Punch Office, Melbourne.
