The Victor and the Voices,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE ocean sleeps to-night; I did not know
This world held half the peace that hovers
round ;
The wavelets whisper as they come and go
And the broad calm gives out no other sound.
Rest lies along the ocean like a cloud—
Far in the night belated fishers hail;
(Who called t) —Rest slumbers—what was that ?
the loud
And languid flap of some recumbent sail ?
lam victorious. Failures harassed me,
Despair felt at my heart, but could not rend
The harmony of power—l, languidly,
Glide down one song of peace unto the end.
The cry of a far liner’s whistle blown
Comes like a bird song at the break of day ;
Afar (Who called ?) the lamps of Williamstown
Throw trembling lines of light across the bay.
Thunder and God’s unutterable night,
The harrowing wrath of ages, the shrill cries
Of hungry children shuddering from the light,
Burden our world of fogs that never rise.
Along a fierce infernal world of woe,
Fast in the toils of commerce that enmesh
The soul and muscle, frayed by storm we go
With rusted fetters burning in our flesh.
Who spoke of silence ? Thunder and the deep
Immitigable darkness of long years !
How the still rapture of untroubled sleep
Hovers, askance, from our transcendent tears l
How can a man lie laughing by the sea
While at his back the world, violent, unjust,
Rides with its thundering hoofs o’er what might be
Fair blossoms of great hope and love and truth ?
Rest l These thin groping arms and faces white
With fear immortal, and these leaden eyes
Shame this calm moon, these quiet waves to-night,
To let the victor slumber with his prize !
Go back ! I have not thought of you for years,
You hungry spectres, blotting God’s fair plan ;
You hungry voices, bitter with all tears
Shed since the savage wars of class began.
Who sent you here ? I hoped for peace to-night;
Your voices, acrid with the world’s regret,
Bring back the ruinous times, and the black
blight
Of the old war ! Oh, can I not forget ?
Out of the track of the ships,
On an isle in the sunlit sea,
With red reluctant lips,
A girl is awaiting me.
Her voice is a melody,
The sun makes gold in her hair,
She loves my dreams and me,
And rest—( Who called ?)—is there.
Out of a chasm of withering nights, of regretful
Slow tears, our cries from this dark
Go, faint, to our f amishing star as niggardly , fret-
ful,
It glimmers—a spark.
We cry to her: “How shall our visions and his-
tories flower
In the grip of a strangling trance ?
We were all of us conquerors, girded with honor
and dower,
If the gods would give us a chance ?
Were rich with great triumph and laurels and
glory and power,
If the gods would give us a chance ! ”
When the sun came up I marched away
And whom did you leave behind ?
To where the world’s great chances lay,
And whom did you leave behind ?
And me from the million men they chose,
To crown with the victor’s wreath of rose ;
And little thy glutted spirit knows
Of the weak ones left behind 1
I was swift of foot and keen of eye,
And we were lame and blind !
And Envy shrieked as I passed by,
Envy was lame and blind !
I slew the Guardians of Success,
Fought failure in the wilderness ;
When will the braggart heart confess
He fought the lame and blind l
The morning lends my soul her wings,
The ocean gives me power;
My hand some drop of triumph wrings
From each successive hour !
Fate fights for me—for me hope sings.
And we, who fail, declare
There's something at the back of things
That is not fighting fair l
I dreamed that peace was his reward
Who struggled upward—yet
Fate sends the conquered, panting hard,
To teach me their regret.
I sought for peace in the morning light
By creeks that laugh and croon,
Through the golden day till the sky was white
With the coming of the moon ;
And I said to my heart in the silent night,
“ Will peace be coming soon ? ”
We sought for peace till the lowering sun
Made gold in the 'west lagoon ;
But women wept for things ill-done
Till Hope fell in a sivoon :
And men tramped past her, one by one,
Their world-cry blotted out the sun—
" Will peace be coming soon l"
VIC.
FURNLEY MAURICE.
The Humorist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE night was as still as the depths of
a valley. Behind the scrub-lined road
leading from Maroubra Bay came the
sound of breaking surf. A lamp shone clearly
from a pier end. A woman’s voice broke the
silence; it seemed to pierce the senses at first
with its shrill delight, then it grew faint as
though oppressed with pain.
Chinese lanterns swung from the verandah of a
red-tiled villa. A number of cabs lay half-con-
cealed in the shadows. A voice said “ Where is
Mrs. Ambrose ? What a night—what a beautiful
night! Someone is going to sing the Jewel Song.”
The first notes of Gounod’s Prelude floated
from the distant drawing-room. A young man
halted suddenly near the house palings, and
listened attentively to the words of the song.
“It is magnificent,” he whispered. “Satan put
his seal on the scales of the Jewel Song. I feel
like composing a hymn to the pawnbroker.”
With his hand on the house-gate, the young
man remembered that he was wearing his office
clothes. It also occurred to him that Mrs.
Ambrose was entertaining her friends. In her
last letter to him she had said something about a
fancy dress ball. He had just returned from
Melbourne by steamer. During the passage he
had been ill, and for two days the world and the
flesh were matters of less interest than the
villainous heave and roll of the steamer. A day
or two ashore found him wondering how he could
have lived a whole month cut off from the society
of Yenetia Ambrose.
He opened the gate stealthily, and approached
the rear of the house. Through orange and violet
colored panes he saw the shapes of men and
women in fancy costumes—toreadors, gondoliers,
Hamlets and Ophelias, with here and there an
Othello or a brigand—all out of joint, limping,
pouting, feeling their parts no more than wood or
ice.
Philip Atherton, in serge clothes, had no desire
to mingle with such a medley of color and
humorless vanity. He strolled disconsolately to an
obscure seat near a side door and waited. He
did not wait long. The questing eyes of Airnee,
the French maid, discovered him. There was a
flutter of ribbons in the hall and the flash of a
vanishing ankle. Then—Atherton breathed—a
rush of white silk and perfumed lace, of flowers
with the aroma of night and woman upon them.
She sat beside him with the breathless humility
of a saint. He observed an exquisite modesty in
her movements. She was like a nun approaching
a prince of the church. Yet her humility struck
him as a token of passionate obedience. Her
mood was more poetic than her beauty. And a
schoolgirl might have guessed that he was her
lover.
“ Dear, when did you come back?”
“Only yesterday. I ought to have written.
Does it matter?”
“Every moment of your absence matters,
Philip. When you are away lam seized with
moments of horrible despair.”
“Despair is the heritage of kings, Venetia.”
She shivered a little as he hissed her. Somewhere
in the illumined house a violin wailed a passionate
accompaniment. Between heartbeats Atherton
caught the slow rhythm of distantju^f; it sounded
intensely tragic. “Why shggj||HP u despair?”
he went on. “You have evif^OT^: —carriages,
horses, an income, and even a husb^pa.”
“Hush, Philip.” She held up her hand
protestingly. An utterable look stole into her
eyes.
“May I not mention him, ? Why does it annoy
you, Yenetia, whenever I speak of him ? ”
“Because he is dead,” she answered sharply.
“ Dead, and there is a mountain on his grave.”
He felt her almost crouch from his touch. A
shade of anger passed over him ; then he laughed.
“ When a woman wears black and speaks of her
husband in a low voice we know that he is dead ;
but when a lady faces the night in white silk and
heliotrope, and tells one that her dear brute is
dead—dead—dead, one supposes that he is renting
a villa for the other woman, Which death has
your husband preferred, Venetia?”
“For pity’s sake don’t irritate me, Phil. I
must go from here soon. The place is hateful.
Each day it becomes . . . Oh, pity me Phil,
pity me ! ” She was almost kneeling at his feet.
The dread of some terrible awakening moved in
her eyes. He had seen it in the faces of opium-
ravaged women.
“You are out of sorts,” he said kindly. “I
had better go. Shall I call your maid ? I will be
here to-morrow, early.”
“ Philip ! ”
“ I will call to-morrow, early,” he repeated
slowly. “You are excited, Venetia. These
fancy dress balls are bad for the nerves. You
cannot entertain Hamlet and Ophelia with im»
punity.”
The French maid appeared breathlessly in the
passage. “ Madame, ze gentlemen en ladies spik
of ze tableau. Zey look eferywhere for you.”
Mrs. Ambrose turned with a sudden gaiety of
manner. “Please don’t go until I return, Mr,
Atherton. Promise me.”
“ I promise. But you may tell the toreador in
your drawing-room that a bull has broken into the
back garden.”
The French maid broke into laughter. Ather-
ton watched then: depart, and re-lit his pipe. He
liked not the part he was playing in the life of
Mrs. Ambrose. A subtle fear of the absent hus-
band, who was alleged to be dead, unnerved him
at times. Someone had told him that Bindon
Ambrose was a sea captain and trader, who made
yearly trips to the Islands, where copra and pearl
still awaited the venturesome man of business.
Atherton had never met him, but in fancy he
pictured him—a raw, uncouth mariner with tat-
tooed arms and the chest of a baresark.
“Some day,” mused Atherton, “I shall meet
him. Then I suppose there will be trouble be-
tween the corpse and me.”
A cart jolting in the bye-lane awoke him from
his brooding. His pipe had gone out. The waltz
music and the sound of the dancers’ feet only
depressed him. The cart drew up at the side
gate. A Chinaman descended from the seat and
assisted a strange figure to the ground.
Atherton was not interested in the cart or the
Chinaman, but the faint tinkling of bells dis-
turbed him. A grotesque figure shambled
through the gate and down the path, and at each
step the bells jangled unmusically.
Atherton stooped forward and saw that the
quaint figure was attired in the costume of a court
jester. “ These fancy dress balls are the safety-
valves which allow a certain number of lunatics
to blow off steam,” he muttered.
A cloud drew across the moon. A thick shadow
swam over the house and grounds. It occurred to
Atherton that the jester was approaching some-
what unsteadily, after the manner of a marionette
or a belated drunkard.
The Chinaman watched the jester for a moment,
then returned to the cart and sat with his back to
the house. The bells tinkled nearer. The odd
figure tottered down the gravel walk until it stood
trembling and ringing in front of Atherton. The
latter did not speak; with his chin resting in
both hands he eyed the bell-capped visitor
sneeringly. “These are the servants’ quarters,”
he said coldly. “ Better ring yourself in at the
front door.”
The jester halted, and sighed wearily. “I
came . . . here to see Philip Atherton, not to eat
and dance.” His head wagged sorrowfully. He
looked back at the cart in the lane, and then
stared vacuously at the illuminated house.
“ name is Philip Atherton. What do you
want ?”
■ .Nothing.” The jester laughed faintly, and
then, with a half-heard whimper of pain, he sat
on the bench beside Atherton. And as he sat
with knees huddled and face averted, the other
grew rigid and breathless like a man with a
knife at his breast. Leaping to his feet he stared
at the swollen mask-like face of the jester, and his
knees grew soft as bread. “Are you . . . wear-
ing something ... or is that your real face ? ” he
asked.
“It was my faee before the devil mauled it with
his branding irons.”
‘ ‘ Who are you ? ” demanded Atherton. 1 ‘ Why
did you come here in a Chinaman’s cart ? ”
The jester looked surprised ; his cap tinkled
plaintively. “I am a humorist. I was a sour,
unlikeable fellow before the devil and I met.
Then I became a humorist.”
“ Have you ever made anyone laugh ? ” Ather-
ton remained standing. A feeling bitter as death
took him by the throat.
“Laugh! Even you may see this evening’s
joke when lam dead.” The jester touched him
lightly on the arm with his hooded stave. “It
will come to you suddenly in the night, and the
humor of it will rip your heart asunder as it
ripped mine. I planned it in my hut, down there
by the sea. The Chinaman helped else I would
never have got here.”
“Who are you ?”
The jester stooped as though concealing his face
from the sudden flood of moonlight. “My name
is Bindon Ambrose.”
Atherton’s teejth seemed as if they wanted to
chatter. “I suppose you are the husband of
Venetia Ambrose? Why don’t you wear decent
clothes?” he .said, thickly. “What is all this
clowning about? She said you were dead. How
was I to know it wore bells and consumed its own
laughter?”
He paced the gravel walk with the slow auger
of his kind. He did not ask himself where
Bindon Ambrose had been for the last few years.
The fool hud returned in the guise of a fool. Then
a thought sprang to his mind, leaving him cold
and sick. “ Why do you show yourself to me?”
he demanded. “Do you want to be revenged
because I love her ?”
The jester held up a finger and the bells tinkled
a childish accompaniment. “Revenge is the
essence of melodrama. Everything is melo-
dramatic —the slave’s anguish, the midnight fear
of a king. War and the insane booming of guns
is foolish. Love itself makes clowns of men and
women. ”
“What is tragedy?” sneered Atherton; The
jester raised his thick white face and the moon-
beams glinted upon it and turned it to silvfeh
“ It is not for me to say,” he answered meekly.
“ I tried to find it once, but it ran away laughing
and left me like this.” He lifted his arm feebly
and it seemed to crack like the joint of a
marionette. “I rejoice to see my wife in the
midst of so much happiness.”
He rose from the seat wearily, and paused
in the path for a moment as he caught the sound
of a woman’s voice. “ That is my wife singing.
Her voice is full of beauty.”
The moonlight seemed to stay in his eyes. He
shaded his face with a thickly-gloved hand as he
hobbled down the path. The Chinaman assisted
him into the cart, and drove slowly down the
narrow road towards the bay.
Atherton followed stealthily. It was a long
way, but the time and the distance were both
unnoticed. The cart creaked on. The road
turned suddenly into the scrub. Beyond and
almost at the water’s edge lay several outbuild-
ings, Atherton halted uncertainly and afar off as
the cart paused near a gate.
A fisherman with a basket came past whistling.
“ What place is that ? ” Atherton pointed to
where the cart had pulled up.
The fisherman eyed him curiously. “That’s
where the lepers live, ”he said. “ Good night I**

The Fine Heart.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
YOU do not understand me ? No ; nor I
Myself do know myself. At times I seem
To stand apart and watch myself go by
As in a troubled dream,
It is no dream, for there is no awaking,
Nor yet the uncertain sleep a dream demands;
But a dim sense of phantoms overtaking
My soui in shadowy lands,
Where all is glorious for one golden hour —
Garden and fountain, flower and lofty fane ;
And then, as falls a sudden summer shower,
Descends the mist again !
But in my mind, half-hid, sits Recollection
Of those ethereal fields and mystic glades;
And on my soul there falls the faint reflection
Of Light that never fades.
Then do I strive to piece each broken glory—
To build again the fountain and the fame ;
And, lo ! there are no words to tell the story,
And all my thought is vain.
But go your way and let me, here remaining,
Shape such fine fancies as the gods shall send ;
Blind, and half-blind—and happy thus attaining
Each to his destined end.
For you shall find a world so weighed with duty
That flowers in vain lie spangled at your feet;
In. vain shall roses in their pride and beauty
Fling out a fragrance sweet.
In vain the Sea uplift in song resounding
His myriad voices jubilant and strong,
Deafened by cries distracting and confounding
You never hear the song.
But I, the Dreamer, have perchance been granted
Some sight, some sound, while you did sleep or
toil,
Uncaring that there lies a Land enchanted
Beyond this mortal soil.
Some rare, brief glimpse, beyond the narrow
portal
Of this dark House whose windows are so blurred,
Of shining domes and palaces, immortal
And wondrous past all word ;
Wherein, ’mid joyous music and light laughter,
At wine and feast, and crowned with fadeless
wreath,
They sit, the Powers of Here and of Hereafter—
The Lords of Life and Death !
And I, the Dreamer, careless of all payment,
Would seek a moment’s audience, and then
Will clothe in bright, imperishable raiment
The naked thoughts of Men !
VIC-
O. C. CABOT.
The Flying Mile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE course was crowded. Every settler,
and his family and dogs, for miles round
was present.
The club had a new starter and a new judge for
this meeting. Doolan (the old starter) and Regan
(the old judge) were discharged men. Doolan
had a habit of starting the field when some par-
ticular horse was well on his journey. It didn’t
alway happen, even then, that this horse would
pass the winning post first; but somehow Regan
never seemed to notice any horse that passed
before that one, and usually awarded him the race
unless he was lying down half-a-mile back.
“Janny, what’ll win the Flyin’ Mile?” in-
quired Doolan of his friend Regan.
“ Dunno,” answered Regan, pulling the tobacco
out of his pipe with great deliberation. “ Dunno,
I’m sure.”
“Well, see,” said Doolan sidling up to him.
“ Cassidy have Sarsfield in it, an’ if he gets a good
start they’ll never come near him. Cassidy tells
me there’s on’y one that might trouble him, an’
that’s the bookmaker’s horse, War Eagle. Now,
if I was on’y the starter ”
“I know—l know,” interrupted Regan. “ Whin
you were starter an’ I was judge a man c’u’d put a
few shillin’s on a horse an’ get a fair chance to
win, but now—pshaw.” He spat on the ground
with great disgust. “ That feller, Dunn, that’s
startin’, is a fool—lie’s a dribblin’ idjit. I had
Moses backed t’ win fifteen shillin’s th’ fust race,
an’ I offered him half t’ let him get a good start.
He refused, an’ sed it was agin his religi’n t’ take
a bribe like that.”
“No wonder,” said Doolan. “I’m surprised
he didn’t knock y’ down fer insultin’ him. Seven-
an’-six ! Regan, y’ want t’ approach a man like
Dunn wid diplomacy. I’ll go an’ see what can be
done wid him.”
"Good day, Mr. Dunn,” said Doolan to the
starter. “How are y’?—yer lookin’ well. Y’
have a most unthankful job, Mr. Dunn. I had a
few years of it, an’ I ought t’ know. No matter
how fair y’ start 'em there’s alius someone grum-
blin’. An’ a funny thing, now, Mr. Dunn, as y’ll
find, there’s very few ov ’em, afther riskin’ yer
life, maybe, t’ give their horse a fair start, that
’ad ax y’ had y’ a mouth on y’ afther winnin’. Th’
on’y grateful man I iver met on this coorse is
Cassidy, that have Sarsfield in th’ Flying Mile.
But come an’ have a taste av somethin’.”
They went to the booth.
Doolan continued —“Yes, as I was sayin’, Cas-
sidy ’ud treat y’ liberal if he had a win,” and he
eyed Dunn like an adder.
Dunn thought awhile between sups of beer, and
then shook his head slowly. “N—no,” he said—
“No, I don’t think it can be done. The public
you know, Mr. Doolan, the public must be con-
sidered. Anyway, these owners won’t give a
man much.”
“The public ! ” snorted Doolan. “ Why, man,
there’s not a mother’s son av ’em can see the
start from here. They niver know what sort av a
start it is. Anyway, it have nothin’ at all t’ do
wid me, on’y Cassidy gave me this ” (he pulled
out a fiver and unfolded it carefully before
Dunn), “ an’, ses he, ‘ If th’ starter gives my horse
anythin’ like a fair start, he’ll win ; an’ if he
does win, hand th’ starter that.’ Thim are his
words- Anyhow, it’s no affair av mine, so I’ll
have t’ hand it back t’ him.”
Doolan put the note in his pocket and turned
awa y- -Dunn coughed and said, “ Have another
nip. He coughed again. “ What sort of a horse
did you say Cassidy’s was ? ’’
“ A fine chestnut, wid a white hind fut. Here
h e AT IS >” , continued Doolan, leading the way.
-Now there’s a horse fer y’. Look at his quarter
—an his line ! ”
‘‘A good sort,” admitted Dunn.
Just then a starved-looking, bandy-legged
mannikin came up and started to give the horse
a rub down, hissing like a serpent the while.
Are you goin’t’ ride Cassidy’s horse, me bi ? ”
inquired Doolan.
“ No >” answered the mannikin ; “ goin’ ter ride
this ’oss.” And he hissed some more.
“ And isn’t this Cassidy’s horse ? ”
‘INIo; this is War Eagle. There’s Cassidy’s
oss over agin th’ fence.”
“ Ah, I thought it couldn’t be him,” said Doolan
as they stood beside Sarsfield. “ That scrubber ”
(pointing to War Eagle) “is nothin’ to this feller.
1 here s shoulder an* a neck fer y\ There’s a
tail. He’s like greased lightnin’ Cassidy tells
me.”
“Very much like the other,” said Dunn.
“White hind foot and all. How am I going to
tell him from War Eagle at the start ?”
“Oh ! are y’ goin’t’ see that he’s not left at th’
post, then ? ” said Doolan with a surprised air
“Well, out of respect for you and your friend,
Mr. Doolan, I’ll see that no horse gets away in
front of him.”
“Now yer talkin’ sinse,” said Doolan with a
cunning leer. “Y’ll make a successful starter, I
can see. Now this is how y’ll know Sarsfield :
The rider’ll have a red handkercher on his head.
Ye’ll say nothin’ t’ him about anything, fer th’
brat’d be sure t’ go blatherin’ about it t’ someone.”
* * * *
“Saddle hup fer the Flyin’ Mile,” shouted a
man from the booth.
Then there was commotion. A man with a
steelyard was busy weighing the boys, who, with
one leg poked through a girth, dangled about in
the air till they got their weight.
The bookmaker had been narrowly watching
Doolan and the starter, and made a mental note
of their movements. He npw started to lay the
odds. “I’ll bet on the Flyin’,” he bellowed.
“ Two to one th’ fee-eld ! Three to one bar one !
Five to one Bandicoot or Santipee ! Two to one
th’ fee-yeld ! Ten to one Sarsfield—one bet!”
Doolan reared round as if he had been shot in the
rear, but Dunn seized him by the coat tail, and
held him while he nervously forced a couple of
notes into his hand with whispered instructions.
“Five to one, sir.” said the bookmaker to
Doolan’s inquiry. “ Tens all gone. How much ?
Thirty to six ? Right.”
The horses started round to the post. Sarsfield’s
rider had the red handkerchief on his head, War
Eagle’s had a blue cap. The others were bare-
headed. The bookmaker hooked his forefinger,
inserted it in his mouth, and whistled like a late
train. His rider waited for him down the course.
“Now remember what I told you,” he said, as
he left the boy. “Doesn’t matter if it costs a
quid.”
The timber soon hid the horses from view. At
the starting-post Dunn got in front of the horses
and fiercely warned the riders against making
false starts. Also he whispered to the boy with
the red handkerchief, “The second time you come
up, shoot away.”
The horses cantered up, then broke away, the
blue cap in the lead.
“Come back ! ” shouted Dunn.
They went back and turned round. The red
handkerchief stuck the spurs into his mount, and
thundered towards the starter. Dunn dropped
the flag, and away they went, the red handker-
chief ten lengths in the lead.
* ■» * *
“Here they :orae,” shouted a boy, as he leaned
forward and out of a cart.
The peop: il rushed on to the course, but it
was only some dogs after a kangaroo rat.
Round the turn and into the straight the horses
rattled at last, the red handkerchief still in the
lead, sie blue cap next, the rest anywhere. The
din was appalling as they passed the winning
post, the red handkerchief two clear lengths to
the good. The crowd roared in excitement,
people rushed about without knowing what they
were rushing for. Hats were thrown in the air
and lost in the confusion. Horses attached to
carts bolted in great terror. Saddle horses pulled
back, and made off through the crowd with sec-
tions of the fence hanging to their bridles. No
one took any notice except those who wer®
knocked down. Doolan and Regan nearly shook
each other’shands off.
“ What did I tell y’, Janny?” said Doolan,
excitedly. “ Had y’ a good win ? ”
“On’y middlin’. I’d ’a’ won a hatful o’ money
on’y fer her,” he replied, pointing his thumb con-
temptuously at his wife, who, with Mrs. Doolan,
came up all smiles.
“Why didn’t y’ do as I done, Janny—take
no notice av ’em ? She ” (pointing at his wife)
“wanted t’ stop nje, but I had me own way fer
wanst, an’ now I have me thirty gooldin’
sovereigns t’ draw. Come an’ have a drink on
th’ strinth av it, Janny.”
“I’ll pay the outsider,” shouted th® book-
maker. “I’ll pay War Eagle. Roll up you lucky
punters. I’ll pay War Eagle,”
No one had backed War Eagle.
Doolan dropped his glass as he would a viper,
and rushed across to where Sarsfield’s rider was
proudly exhibiting a blue cap to a crowd of
excited boys.
“Yes,” he was saying, “ wasn’t he a flat ? W’en
we was goin’ round ter the start, he guv me this
’ere cap an’ a shillun fer me old red handkercher.
An’ I’d a beat ’im in th’ race, too, on’y he got a
flyin’ start.”
Only that the boy got a flying start Doolan
would have killed him.
PAUL CUPID.
“ Hypo ” communicates :
Between the Yarra and the railway, just above Princes
Bridge, Melbourne, a small army of workers are cutting
and dressing the stone for the new Plinders-street railway
station buildings. The scene suggested to me the con-
stant danger to the workmen. The huge blocks of stone,
fresh from the quarries, are first operated upon by men
with sledge-hammers and gads. The man using the ham-
mer has a mask over his face to keep off the flying chips
of stone. The rest of his body is unprotected. The man
holding the gad chances the hammer falling straight. It
does. Once otherwise would be enough. Afterwards the
stone is passed on (by the crane) to other men with chisels
and hammers, who do wonderful work in smoothing the
surface. They breathe stone dust into their lungs all
day long. Meanwhile the giant arm of the crane is
swinging ton blocks 20ft. overhead, with the attendant
perhaps riding on top of the stone to direct operations
the better. The chain does not break, and the grips
holding the stone don’t give way. But such things
have happened, and will happen again. 'Then . The
engine-driver, watAng his crane and its director with
one eye, and his engine with the other, and his hands
working levers and pins arm-deep amongst cogs and
pulleys—an inch too close to one of which would mean a
smashed arm at least —is not the least dangerously
situated of this typical little army of workers. The risks
of labor ! As I watched the scene described I thought of
the fool crowd who talk about the “ risks of capital."
